A. 
Project Development Objective 
1. 
Project 
development 
objective: 
(see 
Annex 
1) 
Improvement Project (IDSMIP) 
is 
to improve 
the effectiveness 
and 
financial 
viability 
of 
irrigation 
water 
distribution 
and 
management 
on 
56,000 
ha, 
through 
provision 
of 
support 
to 
Water Users Associations 
(WUA) 
and 
the 
State 
Amelioration 
and 
Irrigation 
Committee 
(SAIC). 
The 
development 
objective 
of 
the 
proposed 
Irrigation Distribution 
System 
and 
Management 
To 
achieve 
this 
objective 
the 
project 
would 
provide 
funds 
for 
the 
development 
of 
WUA-related 
institutional 
capacity 
in 
SAIC, 
for 
training 
and 
support 
to 
WUAs 
to 
become 
organizations that 
can take 
charge 
of 
the 
operation 
and maintenance 
(O&M) 
of 
irrigation 
and drainage 
systems 
under 
their 
management, 
and 
for 
selective 
rehabilitation 
of 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
systems. 
2. 
Key 
performance 
indicators: 
(see 
Annex 
1) 
and outcome indicators: 
Progress 
towards 
the 
development 
objective 
would 
be 
measured 
by 
the 
following main 
output 
Outtmt 
Indicators: 
Number 
of 
restructured 
WAS 
(according 
to 
the 
amended 
Law 
on 
Amelioration 
and 
Irrigation), 
that 
have 
taken 
over 
the management 
of 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
infrastructure 
(225 
WUAs 
out 
of 
280 
WUAs 
(80 
percent) 
in 
the 
project 
area 
expected 
at 
the 
end 
of 
the 
project); 
Number 
of 
operation and 
maintenance 
plans 
developed 
and 
budgeted 
by 
WAS, 
with 
appropriately 
established 
Irrigation 
Service Fee (ISF) 
structure 
(200 adequate annual 
plans 
out 
of 
225 
plans 
(90 
percent) expected 
at 
the 
end 
of 
the 
project); 
Length 
of 
irrigation 
canals 
and 
drainage channels 
rehabilitated 
(340 
km 
off-farm 
irrigation 
canals, 
1,800 
km 
on-farm 
irrigation 
canals, 
340 
km 
off-farm 
drains and 
550 
km 
on-farm 
drains expected 
at 
the 
end 
of 
the 
project); 
and 
Area 
rehabilitated 
and 
in 
agricultural 
production, 
with 
adequate 
water 
services 
(56,000 
ha 
expected 
in 
45 
project-WUAs 
at 
the 
end 
of 
the project). 
Outcome Indicators: 
e 
Collection 
percentage 
of 
ISF, 
both for 
WUA- 
and 
SAIC-managed 
operation and 
maintenance 
activities 
(90 
percent 
of 
the 
fees 
for both 
SAIC 
and 
45 
project-WUAs 
expected at 
the 
end 
of 
the 
project); 
Amount 
of 
maintenance 
of 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
systems 
executed 
under 
the management 
of 
WUAs, 
as 
percentage 
of 
annual 
O&M 
plans 
(90 
percent 
of 
the 
necessary maintenance 
carried out 
by 
45 
project-WUAs 
at 
the 
end 
of 
the 
project); 
Percentage 
of 
potential 
water 
users 
who 
actually 
receive 
irrigation 
water 
(95 
percent 
of 
all 
potential 
water 
users 
in 
each 
of 
the 
45 
project-WUAs 
expected 
at 
the 
end 
of 
the 
project); 
Volumes 
of 
water 
distributed 
to 
the 
farm 
plots, 
measured 
as 
number 
of 
irrigations 
as 
determined 
by 
irrigation 
plans 
(from 
an average 
of 
2 
to 
4 
irrigations 
for 
summer 
crops 
expected 
at 
the 
end 
of 
the 
project 
for 
80 
percent 
of 
all 
farmers 
in 
the 
45 
project-WUAs); 
and 
Percentage 
of 
water 
users 
satisfied 
with 
WUA 
managerial 
and 
operational 
performance, 
as 
determined 
through 
customer 
satisfaction 
surveys 
(75 
percent 
of 
all 
users 
in 
the 
45 
project-WUAs 
expected 
at the 
end 
of 
the project).

Page  7
B. 
Strategic Context 
1. 
Sector-related 
Country 
Assistance 
Strategy 
(CAS) 
goal 
supported 
by 
the project: 
(see 
Annex 
1) 
Document number: 
19586-AZ 
The 
main 
emphasis 
of 
the 
CAS for 
the 
period 
2000-02 
has 
been 
on 
establishing 
an 
appropriate 
Date 
of 
latest 
CAS 
discussion: 
09/14/1999 
institutional 
and 
policy 
framework, 
as 
well 
as 
on 
focusing 
on 
infrastructure 
development, 
aimed at 
broad-based 
economic growth, employment 
creation, 
and 
poverty alleviation. 
This 
was, 
inter 
alia, 
achieved 
through 
reform 
of 
public 
sector 
institutions 
to 
enable 
them 
to 
manage 
the country's 
natural 
resources 
efficiently 
and 
to 
ensure 
that economic 
growth 
leads 
to 
accelerated 
poverty 
reduction. 
A 
new 
CAS 
is 
under 
preparation, 
with 
an expected 
Board 
discussion 
on 
May 
27,2003. 
The 
future 
challenge 
for 
Azerbaijan 
is 
to 
design 
and 
implement 
a 
policy 
agenda 
that 
would 
lead 
to poverty 
reduction 
and 
would 
improve 
incomes 
as 
well 
as 
equity. 
The 
proposed Bank's 
assistance 
strategy 
would 
tackle 
two 
key 
issues 
that were 
identified 
in 
the 
recently 
approved 
State 
Program 
on 
Poverty Reduction 
and 
Economic 
Development 
(SPPRED; 
approved 
by 
Presidential 
Decree 
of 
February 
20,2003), 
namely: 
(i) 
generating 
productive 
employment, 
especially 
by 
strengthening 
the 
development 
of 
agriculture, 
as 
the 
most 
important 
non-oil 
sector, 
which 
provides 
ample 
opportunities 
for 
expanding output and income 
of 
Azerbaijan's 
rural 
population; and 
(ii) 
reversing 
the 
decline 
in 
physical 
and social infrastructure, and 
improving 
access 
for 
the 
poor. 
Elements 
of 
Government's 
policy 
for 
the 
development 
of 
the 
agricultural 
sector 
are 
described 
in 
the SPPRED. 
This 
development 
is 
to 
be 
accomplished 
within 
the 
framework 
of 
a 
comprehensive 
rural 
development 
policy, crucial 
for 
the 
reduction 
in 
differences 
in 
living 
standards 
between 
Baku 
and 
rural 
regions. 
The 
SPPRED 
puts 
the development 
of 
agriculture 
in 
the 
context 
of 
an 
integrated 
rural 
development 
policy, 
including 
linkages 
to 
non-farming activities 
such 
as 
ago-processing, 
industry, 
handicrafts, 
retail, distribution, 
transport, 
and 
construction, and 
taking 
into 
account 
environmental 
concerns. 
The 
SPPRED also presents 
the importance 
of 
irrigation 
for 
agricultural 
production, and the 
need 
for 
public 
investment 
to 
rehabilitate 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
systems 
to 
reverse 
the 
ongoing 
deterioration, and 
make 
them 
more efficient. 
Higher 
efficiency 
and 
effectiveness also 
require 
improvements 
in 
management, 
which 
is 
why 
further 
development 
of 
WAS 
is 
mentioned 
as 
important. 
Finally, 
it 
is 
stated 
in 
the 
SPPRED that 
it 
is 
important 
to 
introduce 
gradually 
cost 
recovery 
systems 
for 
operation 
and 
maintenance 
to 
assure 
sustainability, 
but 
until 
this 
is 
achieved, 
it 
would 
be 
necessary 
to 
allocate 
public 
money 
for 
O&M 
of 
state 
owned 
infrastructure. 
The 
proposed 
project 
would 
be 
consistent 
with 
Government's 
strategy. 
The 
project 
would 
also 
respond 
to 
a recommendation 
of 
the 
2002 
Poverty 
Assessment, 
which 
calls 
for 
the 
rehabilitation 
of 
irrigation 
infrastructure, 
institutional 
reforms 
in 
irrigation 
management, 
and 
the 
development 
of 
WAS. 
The 
project 
would 
be 
consistent 
with 
the 
objectives 
of 
the 
Bank's 
new Rural 
Development 
Strategy 
(RDS) 
- 
Reaching 
the 
Rural Poor 
(October 2003). 
This 
Strategy acknowledges 
that 
agricultural 
development 
plays a central 
role 
in 
poverty 
reduction 
in 
most developing 
countries. 
It 
puts 
a 
special 
emphasis 
on 
the 
management 
of 
natural 
resources 
as 
a prerequisite 
for 
the 
sustainability 
of 
any 
rural 
development and 
poverty 
reduction effort. 
Agriculture 
depends 
fundamentally 
on 
natural 
resources 
and 
has 
an 
important 
role 
in 
their 
conservation. 
The 
project 
would 
thus 
address 
one 
of 
the objectives 
of 
the 
Bank's 
RDS 
by 
improving 
the 
sustainable 
use 
of 
water through 
improved 
water 
management. 
It 
would 
focus 
on 
several 
important 
components 
of 
achieving 
efficient 
use 
of 
water 
in 
agriculture 
as 
proposed 
by 
the 
new 
RDS 
and the 
recently 
approved 
Bank 
Water 
Resources 
Management 
Strategy (February 
2003), 
including: 
(i) 
scaling-up 
user 
participation 
in 
irrigation 
management, 
notably 
through 
WUAs, 
and 
ensuring 
their 
professional and 
financial 
independence; 
(ii) 
concentrating the investment 
priorities 
in 
-3- 
Page  8
agricultural water 
on 
improving 
the 
productivity 
of 
existing irrigation 
systems; 
and 
(iii) 
promoting 
institutional reforms 
and 
capacity 
building 
of 
sector 
agencies 
responsible 
for 
irrigation 
system 
management. 
The 
project 
was 
first 
proposed 
in 
the 
mid-nineties 
as 
one 
of 
two 
IDA-funded 
investments 
in 
the 
irrigation 
sector. 
Preparation 
of 
the 
first 
irrigation project took much 
longer 
than anticipated 
- 
the 
Rehabilitation and 
Completion 
of 
Irrigation 
and 
Drainage 
Infrastructure Project 
(RIDIP, 
P008284; 
FYOO) 
was 
approved 
only 
in 
June 
2000 
- 
thus 
preparation 
of 
the 
second 
project 
was 
postponed, 
after 
some 
initial 
preparation 
work 
took 
place. 
RIDIP 
focuses 
on 
rehabilitation 
and 
completion 
of 
major 
conveyance 
irrigation 
systems 
and 
collector 
drains, 
and 
does 
not 
work 
on 
infrastructure 
within 
former 
state 
and 
collective 
farms 
(defined 
in 
this 
PAD 
as 
'former 
farm', 
while 
the 
irrigation 
and drainage 
infrastructure 
in 
these 
former 
farms 
is 
defined 
as 
'on-farm' 
infrastructure). 
It 
also 
does 
not 
focus 
on 
the 
development 
of 
management 
capacity 
within 
these 
former 
farms. 
During 
the 
last 
few 
years, 
however, there 
has 
been 
a 
positive 
momentum 
with 
the 
development 
of 
an 
institutional 
management structure 
at 
the 
former 
farm 
level 
- 
through 
establishment 
of 
WUAs 
- 
which 
this 
project 
intends 
to 
build 
on. 
2. 
Main 
sector 
issues 
and 
Government 
strategy: 
Agricultural 
Sector 
Background. 
The agricultural 
sector 
is 
an important part 
of 
the 
economy 
in 
Azerbaijan. 
In 
2001, 
it 
contributed 
about 
17 
percent 
of 
Gross 
Domestic 
Product 
(GDP), 
and 
provided income and 
employment 
for 
about 
3 
1 
percent 
of 
the 
economically 
active 
population. 
The 
sector produces 
a 
wide 
variety 
of 
products 
and 
was 
at 
one 
time 
a large producer 
of 
cotton, 
fruits, 
vegetables, wheat, 
and 
wine 
for 
Soviet 
Union 
markets. 
During 
the 
transition 
period (from 
1991 
to 
1995), 
agncultural 
output 
fell 
rapidly 
as 
a 
result 
of 
the 
collapse 
of 
trade 
relations 
with 
the 
former 
Soviet 
partners, macro-economic 
instability, and 
the 
conflict 
with 
Armenia. 
Agricultural 
output 
declined 
by 
more 
than 
50 
percent 
and the 
share 
of 
agriculture 
in 
total 
exports 
declined 
from 
30 percent 
in 
1990 
to 
seven 
percent 
in 
1995. 
Ongoing 
Reforms. 
After 
an 
initially 
slow 
start 
to 
the 
agncultural 
reform 
process, 
the 
country 
has 
moved 
ahead 
rapidly 
since 1996 
with 
implementation 
of 
basic 
reforms 
in 
agriculture, 
following 
the 
success 
of 
the 
macro-economic 
stabilization 
program. 
The 
long-term goal 
of 
the 
agricultural reforms 
is 
development 
of 
a 
market-oriented, 
competitive 
and diverse 
agricultural 
sector, 
serviced 
by 
well-functioning 
input 
and 
output 
markets. 
The 
main 
achievements 
to 
date 
include: 
(i) 
the 
discontinuation 
of 
central 
planning 
and 
price 
control; 
(ii) 
abolition 
of 
the 
state 
order 
system; 
(iii) 
the 
liberalization 
of 
domestic and external 
trade 
in 
agricultural 
commodities; 
(iv) 
the 
liquidation 
of 
state 
and 
collective 
farms 
and 
distribution 
of 
land; 
and 
(v) 
initial 
phases 
of 
restructuring 
in 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Agriculture. 
Almost 
all 
state 
and 
collective 
farms 
have been disbanded and 97 
percent 
of 
the 
total 
arable 
land 
(1.6 
million 
ha) 
has 
been 
privatized 
and 
distributed 
to 
869,000 
farm 
families 
in 
one 
of 
the 
most 
comprehensive 
agrarian 
land reform 
programs 
in 
the 
FSU. 
As 
a 
result 
of 
these 
agricultural reforms 
the 
agricultural 
output 
has 
increased, 
and 
in 
2000 
was 
at 
a 
level 
of 
23 
percent 
higher 
than 
in 
1995. 
The 
growth 
in 
agriculture 
continued 
in 
2001 
and 
2002, 
with 
11 
and 6 
percent 
respectively. 
This 
recovery 
was 
mainly 
due 
to 
early 
easy 
gains 
from 
improved 
farm 
management and increased 
labor 
productivity arising 
from 
the 
reforms. 
About 
96 percent 
of 
agncultural 
output 
is 
now 
produced 
by 
small-scale 
individual 
farmers 
working 
on 
the 
privatized 
plots. 
Agriculture 
remains 
the 
second 
largest 
sector 
(after 
industry 
- 
mainly 
oil), 
with 
potential 
export markets 
in 
the 
Middle 
East, 
Europe, 
and 
the 
countries 
of 
the 
former 
Soviet 
Union 
(FSU). 
-4- 
Page  9
The 
main 
crops 
now 
are 
cereals 
(predominantly 
wheat), 
cotton, 
potatoes, 
fruits 
and 
vegetables. 
Areas, 
yields 
and 
production 
of 
the 
main 
crops 
are 
given 
in 
table 
1 
below. 
The 
area 
under 
cereals 
has 
increased 
from 
583,000 
ha 
in 
1990 
to 
647,000 
ha 
in 
2001, 
and 
the 
average 
yield 
in 
2001 
was 
2.4 ton/ha, 
up 
from 
a 
low 
of 
1.5 
todha 
in 
1995. 
The 
area 
under 
cotton 
and 
its 
yield 
declined 
dramatically 
since 
1990, 
which 
is 
mainly 
caused 
by 
input 
and 
marketing 
problems, and 
the 
unreliability 
of 
irrigation 
water 
supply 
and 
distribution. 
The 
areas 
under 
potatoes and 
vegetables 
have also increased. Vegetable 
yields 
are 
still 
much 
lower 
than 
in 
1991 and even 
still 
somewhat 
lower 
than 
in 
1995. 
From 
1995 
to 
2001 
fruit 
yields 
doubled, 
but 
the 
aggregate area decreased 
by 
18 
percent. 
Despite 
the general 
positive 
trend, the 
yields 
remain 
low 
by 
world 
standards, 
and there 
is 
still 
much 
room for 
yield 
increases. 
Crop

Remaining 
Reform 
and 
Development Challenges. 
While 
the 
reforms 
have created 
an 
enabling 
environment 
for 
the development 
of 
the 
agricultural 
sector 
and 
helped 
to 
restore 
growth, 
the 
sector 
still 
faces 
a 
wide 
range 
of 
remaining 
constraints 
which 
impede 
the increase 
of 
output 
to 
potential 
levels. 
Principal 
constraints 
include: 
(i) 
lack 
of 
a 
functioning real 
estate 
registry 
system, 
which 
undermines 
security 
of 
tenure based 
on 
land 
titles; 
(ii) 
lack 
of 
access 
to 
credit 
due 
to 
a 
non-functional 
rural 
financial 
market; 
(iii) 
lack 
of 
access 
to 
good 
quality, 
high 
yielding 
seeds; 
(iv) 
limited 
technical 
and 
managerial 
know-how 
of 
farmers 
and 
insufficient 
advisory 
services; 
(v) 
demise 
of 
the 
agro-industrial 
sector; 
(vi) 
absence 
of 
an 
adequate 
marketing 
system; 
and 
(vii) 
declining 
access 
to 
water 
due 
to 
the 
rapid 
deterioration 
of 
the 
irrigation 
systems. 
The 
Government 
is 
committed 
to 
continue 
the 
reform 
process 
and 
the 
assistance 
to 
the 
agricultural 
sector, 
realizing 
it 
serves 
as 
an important 
source 
of 
livelihood 
for 
the 
majority 
of 
the 
rural 
population. 
-5- 
Page  10
It 
is 
expected 
that, 
with 
the 
assistance 
of 
donors, 
most 
of 
the 
issues 
that 
constrain 
agricultural 
growth 
mentioned 
above 
would 
continue 
to 
be 
addressed. 
IDA 
provides 
substantial 
assistance 
to 
the 
sector. 
The 
Farm 
Privatization Project 
(FPP; 
P040544, 
FY97) 
and 
the 
Agricultural 
Development 
and 
Credit Program 
(ADCP; 
P035813, 
FY99) 
are 
designed 
to 
support 
the 
development 
of 
a 
farm 
information 
and advisory 
system, 
real 
estate 
registration, 
and 
rural 
credit 
system. 
The 
first 
IDA 
project 
in 
the 
irrigation 
sub-sector, 
RIDIP, 
supports 
the 
rehabilitation 
of 
main irrigation 
and 
drainage 
systems 
and 
makes 
a 
start 
with 
institutional 
development 
to 
improve 
irrigation 
management 
by 
the 
State 
Amelioration 
and 
Irrigation 
Committee 
of 
some 
larger 
hydraulic 
units. 
A 
Rural 
Investment 
Project 
is 
planned 
for 
FY04. 
Irrigation 
and 
Drainage 
Sub-sector 
Background. 
Azerbaijan 
has 
nine 
distinct agro-climatic 
zones 
and 
offers potential 
for 
highly 
diversified 
agriculture. 
Except 
for 
areas 
near 
the 
foothills 
and 
the south-eastern 
part 
of 
the 
country, 
apculture 
is 
to 
a large 
degree 
dependent 
on 
irrigation, 
due 
to 
low 
rainfall.  Almost 
all 
agricultural 
land 
faces 
arid 
or 
semi-arid 
conditions 
with 
average 
annual 
rainfall 
of 
200-300 
mm. 
For 
example, 
the 
average 
annual 
rainfall 
in 
Imishli 
in 
the 
center 
of 
the 
Kura 
plain 
is 
254 
mm, 
with 
only 
55 
percent 
falling 
inside 
the 
main 
growing 
season 
from 
April 
through 
October. 
Evapotranspiration 
during 
the 
growing 
season 
is 
high 
due 
to 
low 
humidity 
and 
high 
day 
time 
temperatures. 
On 
average 
it 
will 
exceed 
effective 
rainfall 
by 
a 
factor 
of 
five or 
more.  Consequently, 
irrigation 
is 
a 
key 
input 
for 
reliable 
crop 
production, 
and about 
90 percent 
of 
all 
agricultural 
output 
comes 
from 
irrigated 
lands. 
Thus 
issues 
and constraints 
affecting 
the 
irrigation 
sub-sector have 
a large 
impact 
on 
the 
performance 
of 
the 
agricultural 
sector. 
The 
total 
area 
developed 
for 
irrigation 
is 
1.45 
million 
ha. 
There 
is 
a 
high 
reliance 
on 
pumped 
irrigation to 
abstract 
water 
from 
main 
canals, 
with 
about 
440,000 
ha 
receiving 
water 
through 
at 
least one 
level 
of 
pumping. 
Irrigation 
water 
is 
predominantly distributed through 
open canal 
systems 
and 
surface-applied 
to 
the 
land. 
At 
present about 
1.1 
million 
ha 
is 
being 
irrigated, although 
often 
this 
is 
only 
a 
single 
or 
double 
irrigation 
turn. 
Drainage 
is 
essential 
for 
many 
irrigation 
systems 
in 
Azerbaijan, 
but 
most 
critical 
in 
the 
flat 
low-lying 
Kura-Araz 
and Mugan-Salyan plains, 
totaling 
about 
760,000 
ha, 
where 
soil 
salinity 
has 
developed 
for lack 
of 
natural 
drainage. 
This 
is 
a 
normal 
phenomenon 
in 
all 
aridsemi-arid 
zones. 
Unavoidable 
losses 
of 
irrigation 
water, even 
when 
irrigation 
is 
efficient, 
will 
cause 
the 
groundwater 
to 
rise. 
When 
natural 
drainage 
is 
insufficient, 
the 
rootzone 
becomes 
waterlogged 
and, 
due 
to 
high 
evaporation 
rates 
in 
these 
climatic 
zones, 
salinization 
occurs. 
Natural 
drainage 
has 
to 
be 
complemented 
by 
artificially 
installed 
drainage 
systems, 
and 
leaching 
has 
to 
be 
carried 
out. 
Water Resources. 
Azerbaijan 
has 
an 
average 
annual renewable water 
supply 
of 
32 
billion 
m3, 
only 
30 
percent 
of 
which 
is 
generated 
within 
Azerbaijan. 
River 
flows 
are considerable, 
but 
there 
are 
large 
annual 
and 
seasonal 
fluctuations. 
Most 
of 
the 
flow 
is 
from 
rainfall, 
with 
a 
contribution 
from 
snow 
of around 
25 
percent. 
The 
reliance 
on 
storage 
is 
high, 
with 
an 
overall 
capacity 
of 
about 
22 
billion 
m3. 
The 
average 
annual 
water 
use 
is 
about 
17 
billion 
m3, 
with 
agriculture 
accounting 
for 
about 
70 
percent. 
Almost 
all 
irrigation 
water 
is 
from 
international 
rivers, 
the 
major 
ones 
being 
the 
Kura, 
Araz, 
Alazani, 
Iori 
(all 
tributaries 
of 
Kura), 
and 
the 
Samur. 
Azerbaijan 
is 
the lowest 
riparian 
for 
all 
these 
rivers. 
The 
Kura 
River 
rises 
in 
Georgia 
and 
flows 
southeast 
through 
Azerbaijan, 
draining 
into 
the 
Caspian 
Sea. 
The 
Araz 
River 
rises 
in 
Turkey 
and 
flows 
east 
and 
southeast 
along 
the 
Armenian-Turkish 
border, 
and 
then 
along 
the 
border 
of 
Azerbaijan 
and 
Iran. 
It 
then 
turns 
northeast 
and 
joins 
the 
Kura 
River 
in 
Azerbaijan,  120 
km 
before 
it 
drains 
into 
the Caspian 
Sea. 
Both 
the 
Iori 
and 
Alazani 
start 
in 
Georgia 
and 
the 
Samur 
river 
in 
Dagistan. 
River 
characteristics 
are 
given 
in 
the 
next 
table. 
There 
are 
no 
formal 
-6- 
Page  11
water sharing 
agreements 
between 
Georgia 
and 
Azerbaijan, 
except 
for 
the 
Iori 
River. 
However, 
the 
Kura 
flow 
far 
exceeds 
the 
use 
in 
Georgia, 
and 
reservoirs 
on 
the 
Kura 
in 
Azerbaijan 
balance 
out major 
fluctuations 
in 
flow. 
A 
1963 
water 
sharing 
agreement 
for 
the 
Araz 
River 
between 
the 
Soviet 
Union 
and 
Iran 
remains 
in 
place, 
as 
Azerbaijan 
and 
Iran 
have agreed 
to 
continue 
to 
honor 
the 
terms 
of 
this 
agreement. 
The 
two 
countries 
meet 
at least 
once 
per 
year 
- 
normally 
in 
July 
- 
to 
agree 
on 
the 
water 
sharing 
for 
the 
next 
season. 
This 
involves 
both 
energy 
and 
water 
sharing 
from 
the 
Araz 
Reservoir, 
located 
on 
the 
border 
between 
Nakhchivan 
and Iran, and 
from 
the 
Mill-Mugan 
headworks 
at 
Horadz 
on 
the 
Araz 
River. 
The 
sharing 
principle 
is 
on 
a 
50 
- 
50 
basis, 
but 
exceptions 
are 
sometimes agreed 
when 
a 
country 
requests 
more 
water. 
The 
requirements 
at 
the 
Mill-Mugan 
headworks 
are 
such 
that a large 
volume of 
water 
is 
released 
for 
downstream 
use 
in 
Azerbaijan. 
Name 
Capacity 
Live 
Storage 
Full 
Supply 
(million 
m3) 
for 
Irrigation 
Level 
(m) 
Table 
2: 
Parameters 
of 
Azerbaijan's 
Major 
Rivers 
Minimum 
Service 
Area 
Intake 
Level 
(ha) 
As 
indicated 
above, 
Azerbaijan 
relies 
heavily 
on 
reservoirs 
to 
manage 
its 
water 
resources. 
The 
three largest 
reservoirs 
in 
the 
country 
are 
Shamlur 
and 
Mingechevir 
on 
the 
Kura 
River, 
and 
Araz 
on 
the 
Araz 
River. 
There 
are 
a number 
of 
smaller reservoirs 
with 
storage 
capacity 
less 
than 
100 
million 
m3. 
The 
Mingechevir 
and 
Araz 
waters 
are 
not only 
used 
for 
irrigation, 
but 
also 
for 
hydropower. 
There 
are 
no 
reservoirs 
on 
the 
Alazani 
and 
Kura 
upstream 
in 
Georgia, 
so 
inflow from 
these 
rivers 
into 
Shamlur 
and 
Mingechevir 
varies 
from 
year 
to 
year due 
to 
rainfall 
and 
snowfall 
variation. 
Reservoir 
parameters 
are 
given 
in 
the 
following 
table. 
Mingechevir 
(million 
m3)

Current 
State 
Policy. 
The 
Government 
has 
initiated 
various 
reform 
measures 
in 
the 
irrigation 
sub-sector 
as 
a 
part 
of 
the 
agricultural 
sector 
reform. 
The 
Law 
on 
Agricultural 
Reform 
Principles 
(1 
995) 
identified 
reforms 
in 
the 
water and 
amelioration 
sector 
as 
one 
of 
the 
main 
directions 
of 
the 
reform 
process. 
A 
concept 
for 
the 
development 
of 
the 
sector 
for 
the 
period 
up 
to 
2010 
was 
approved 
in 
1996, 
in 
which 
the 
main 
directions 
for 
development 
activities 
were determined, such 
as 
rehabilitation 
of 
priority 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
schemes, 
improvement 
of 
water 
management, 
and 
the 
introduction 
of 
an 
Irrigation 
Service 
Fee. 
As 
noted 
above, 
the 
recently 
approved 
State 
Program 
on 
Poverty 
Reduction 
and Economic 
Development 
recognizes the 
importance 
of 
irrigation 
for 
agricultural 
production 
and 
indicates 
State 
policy 
towards 
continuing 
institutional 
reforms 
in 
the 
agricultural 
sector 
in 
order 
to improve 
the 
management 
of 
the 
country's 
natural 
resources and create 
conditions 
for 
sustainable 
agricultural 
production. 
Legislation. 
Azerbaijan 
does 
not 
have an 
adequate 
and appropriate 
legal framework 
in 
place 
for 
the 
establishment 
and 
sustainable 
operation 
of 
WUAs. 
A 
review 
of 
the 
existing 
legislation found 
no 
specific 
legal 
framework 
for 
WUAs 
in 
the current legislation. 
There 
are 
only 
a 
couple 
of 
references 
to 
WAS, 
one 
in 
the 
Law 
on 
Amelioration 
and 
Irrigation 
(Law No. 
116-IQ, 
dated June 
5, 
1996), 
and 
others 
in 
subordinate 
legislation.  The 
Law 
on 
Amelioration 
and 
Irrigation 
makes 
a 
provision (Article 
24) 
that 
farmers 
could 
establish Water Users Associations 
to 
manage 
irrigation facilities 
within 
the 
boundaries 
of 
the 
former 
state 
and 
collective 
farms. 
However 
the 
law 
provides 
no 
detail 
as 
to 
how 
WAS 
are 
to 
be 
established 
or 
operate. 
The 
only 
other 
references 
to 
WAS 
are 
in 
subordinate 
legislation 
and 
these 
too 
are 
little 
more 
than 
passing references. 
The 
existing 
WUAs 
are 
in 
fact 
companies or, 
to 
be 
more 
specific, 
limited liability 
enterprises 
established 
in 
accordance 
with 
the 
Law 
on Limited Liability 
Enterprises, 
dated 
December 
29, 
1998. 
From 
a 
legal 
perspective they 
are 
not 
'associations' 
at 
all 
and they 
are 
basically 
free 
to 
undertake any 
kind 
of 
lawful 
commercial activity.  The 
standard 
model 
charter, 
which 
was 
developed 
with 
the 
assistance 
of 
the 
Farm 
Privatization 
Project, 
and 
has 
been 
approved 
by 
the 
Government, 
would 
not 
be 
adequate 
for 
the proposed 
IDSMIP. 
Even 
if 
it 
was 
to 
be 
revised, the 
fact 
remains that 
the 
limited 
liability 
enterprise 
is 
not 
a suitable 
legal 
form 
on 
which 
to 
base 
the 
establishment 
of 
WAS. 
The 
Water 
Code (December 1997) 
purports 
to 
regulate 
the 
legal 
aspects 
of 
water relations 
between 
the 
State 
and 
users, 
as 
well 
as 
the 
private and 
public 
sector. 
However, 
the 
institutional 
arrangements 
called 
for 
by 
the 
code 
are 
not 
clear and 
thus 
there 
is 
some 
overlap 
and uncertainty 
as 
to 
the 
respective 
roles 
of 
SAIC 
and 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Ecology 
and 
Natural 
Resources 
(MENR). 
Another 
weakness 
of 
the code 
is 
that 
it 
does 
not 
create 
an 
appropriate 
framework 
for 
overall 
water 
resources 
planning, water 
allocation 
and 
the 
creation 
of 
secure 
water 
rights, 
thus 
failing to 
address 
the 
key 
issues 
needed 
in 
a 
water code 
in 
a market 
economy. 
SAIC 
has expressed 
interest 
to 
receive 
FA0 
assistance 
in 
addressing 
some 
of 
the broader water 
resources 
issues. 
By 
Resolution 
of 
the 
Cabinet 
of 
Ministers 
(No. 
43, 
dated 
March 
15,2000), 
the 
on-farm 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
infrastructure 
was 
transferred 
to 
the 
books 
of 
SAIC, 
which 
was 
compelled 
to 
take 
responsibility 
for 
maintenance. 
Until 
then, 
SAIC had 
only 
been 
responsible 
for 
the 
O&M 
of 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
infrastructure 
outside 
the boundaries 
of 
the 
former 
state 
and 
collective 
farms (defined 
in 
this 
document 
as 
'off-farm' 
infrastructure). 
This 
additional responsibility 
places a 
serious 
burden 
on 
the 
Committee 
during 
times 
of 
funding 
shortage. 
Resolution 
No. 
17 
of 
January 
2 
1,2002 
changed 
the 
ISF 
-8- 
Page  13
from 
volumetric 
to 
area 
based, 
with 
a 
fixed 
per 
hectare 
rate 
per 
raion, 
independent 
of 
the crop irrigated. 
This 
fee 
setting 
structure 
does 
not 
encourage 
efficient 
water 
use. 
Resolution 
No. 
97 
(June 17,2002) 
restricts the 
amount 
of 
fees 
to 
be 
collected 
by 
use 
of

WUA 
for 
O&M 
to 
25 
percent 
of 
the 
amount 
required 
to 
be 
paid 
to 
SAIC. 
The 
argument 
for 
this 
resolution 
is 
that 
it 
protects 
members 
from 
unresponsive 
chairmen 
and 
other 
hired 
staff. 
This 
problem 
can 
be 
solved 
when 
WUA 
members 
are 
in 
control, 
not 
hired 
staff. 
Once 
WUAs 
are 
organized 
as 
true 
farmer 
associations, 
the 
requirement 
for 
'protection' 
would no 
longer 
be 
a 
reason 
for 
SAIC 
to 
involve 
itself 
in 
the 
internal affairs 
of 
an association. 
Clear and appropriate 
provisions 
on 
tariff 
setting 
are needed, 
which would 
allow 
WUAs 
to 
determine 
their 
own 
tariffs 
for 
the 
operation and 
maintenance 
of 
the 
on-farm 
systems, 
free 
of 
government 
interference. 
Insufficient 
Funds for  Operation 
and 
Maintenance. 
Since 
its 
establishment 
in 
1993, 
SAIC 
is 
in 
charge 
of 
operation 
and 
maintenance 
of 
off-farm 
infrastructure. 
Almost 
all 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
infrastructure was developed 
during 
the 
Soviet 
era. 
It 
was 
often 
constructed 
with 
little 
consideration 
of 
economic cost 
or 
long-term 
viability. 
O&M 
budgets 
have 
been 
drastically cut after 
Independence 
in 
1991. 
A 
recent 
survey 
of 
project 
areas 
of 
the 
Rehabilitation 
and 
Completion 
of 
Irrigation 
and 
Drainage 
Infrastructure Project 
found 
that around 
80 
percent 
of 
the 
infrastructure 
has been 
deteriorating 
in 
some 
form 
and 
needs 
attention. 
The 
2002 
budget 
for SAIC 
was 
AZM 
175 
billion 
(about 
US 
$36 
million, 
using 
an 
April 
2003 exchange 
rate 
of 
AZM 
4,885 
per 
US 
S), 
about 
AZM 
15 
billion 
higher 
than 
the 2001 
budget. 
The 
budget 
is 
about 
half 
of 
SAIC's estimated 
requirement 
of 
AZM 
350 
billion 
to 
adequately 
operate 
and 
maintain off-farm 
systems, 
including major irrigation 
diversion 
structures 
and 
reservoirs. 
The current budget 
is 
mostly 
used 
for 
staff 
costs 
and recurrent operational 
expenditures, 
especially 
energy 
costs. 
A 
major 
issue 
with 
pumps 
is 
that 
many 
are 
old 
and 
becoming increasingly 
inefficient 
and 
thus 
expensive 
to 
run. 
The 
recent 
budgets 
include 
also 
small 
allocations 
for 
maintenance 
of 
on-farm 
works. 
For 
example, 
AZM 
9 
billion 
was 
made 
available 
in 
200 
1. 
Unfortunately, 
this 
is 
a 
token 
amount 
compared 
to 
the actual budget 
required 
to 
maintain 
the 
on-farm 
irrigation 
systems 
properly. 
The 
lack 
of 
maintenance 
has 
a 
serious 
impact 
on 
overall 
water 
use 
efficiency, 
which 
is 
estimated 
to 
be 
no 
more 
than 
20 
to 
30 
percent. 
This 
low 
efficiency 
requires 
large water 
abstractions, 
which 
increases 
the 
entrance 
of 
sediments 
in 
systems. 
Low 
Irrigation 
Service 
Fee. 
The 
Law 
on 
Amelioration 
and 
Irrigation 
stipulates 
the 
transition 
to 
a 
system 
of 
payment 
of 
ISF. Azerbaijan introduced 
ISF 
in 
1997. 
Through 
a 
Water 
Tariff 
Committee, 
with 
the 
participation 
of 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Economic 
Development, 
Ministry 
of 
Finance, 
and 
Ministry 
of 
Agriculture, 
SAIC 
calculated a 
blend 
ISF for 
each 
of 
the 
raions 
varying 
from 
AZM 
3,800 
to 
AZM 
203,000 
per 
1,000 
m3. 
The country-wide 
blend 
ISF would 
be 
AZM 
28,700 
per 
1,000 
m3. 
SAIC 
calculated that water 
users 
would 
contribute 
AZM 
172 
billion 
to 
its 
budget 
per 
year, 
if 
full 
payments 
would 
be 
made, 
considering 
an estimated annual 
delivery 
of 
water 
of 
about 
6 
billion 
m3 
at 
the 
on-farm 
boundaries. 
This

would 
be 
short 
of 
covering 
the 
full 
cost 
of 
O&M 
for 
the 
systems 
under 
the 
management 
of 
SAIC, and 
would 
require continued

by 
Government. 
Realizing

users' 
low

5

AZM 
600 
to 
AZM

per 
1,000 
m3. 
The

reflect

If

in

it

AZM 
7

government budget 
to

Lack 
of 
Operation 
and Maintenance 
within 
the 
Former 
Farms. 
The 
situation 
in 
the 
former 
state 
and 
collective 
farms 
with

to 
management 
and

of 
O&M 
is

There 
were 
about 
2,000 
such farms 
before 
Independence 
that 
had their 
own 
management organizations 
to provide 
O&M 
for 
on-farm 
systems. 
The dissolution 
of 
the 
former 
farms 
left 
a 
void 
in 
management 
responsibility, 
with 
no 
one 
having 
ownership 
of 
the infrastructure and 
no

taking 
full

for 
O&M, 
or 
with

fm

taking

but 
providing

This 
has 
been 
a

reason 
for

and

of

water 
at

and 
low

of

which

is 
resulting 
in 
much 
lower

and

under

Recent

showed 
that around 
70 
percent 
of 
the 
farmers have 
a 
less 
than 
adequate 
water 
supply 
to 
their

The 
transfer 
of 
ownership 
of 
the 
on-farm 
infrastructure 
to 
SAIC 
has 
not 
been 
a solution. 
Adding 
to

agency's 
responsibilities,

is 
not 
a

of

irrigation 
systems. 
It 
actually 
goes against 
common 
practice 
in 
many 
other 
countries 
where 
government 
agencies are 
gradually 
reducing 
their involvement 
in 
O&M, 
and 
concentrating 
more 
on 
policy 
and 
regulation 
setting 
and 
implementation. 
SAIC 
has 
indicated 
that 
this 
transfer 
is 
a temporary 
measure 
and that 
it 
would 
hand 
over 
management 
responsibilities 
to 
WAS 
with 
capacity 
to 
carry out 
these 
tasks. 
Inequitable and 
Unreliable 
Access 
to 
Water. 
The 
deteriorated infrastructure and the 
on-farm 
management 
vacuum 
have 
led 
to 
inadequate 
and 
inequitably 
distributed 
water 
supplies. 
In 
most 
cases, 
the 
margins 
of 
the 
systems 
are 
no 
longer 
irrigated 
and 
service 
on 
the lands 
still 
under 
irrigation 
is 
erratic. 
Influential 
farmers 
often 
receive 
priority 
access 
and 
conflicts 
are 
becoming 
more 
frequent. 
Existing 
WAS 
have 
not 
been 
able 
to 
resolve 
these 
issues 
due 
to 
the 
power 
of 
vested interests 
and 
lack 
of 
grass 
roots 
support. 
Status 
ofthe  Development 
of 
Water Users 
Associations. 
Government 
has 
recognized 
the 
importance 
of 
establishing and 
strengthening 
WAS 
as 
the 
organizations that 
would 
eventually 
take 
over 
- 
10- 
Page  15
responsibility 
for 
O&M 
of 
on-farm 
irrigation 
and drainage 
systems. 
A 
start 
was 
made 
with 
the 
promotion 
of 
WUAs 
under 
the 
IDA 
and 
IFAD 
financed 
FPP 
with 
the 
main 
purpose 
to 
pilot 
the 
transfer 
of 
management 
responsibilities 
to 
associations 
of 
water 
users 
in 
six 
pilot 
raions. 
WUAs 
were 
established 
with 
command 
areas 
ranging 
from 
1,000 
ha 
to 
3,900 
ha. 
They 
perform 
a 
main 
function 
in 
allocating 
irrigation 
water 
to 
members 
in 
rotation, 
collecting 
water 
fees 
from 
users, 
and 
resolving 
water 
related 
disputes 
among 
users. 
These 
six 
WUAs 
presently 
serve 
9,383 
households 
in 
3 
1 
villages 
with 
a 
total 
area 
of 
16,942 
ha. 
Within 
the 
six 
WUAs 
a 
total 
of 
198 
Water 
Users 
Groups 
have been established, each 
with 
responsibility 
for 
the 
distribution 
of 
water 
in 
a small canal 
section. 
As 
noted 
above, 
the 
Law 
on 
Amelioration 
and 
Irrigation 
does 
not 
specify a 
framework 
for 
the 
formation 
of 
WUAs 
and 
therefore, 
the 
six 
pilot 
WUAs 
have 
been 
registered 
with 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Justice 
as 
private 
limited 
enterprises 
under 
the 
Law 
on 
Limited Liability 
Enterprises. 
Typically, 
the 
pilot 
WUAs 
have 
a Management 
Board 
with 
a 
chairman, water management specialist, treasurer, 
and 
up 
to 
10 
water 
bailiffdfee 
collection 
agents, 
with 
the 
latter 
number 
depending 
on 
the size 
of 
the 
WUA 
area. 
There 
is 
no 
separate, 
elected 
Governance 
Council. 
Some 
of 
the 
WUAs 
do 
have 
an 
elected 
Audit 
Committee 
(normally 
3 
people) 
to 
provide 
control on 
budgets, 
and 
an 
elected 
Legal 
Committee (normally 
4 people) 
to 
provide control 
on 
the 
volumes 
of 
water 
supplied 
to 
the 
WUA 
members 
and 
on 
the 
payment 
by 
members 
according 
to 
set 
tariffs. 
Up 
to 
the end 
of 
2002, 
members 
of 
a 
management 
board 
were 
paid 
out 
of 
the 
project 
proceeds 
on 
a 
declining 
basis, 
with 
a 
25 
percent 
reduction 
annually 
after 
the 
third 
year 
of 
the 
formation 
of 
a 
WUA. 
The 
sustainability 
of 
this 
arrangement 
has 
been questioned 
by 
SAIC 
management. 
The project 
has 
also 
provided 
training, 
equipment, 
communication 
and transportation 
facilities, 
and 
until 
recently 
has 
been 
paying 
for 
the 
operational expenditures 
of 
the 
six 
WUAs. 
FPP 
also assisted 
with 
comprehensive 
rehabilitation 
of 
the 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
infrastructure 
within 
the 
WUA 
management 
area, 
at a 
cost 
ranging 
from 
US 
$220 
to 
1,070 
per 
ha. 
The 
improved 
condition 
of 
the 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
facilities 
and organization 
of 
farmers 
in 
WUAs 
had 
a 
major 
impact 
on 
the 
timely 
water 
supply 
to 
the 
farm 
fields. 
These 
factors, 
as 
well 
as 
provision 
of 
credits, are 
principal 
reasons 
for 
the 
significant 
increases 
in 
crop yields 
since 1998 
(see 
table 
5). 
During 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation 
(M&E) 
surveys, 
WUA 
members 
have 
indicated 
that 
sustainability 
of 
the 
yield 
levels 
in 
the 
project 
farms 
will 
depend 
to 
a 
great extent 
on 
proper 
functioning 
of 
the 
imgation 
and 
drainage 
systems 
and 
timely 
availability 
of 
water 
in 
adequate 
quantities. 
A 
baseline 
survey 
for 
RIDIP 
that 
was 
carried out recently 
in 
the Samur-Apsheron and 
Kura/Araz 
areas 
found 
- 
as 
expected 
- 
that the 
number 
of 
irrigations had 
a large 
impact 
on 
crop 
yields. 
Farmers 
receiving 
more 
than 
75 
percent 
of 
the 
required 
water 
had 
wheat 
yields 
that were about 
70 
percent 
higher 
than 
farmers 
who 
received around 
50 
percent 
of 
the 
required 
water. 
The raion-level 
irrigation exploitation 
units 
of 
SAIC 
supply 
water 
to 
the 
FPP 
pilot 
WUAs 
according 
to 
norms and 
contracts 
that 
are 
concluded 
by 
the 
two 
parties 
at 
the 
beginning 
of 
each 
irrigation 
season. 
At 
the 
WUA 
level, 
annual 
cropping 
plans 
and 
water 
distribution 
plans 
are 
prepared and 
contracts 
are 
concluded 
with 
farmers 
for 
water 
delivery 
during 
the 
season. 
The 
recovery 
of 
ISF for SAIC 
ranges 
from 72 
to 
100 percent, 
which 
was 
higher 
than 
the 
typical 
recovery 
rates 
outside the 
pilot 
farms 
during 
the 
period 
from 
1998 
to 
2001. 
Until 
the 
switch to 
hectare based 
water 
charges, 
i.e. 
before 
July 
1,2002, 
the 
WUAs 
charged 
their 
members 
an 
ISF 
ranging 
from 
AZM 
2,750 
to 
8,000 
per 
1,000 
m3, 
including 
AZM 
600 
to 
3,000 
for 
SAX. 
At 
present, 
ISF 
charged 
by 
the 
concerned 
WUAs 
to their 
members 
are 
fixed 
at 
the 
level 
of 
AZM 
10,325 
to 
18,438 
per 
hectare 
(including 
VAT), 
about 75 
percent 
of 
which 
is 
for 
SAIC. 
This 
seriously 
affects 
the 
operation 
of 
the 
six 
pilot 
WUAs. 
Insufficient 
hnds 
are 
available 
for 
maintenance 
of 
the 
rehabilitated 
systems, 
and 
some 
of 
the 
rehabilitated 
canals have 
started 
to 
silt 
up 
again. 
Water 
delivery 
per 
hectare 
continues 
to 
be 
high 
at 
8,000 
to 
12,000 
m3 
per 
ha, 
as 
modem 
methods 
for 
estimation 
of 
crop water 
requirements, 
irrigation 
scheduling and water 
management have 
not 
yet been

Changes 
in 
FPP Pilot 
Farms 
Under 
FPP, 
procedures 
and documentation 
were developed 
to 
provide 
guidance 
in 
the 
WUA 
establishment process. 
Normative 
documents 
were developed 
on 
creation, 
legal 
establishment, 
and 
operation. 
These 
documents have 
been approved 
by 
the 
Project 
Steering 
Committee 
of 
the Cabinet 
of 
Ministers 
and adopted 
by 
SAIC 
for 
the establishment 
and 
registration 
of 
new 
WUAs 
in 
the country. 
At 
the 
same 
time, 
SAIC 
recognized that 
the FPP 
pilot 
WUA 
model 
is 
not 
fully 
replicable 
as 
the 
Government 
cannot 
at 
the 
moment 
afford 
to 
invest large 
amounts 
of 
funds 
in 
individual 
WUAs. 
Work on 
creating 
a 
large number 
of 
WUAs 
was started 
in 
2000. 
Given 
the 
limited 
amount 
of 
staff 
and 
financial 
r, 
Psources 
invested, 
the 
results 
have been impressive. 
A 
total 
of 
552 
WUAs 
have been 
legally 
established 
in 
44 
raions, 
with 
387,000 members 
and 
a 
total 
command 
area 
of 
644,000 
ha 
(45 
percent 
of 
the 
developed 
irrigation 
area). 
The 
general 
level 
of 
knowledge 
of 
the 
members 
about 
WUAs 
is 
limited 
and, 
in 
most 
communities, 
WUAs 
are 
led 
by 
people 
who 
are 
not 
accountable 
to 
WUA 
members. 
Even 
though 
WUAs 
have already 
shown 
themselves 
to 
be 
quite effective 
in 
collecting 
water 
fees, 
they 
have been 
less 
effective 
in 
informing 
general 
membership 
about 
the 
purpose 
or 
use 
of 
the 
fees, 
which 
will 
become 
increasingly important 
as 
fees 
are 
raised 
over 
time. 
A 
WUA 
is 
thus 
mainly 
seen 
as 
an 
organization 
to 
distribute 
water 
and 
collect 
the 
ISF 
for 
SAIC, rather 
than 
a genuine 
farmer-led 
organization 
with 
the 
mandate 
of 
managing 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
infrastructure. 
SAIC 
supports 
the 
establishment 
of 
WUAs 
because 
it 
simplifies 
contracting 
of 
water 
supply, 
as 
it 
can 
deal 
with 
one 
WUA 
rather than 
a 
large 
number 
of 
individual 
farmers 
in 
a 
scheme. 
Although 
understandable, 
this 
should not 
be 
the 
main 
reason 
to 
establish 
WUAs. 
In 
January 2003, 
SAIC 
established 
the 
concept 
of 
WUA 
Support 
Units. 
SAIC's 
Department 
of 
Water 
Resource Management 
and 
Water 
Fee 
Collection 
in 
Baku 
was 
given 
additional functions 
to provide 
assistance 
to 
WUA 
development. 
The 
small 
Department 
has seven 
staffing 
positions, 
three 
of 
which 
are 
vacant. 
Three 
staff 
in 
each 
of 
the 
60 
raion-level exploitation 
units 
were 
also 
given 
a mandate 
to provide 
WUAs 
with 
consultations 
on 
their 
organization and 
operation. 
None 
of 
the 
staff 
have received 
training 
on 
how 
to 
assist 
WUAs, 
except 
for 
a short seminar 
in 
Baku, where 
they 
were 
presented 
with 
some 
written 
material 
that 
was 
prepared 
under 
FPP.

3. 
Sector 
issues 
to 
be 
addressed 
by 
the project 
and 
strategic 
choices: 
Remaining 
problems 
facing 
the 
agricultural 
sector 
include 
inefficient 
extension 
services 
and 
lack 
of 
credit, and 
inadequate agro-processing 
and 
marketing 
arrangements, 
in 
addition 
to 
the 
irrigation 
issues. 
Tackling 
issues 
in 
isolation 
would 
not 
make 
a 
major 
impact 
on 
agricultural 
development. 
This 
would 
call 
for 
a 
broad 
project 
scope 
on 
development 
of 
the 
agricultural 
sector. 
The 
project 
recognizes 
the 
importance 
of 
complementarity 
between 
irrigation, credit 
and 
advisory 
services. 
The 
ongoing 
Agricultural 
Development 
and 
Credit Project 
(first 
phase 
of 
a 
ten 
year 
APL) 
has 
major 
extension and 
credit 
components, and 
is 
taking 
the 
lead 
in 
assisting government 
in 
agricultural 
developments. 
The 
proposed 
Rural 
Investment Project 
would 
have an agro-processing 
and 
marketing 
component, expected 
to 
invest 
in 
value-added 
agricultural 
activity 
at 
the 
community 
level. 
Considering 
these 
other 
projects, 
and 
the 
importance 
of 
irrigation 
and 
the 
need 
for 
substantial 
investments, 
a choice 
was 
made 
to 
have 
IDSMIP 
concentrate 
on 
irrigation only 
which, 
even 
by 
its 
current 
design, 
would 
be 
a 
challenging 
one 
to 
implement. 
It 
is 
very 
important, 
however, 
to 
have close 
linkages 
with 
the 
other operations 
in 
the 
agricultural 
sector, 
and 
make 
sure 
that 
project activities 
complement 
each other, 
so 
that 
as 
many 
farmers 
as 
possible 
would 
have 
access 
to credit, 
inputs 
and extension 
to 
transfer the 
more 
reliable 
water 
delivery 
into 
additional production 
and income. 
To 
achieve that, 
the 
project 
would 
work 
in 
a 
number 
of 
raions 
where 
ADCP 
is 
or 
would 
be 
working 
in 
future. 
ADCP 
currently provides 
extension 
services 
in 
three 
regions 
(including 
all 
raions 
in 
Nakhchivan), 
and 
starting 
in 
July 
2003, 
the 
information 
and 
advisory 
services 
will 
also 
be 
available 
in 
other 
raions 
(including 
Aghjabedi, 
Beylagan, 
and 
Imishli 
raions). 
ADCP 
also 
launched 
its 
credit 
program 
for 
private 
farmers 
in 
Nakhchivan's 
raions, 
with 
planned 
expansion 
to 
other regions 
by 
the 
end 
of 
2003 
(including 
Aghjabedi, 
Beylagan, 
and 
Imishli 
raions). 
ADCP 
has 
also 
conducted 
one 
information 
campaign and a number 
of 
short 
village-level 
training 
courses 
in 
water 
management, 
which 
would 
be 
expanded 
on 
by 
IDSMIP. 
The 
major 
challenges 
facing 
the 
irrigation 
and 
drainage sub-sector 
are 
to: 
(i) 
increase 
the 
participation 
of 
farmers 
in 
scheme 
management 
and 
O&M; 
(ii) 
improve 
the 
technical performance 
of 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
systems 
to 
achieve 
higher water 
use 
efficiency 
and 
reduce 
waterlogging 
and 
salinity 
problems; 
(iii) 
increase 
water 
users' 
financial 
contributions 
for 
O&M 
of 
infrastructure 
under 
both 
SAIC 
and 
WUA 
management; 
and 
(iv) 
improve 
the 
legal 
enabling environment. 
If 
nothing 
is 
done, 
there 
would 
surely 
be a 
continued 
decline 
of 
the 
sub-sector, 
with 
deterioration 
of 
infrastructure, 
decrease 
in 
water 
deliveries 
to 
crops, 
and 
areas 
going 
out 
of 
production. 
This 
would 
have 
a 
serious 
impact 
on 
the 
livelihood 
of 
rural 
people, 
with 
few 
if 
any options 
for 
alternative employment 
for 
the 
time 
being. 
Assistance 
to 
the 
sub-sector 
is 
therefore 
needed 
to 
rehabilitate important infrastructure and 
prepare 
the 
foundation 
for 
long-term institutional 
support 
for 
a 
sustainable 
irrigation 
sector. 
If 
this 
is 
not 
done 
soon, 
the 
decline 
of 
the 
country's 
agriculture 
would 
inevitably follow, 
which 
would 
severely 
affect 
the 
employment 
base 
in 
the 
rural 
areas. 
The 
pilot 
farms 
under 
the 
FPP 
and 
the baseline surveys 
under 
RIDIP 
showed that a 
reliable 
irrigation 
service 
is 
directly 
linked to 
improvements 
in 
yields. 
Despite 
the 
successes 
in 
the 
FPP 
pilot 
schemes, 
it 
was nevertheless 
decided 
to 
change 
the 
legal 
status 
of 
WUAs 
from limited liability 
private 
entities 
to 
non-profit 
associations. 
The 
arguments 
for 
this 
include: 
(i) 
the 
high 
cost 
for 
supporting 
the 
FPP 
pilot 
farms, 
which 
is 
not 
sustainable; 
and 
(ii) 
the 
lack 
of 
separation 
between 
governance 
and 
management 
of 
a 
WUA, 
which 
is 
not 
conducive 
to 
engage 
all 
water 
users 
in 
decision 
making 
about 
important 
aspects 
such 
as 
equitable water 
use 
and 
management and 
reducing 
conflict 
among 
farmers 
for 
water. 
To 
establish sustainable 
WUAs 
successfully, 
the 
following 
minimum 
legal 
requirements 
are

In 
response 
to 
the 
inadequate 
legal 
basis, 
a 
set 
of 
amendments 
to 
the 
Law on 
Amelioration 
and 
Irrigation, 
including 
a 
new 
chapter 
that 
would 
permit 
the 
establishment 
of 
WUAs 
as 
a special 
form 
of 
legal 
person 
and 
which 
also regulates 
their 
establishment and operation, 
has 
already 
been 
developed 
during 
project 
preparation.  The 
amended 
law would 
make 
the 
WUA 
a 
non-profit 
public 
association, 
with 
limited 
tax 
liability. 
Membership 
would 
be 
voluntary, and 
the 
WUA 
would 
have 
the 
legal 
right 
to 
charge 
non-members 
more 
for 
the 
service. 
The draft 
is 
now 
under 
review 
in 
Government 
and 
discussions 
with 
the 
Agriculture 
Policy 
Committee 
in 
Parliament 
have 
started. 
Problems 
with 
enacting 
amendments 
to 
a 
law 
that 
assists 
rural 
development 
in 
the 
country 
are 
not 
foreseen. 
The 
First 
Deputy 
Prime 
Minister 
in 
charge 
of 
agricultural 
reform 
in 
Azerbaijan 
(who 
was 
the 
head 
of 
the 
Negotiation 
Team) also 
gave 
his 
full 
support 
to 
amend 
the 
law. 
Enactment 
is 
expected 
before 
the 
end 
of 
the 
2003 
autumn 
session 
on 
December 
3 
1,2003. 
With 
this 
commitment, 
the 
enactment and 
promulgation 
of 
the 
amended 
Law 
has 
been made 
a 
disbursement 
condition 
for 
Civil 
Works. 
This 
would allow 
the 
project 
to 
become 
effective 
as 
soon 
as 
possible and 
start 
with 
the 
important 
institutional 
developments. 
SAIC 
has agreed 
to 
transfer 
on-farm 
infrastructure 
to 
WUAs 
under usufruct 
arrangements 
for 
up 
to 
20 
years 
and 
renewable, and 
to 
negotiate 
long-term 
(10 
years, 
renewable) water 
supply 
contracts 
between 
SAIC 
and 
WUAs. 
These 
water 
supply 
contracts 
would 
also 
explain 
the 
rules 
for 
setting 
off-farm 
ISF. 
Several documents 
would 
be drafted 
during 
the 
next 
few 
months 
in 
order 
to 
meet 
these 
requirements, 
including 
a 
draft 
WUA 
Statute, 
an 
infrastructure 
transfer 
agreement, 
and 
a water 
supply 
contract. 
This 
would 
be 
done 
by 
SAIC 
with 
the 
assistance 
of 
a consultant 
water 
lawyer. 
Of 
course, 
these 
drafts can 
only 
be 
completed after 
the 
amended 
Law 
on 
Amelioration 
and 
Irrigation 
is 
enacted. 
In 
any 
case 
it 
would 
be 
ensured 
that 
the support 
to 
WUAs 
with 
funds 
for 
rehabilitation 
would 
be 
subject 
to 
having 
all 
necessary 
Regulations 
in 
place, 
satisfactory 
to 
the Association, and 
in 
compliance 
with 
amended 
primary 
legislation. 
It 
was agreed 
with 
SAIC 
that 
this 
would 
include 
the 
revision 
of 
Decree 
No. 
97, 
which 
limits 
the 
collection 
of 
ISF for 
use 
in 
a 
WUA 
to 
25 
percent 
of 
the 
SAIC 
fee, and, 
where 
possible, an eventual 
return 
to 
setting 
of 
SAIC-ISF 
based 
on 
volumetric 
measuring 
after 
re-instatement 
of 
hydroposts 
in 
the 
project 
area. 
The 
land 
reform 
has 
been 
very 
extensive 
and 
largely 
successful, 
but 
has 
paid 
insufficient 
attention 
to 
on-farm 
infrastructure and 
responsibility 
for 
O&M. 
Therefore 
the 
institutional 
aspects 
are 
of 
paramount 
importance 
in 
restructuring on-farm 
irrigation.  Ineffective 
as 
it 
still 
may 
be, 
there 
is 
now 
a 
positive momentum 
in 
the organization 
of 
water users 
and 
their 
empowerment 
to 
be 
involved 
in 
the 
management 
of 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
systems. 
These 
efforts 
deserve 
support 
in 
order 
to 
reverse 
the 
physical 
decline 
of 
the 
systems, 
and the proposed 
project 
would 
assist 
Government 
to 
improve 
the 
effectiveness, 
reliability 
and 
sustainability 
of 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
systems. 
The 
proposed 
project 
would 
assist 
with 
rehabilitation 
and 
improvement 
of 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
infrastructure 
and the 
training 
of 
and 
support 
to 
WAS, 
which would 
take 
full 
responsibility 
for 
the 
O&M 
of 
the 
infrastructure 
within 
their 
service 
territories. 
The 
lack 
of 
support 
at 
the 
moment 
limits 
the 
role 
of 
WUAs 
and 
dampens 
the 
confidence 
of 
members, 
as 
well 
as 
SAIC 
staff, 
who 
seem 
to 
underestimate 
the 
potential 
value 
of 
WUAs 
as 
partners 
in 
the 
effort 
to 
make 
the best use 
of 
national 
water 
resources. 
The 
social 
assessment 
revealed that 
people 
in 
the 
communities strongly 
feel 
the 
need 
to 
- 
14- 
Page  19
improve 
irrigation 
infrastructure 
and 
water 
delivery 
and 
see 
WUAs 
as 
a 
vehicle 
to 
address 
this 
need. 
Rehabilitation 
would 
only 
be 
worthwhile 
if 
there 
is 
adequate 
maintenance afterwards. 
Until 
recently, 
irrigation 
water 
was 
supplied 
free 
of 
charge 
to 
the 
users 
in 
the 
state 
and 
collective 
farm. 
Its 
value 
is 
still 
not 
fully 
appreciated 
by 
current 
users, 
and 
its 
delivery 
is 
often 
seen 
by 
farmers 
as 
a 
fundamental 
right, 
not 
influenced 
by 
payment. 
The 
proposed 
project 
would 
create 
awareness 
and 
educate 
farmers 
that 
payment 
for 
water 
delivery 
is 
a 
users 
responsibility, 
and 
it 
would 
assist 
WUAs 
to 
take 
full 
charge 
of 
the 
necessary 
O&M 
costs 
within 
their 
command 
areas. 
To 
achieve 
this, 
self-sufficiency 
budgets 
would 
be 
established, 
as 
well 
as 
plans 
to 
achieve 
the 
payment 
of 
sufficient 
fees 
to 
achieve adequate 
budgets. 
Annual 
on-farm 
O&M 
costs 
would 
vary 
from 
scheme 
to 
scheme, 
but 
have 
been estimated at 
an 
average 
of 
US 
$15 
per 
ha. 
SAIC 
has done 
a credible 
job 
in 
irrigation 
supply 
during 
the 
past 
ten 
years, 
under 
difficult 
circumstances 
and 
with 
inadequate 
budget. 
The 
proposed 
project 
would 
not 
be 
expected 
to 
make 
major 
changes 
to 
SAIC's 
institutional 
structure, 
but 
it 
would aim 
at 
introducing 
a 
client-driven 
approach 
in 
their 
dealings 
with 
WAS 
conceming 
the 
delivery 
of 
irrigation 
water. 
Once 
WUAs 
have 
reached 
a 
workable 
level 
of 
operation, 
they 
would 
be 
consulted 
in 
the 
process 
of 
setting 
ISF for 
the 
service 
provided 
by 
SAIC, 
which 
should 
be 
done 
through 
a 
transparent 
process, 
whereby 
the 
water 
users 
would 
pay 
the 
actual 
cost 
of 
off-farm 
O&M 
that 
would 
reflect 
the 
real 
need 
to 
provide 
the water 
delivery 
service. 
The 
proposed 
project 
would 
fit 
in 
the 
long-term 
strategy 
for 
the 
irrigation 
sector. 
Irrigation 
is 
a 
critical 
input 
into 
agricultural 
production, 
which 
would 
require 
strong 
agencies 
with 
adequate 
ability 
to 
properly 
operate 
and 
maintain irrigation 
infrastructure. 
SAIC 
would 
continue 
to 
carry 
out 
this 
role 
for 
the 
off-farm 
infrastructure 
for 
the 
foreseeable future, 
with 
increased 
funding 
from 
the 
users. 
As 
WUAs 
become 
stronger, 
assisted 
by 
the support 
provided though 
SAIC, 
they 
would 
gradually 
take over 
O&M 
responsibility 
for 
off-farm 
infrastructure 
as 
well, 
which 
is 
likely to 
happen 
10 
to 
15 
years 
from 
now. 
This 
would 
allow 
SAIC to 
gradually 
concentrate 
more 
on 
policy 
setting and 
oversight. 
C. 
Project Description 
Summary 
1. 
Project 
components 
(see 
Annex 
2 
for 
a detailed 
description and 
Annex 
3 
for 
a detailed 
cost 
breakdown) 
: 
The 
proposed 
project 
would 
have 
two 
primary 
activities: 
(i) 
assist 
Government 
in 
building 
capacity 
in 
SAIC 
to provide training 
and 
support 
to 
WUAs, 
and 
to 
provide 
this 
training 
and 
support 
to 
WUAs; 
and 
(ii) 
assist 
in 
the 
rehabilitation 
and 
improvement 
of 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
infrastructure. 
To 
achieve 
this, 
the 
project 
would 
have 
two 
major 
components, 
while 
a smaller 
third 
component 
would 
provide project 
management support. 
The 
project 
would 
provide 
support 
to 
SAIC 
offices 
in 
about 
60 
raions, 
but 
focus 
more 
comprehensively 
on 
institutional 
support 
in 
the 
1 
1 
raions 
where 
rehabilitation 
activities 
would 
take place. 
The 
following 
11 
raions 
would 
be 
covered 
Aghjabedi, 
Babek, 
Beylagan, 
Geranboy, 
Guba, 
Imishli, 
Khachmaz, Saatli, Sabirabad, 
Sharur, 
and 
Zardab. 
(The map at 
the 
end 
of 
this 
PAD 
shows 
the 
location 
of 
the raions.) 
The 
selected 
raions have 
large 
irrigation 
areas, 
and 
they 
can 
be 
linked 
to 
other donor-assisted 
interventions 
in 
the 
sector. 
Several 
of 
the 
raions 
are 
benefiting 
from 
the 
ongoing 
RIDIP, 
while 
FPP 
and 
ADCP 
are 
active 
in 
some 
of 
the 
raions 
as 
well. 
A 
recent 
Poverty 
Assessment 
Study 
found 
that 
the 
poverty 
level 
is 
quite 
similar 
across 
all 
rural 
regions 
in 
the country. 
This 
suggests 
that targeting 
specific 
regions, 
using 
poverty 
as 
a 
benchmark, 
is 
difficult. 
However, 
most 
of 
the 
11 
raions 
are 
in 
areas 
with 
an 
average 
or 
above 
average share 
of 
poor. 
Many 
people 
in 
the 
1 
1 
raions 
live 
below 
the 
poverty 
line, 
and 
generally 
lack 
adequate 
access 
to 
basic 
services 
and 
infrastructure. 
Agriculture, 
heavily 
dependent 
on 
- 
15- 
Page  20
irrigation, 
is 
the 
major 
source 
of 
income 
for 
the 
majority 
of 
rural 
households 
in 
the 
11 
raions. 
Further 
deterioration 
of 
the 
irrigation 
and drainage 
network could 
cause 
recurrent crop failure, 
which 
would 
increase 
the 
incidence 
of 
poverty 
in 
the 
project 
areas. 
Component 
1 
- 
Development 
of 
Water 
User 
Associations 
(US 
$6.95 
million) 
This 
component 
would 
include 
several 
activities, 
designed 
to 
develop sustainable 
operating 
WUAs 
in 
the country. 
It 
would 
finance technical 
assistance, goods 
and training. 
The 
technical 
assistance 
would 
be 
used 
to 
develop 
training 
and 
support plans 
for 
SAIC 
and 
WUAs, 
prepare 
training 
material, and 
provide 
training 
courses. 
Goods, such 
as 
office 
and 
training 
equipment 
and 
vehicles, 
would 
be 
provided to 
strengthen the 
agencies 
involved 
in 
the 
training 
and 
support 
of 
WUAs. 
Participating 
WUAs 
would 
also 
be 
able 
to 
source 
goods, 
including 
office 
equipment, 
motor 
bikes, 
small 
canal 
maintenance 
machinery, 
and 
field 
survey 
and 
monitoring 
equipment, 
to 
facilitate 
their 
operations. 
Training 
programs 
would 
aim 
at 
increasing 
the 
capacity 
of 
WUAs 
to 
manage 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
systems. 
Activity 
1: 
Development 
of 
Capacity to 
Train 
and Strengthen 
WAS 
Proper 
development 
of 
WUAs, 
with 
adequate 
member 
participation, 
would 
require 
a 
comprehensive 
program 
of 
assistance. 
In 
order 
to 
establish 
the 
institutional 
capability 
to 
develop 
in 
excess 
of 
800-1,000 
WUAs, 
as 
well 
as 
to 
ensure 
that 
the 
Government 
has 
the 
long-term technical 
capacity 
to 
support 
them, 
SAIC 
needs 
staff 
fully 
dedicated 
to 
these 
important 
tasks. 
In 
January 
2003, 
small 
WUA 
Support 
Units 
(SU) 
were 
established 
in 
Baku 
headquarters (Central-SU), 
Nakhchivan 
City 
(Nak-Center-SU), 
and 
each 
of 
the 
60 
Regional 
Irrigation 
Exploitation 
Units 
(Raion-SU). 
What 
is 
lacking 
is 
the 
experience 
and 
institutional 
capacity 
to provide 
the 
training 
and 
technical 
support 
needed 
to 
develop 
WUAs 
into 
broad-based, 
effective 
and 
sustainable organizations. 
The project 
would 
assist 
with 
the development 
of 
these 
newly 
established 
SUs. 
The 
staff 
of 
these 
SUs 
are 
civil 
servants, 
paid 
by 
SAIC. 
The project 
would 
distinguish 
between Raion-SUs 
in 
the 
11 
raions where 
rehabilitation 
activities 
would 
take place, 
and 
other 
raions. 
There 
would 
actually 
be 
12 
Raion-SUs, 
including 
two 
in 
Sabirabad 
Raion. 
The 
Baku 
Central-SU, Nak-Center-SU, 
and 
the 
12 
Raion-SUs 
would 
be 
supported 
with 
office 
and 
training 
equipment, vehicles, 
and incremental operational 
funds. 
The 
Central-SU and Nak-Center-SU 
would 
receive 
more 
training 
equipment 
to 
set 
up 
professional 
training 
rooms. 
The 
staff 
of 
the 
14 
SUs 
would 
receive 
the 
training 
necessary 
to 
implement their 
WUA 
support 
and 
strengthening 
tasks. 
For 
this, 
national and international 
consultants 
would 
be recruited 
to 
develop 
the 
capacity 
within 
the SUs 
to 
establish, 
train, 
support 
and 
strengthen 
WUAs. 
Considering 
the 
critical function 
the 
SUs have 
to 
play 
in 
WUA 
development 
- 
which 
would 
be 
key 
to 
the 
success 
of 
the 
project 
- 
the 
staff 
would 
receive a 
small 
financial 
incentive, 
in 
order 
to 
get 
their 
full 
commitment. 
This 
incentive 
would 
be 
paid 
from 
counterpart 
funds. 
The 
monthly 
compensation 
would 
range 
from 
US 
$80 
for 
a 
raion staff 
to 
US 
$200 
for 
the 
head 
of 
the 
Central-SU 
in 
Baku.  The 
cost 
tables 
that 
are 
annexed 
to 
the 
Project Implementation 
Plan 
(PIP) 
reflect 
this 
government 
contribution. 
Each 
of 
the 
48 
SUs 
outside 
the immediate 
project 
area 
would 
receive a 
small allocation 
for office 
hiture 
and 
equipment. 
The 
staff 
would 
also 
benefit 
from 
basic 
training 
which 
would 
allow 
them 
to 
start 
a 
program 
of 
support 
to 
WUAs 
and 
would 
ensure 
that at 
the 
end 
of 
the project, 
the concept 
of 
WUA 
Support 
Units, 
along 
with 
the 
staff, 
would 
be incorporated 
into 
the 
SAIC 
regular 
line 
structure. 
The 
main 
tasks 
of 
the 
Baku 
Central-SU 
would 
be 
to 
develop a 
long-term 
strategy 
for 
WUA 
development, organize 
and implement 
the 
national 
WUA

and

restructuring program

WUAs

WUAs 
in

The 
project 
would 
provide 
support 
for 
the

and execution 
of 
the

and

programs. 
The 
Central-SU 
would 
also 
be

for

of 
all 
WUAs 
in 
the 
country. 
It 
would initially 
have 
a

of

members. 
In

of 
the 
country-wide 
training 
that 
would 
be 
provided, 
more 
staff 
may 
have 
to 
be 
added 
as 
the 
project 
progresses 
and

demands 
on 
the

increase.  Permanent

to

in

would 
include: 
WUA 
Strengthening 
and 
Development, 
Training 
and 
Promotion, 
FinancialASF 
Management, 
and 
Database/Information Technology. 
The 
Nak-Center-SU 
would 
coordinate 
the 
WUA 
restructuring 
and 
training 
programs 
in 
Nakhchivan, 
in 
close 
cooperation 
with 
the 
Baku 
Central-SU. 
It 
would 
have 
three 
staff 
members, 
with 
skills 
in 
WUA 
Development, 
Training 
and Promotion, 
and 
FinanciaYISF 
Management. 
SAIC 
intends 
to 
monitor 
WUA 
performance, 
which 
the 
draft 
amendments 
to 
the 
Law 
on 
Amelioration 
and 
Irrigation allow 
for.  The 
main 
functions 
would 
include 
the 
monitoring 
of 
technical, 
operational and 
financial 
performance 
of 
WUAs. 
As 
long 
as 
the 
on-farm infrastructure 
belongs 
to 
the 
State, 
SAIC 
in 
conjunction 
with 
the 
WUAs 
would 
need 
to monitor 
the 
condition 
of 
the 
infrastructure. 
This 
would 
be 
done 
in 
tandem 
with 
monitoring 
the 
level 
of 
funds 
collected 
by 
the 
WUA 
for 
O&M. 
Regular 
monitoring 
would 
also 
be 
carried 
out 
to 
ensure 
that 
WUAs 
conform to 
the 
set 
standards 
and that 
committees 
are 
elected 
fairly 
and operate 
within 
the 
bounds 
of 
their 
constitutions. 
The 
project 
would 
provide 
support 
so 
that 
these 
oversight functions can 
be 
carried 
out 
by 
the 
Central-SU 
by 
June 
30,2004. 
It 
is 
envisioned 
that there 
would 
be 
three 
staff working 
in 
each 
Raion-SU, 
namely 
an Engineer, 
Water 
Management 
specialist 
and 
WUA 
Support specialist. 
This 
staff 
would 
be 
trained 
under 
the 
project 
to 
equip 
them 
with 
knowledge and 
skills 
to provide 
technical and 
institutional 
support 
to 
WUAs. 
This 
would 
ensure 
that 
WUAs 
become 
viable 
farmer-governed 
organizations 
that 
are 
able 
to 
operate 
and 
maintain 
the 
infrastructure 
under 
their 
management 
in 
a 
sustainable way. 
The 
training 
to 
Raion-SU 
staff 
would 
be 
provided 
by 
Central-SU 
staff 
and consultants. 
Early 
during 
the 
project 
a 
Technical 
Assistance 
(TA) 
team 
would 
be 
recruited 
to 
provide 
technical 
expertise 
in 
WUA 
development that 
is 
currently 
not 
available 
in 
the 
country. 
This 
team 
would 
include 
a 
mix 
of 
long-and short-term 
consultants, 
including 
a 
WUA 
Development 
Specialist, 
Training 
and 
Promotion 
Specialist, 
Financial 
Specialist, 
Legal

Specialist. 
These consultants 
would 
assist 
the 
Central-SU 
to 
develop 
the 
WUA 
support 
program, prepare 
promotional 
and 
training

and

the trainers, 
comprising 
mainly 
the 
staff 
of 
the 
Raion-SUs. 
The 
consultants 
would 
be 
housed at 
SAIC 
and 
fully 
integrate 
their 
activities 
with 
the 
staff 
from 
the 
Central-SU, 
and 
other 
related 
SAIC 
departments. 
The Central-SU 
would 
also 
hire 
several 
short-term 
national 
consultants, 
as 
and 
when 
needed, 
especially 
with

in

and

and organization, 
as

skills 
to

in 
some 
of 
the 
training 
courses, 
such 
as 
financial 
management. 
Activity 
2: 
WUA 
Strengthening 
Training 
programs 
for 
WUAs 
would 
have 
two 
components, 
one 
directed 
at sensitizing water 
users 
and 
the 
other 
directed 
at 
formal 
and 
informal 
training. 
The 
project 
would 
provide 
on-demand 
support 
to 
farmers 
that 
are 
interested 
in 
participating 
in 
WUAs 
through 
promotional material 
and 
workshops. 
Information 
dissemination 
would 
be 
a 
priority 
of 
the 
early 
stages 
of 
the 
project, 
following 
traditional 
information 
transmission 
mechanisms, 
as 
well 
as 
developing 
new 
ones 
that 
ensure 
that 
all 
stakeholders 
are 
reached 
and 
able 
to 
make 
informed 
decisions 
regarding restructuring and 
development 
of 
the 
WUA. 
The 
project 
would 
also increase 
the 
awareness 
among 
local 
government 
and 
local staff 
of 
relevant ministries. 
Gaining 
support 
from 
a 
wider 
audience 
would 
contribute 
positively 
to 
the 
reforms 
proposed 
for 
the 
raions and 
the 
development 
of 
WUAs.
