17- 
Page  22
When 
the 
legal framework 
is 
in 
place, one 
of 
the 
first 
tasks 
of 
Raion-SUs 
would 
be 
to 
help 
all 
existing 
WUAs 
to 
restructure, 
and 
gain legal 
re-registration 
according 
to 
the 
amended 
Law 
on 
Amelioration 
and 
Irrigation, 
as 
public, non-profit 
associations 
rather 
than 
private, 
for-profit 
organizations 
as 
they 
are 
now. 
The 
legislation 
proposed 
as 
part 
of 
project 
design proposes a 
new 
structure 
in 
which 
governance 
and 
management 
are separated. 
Successful 
restructuring 
of 
existing 
WUAs 
would 
require 
significant investment 
in 
public 
information, 
accompanied 
by 
a series 
of 
neighborhood-level 
discussions 
and 
public 
meetings 
in 
order 
to 
overcome the 
currently restricted 
patterns 
of 
information 
dissemination 
and 
establish 
a 
basis 
for 
replacing existing 
leadership 
with 
people 
who 
have grassroots support. 
The 
Raion-SUs 
would 
have 
funds 
to 
hire 
respected 
people 
in 
the 
WUA 
areas 
for 
short periods, 
to 
assist 
with 
the 
information 
campaign and 
development 
of 
an 
effective communication 
network 
between 
the 
users. 
The 
new 
structure 
must 
allow policy 
decisions 
to 
be 
made 
by 
the 
WUA 
Administrative 
Council 
that 
represents 
all 
the 
members. 
At 
the 
time 
of 
restructuring, 
the 
WUA 
would 
also 
receive 
the 
irrigation 
infrastructure 
under 
a 20-year 
renewable 
usufruct 
agreement. 
After 
the 
initial 
awareness campaigns, 
a 
major 
focus 
would 
be 
on 
the 
provision 
of 
strengthening 
support 
to 
WUAs, 
in 
the 
form 
of 
formal 
training, 
and 
ad 
hoc advice 
through 
regular, 
informal 
contacts 
by 
trained 
Raion-SU staff 
with 
a 
broad 
range 
of 
WUA 
members. 
In 
order 
to 
develop the 
formal training 
program, one 
of 
the 
first 
tasks 
of 
the 
Central-SU, assisted 
by 
its 
TA 
consultants, 
would 
be 
to 
carry 
out 
a 
detailed 
training 
needs 
assessment, 
building 
on 
the 
initial 
assessment 
that 
was done 
during 
project 
preparation. 
In 
areas 
such 
as 
agronomy, 
crop 
production 
and crop-water 
management, 
other projects, 
including 
ADCP 
and 
FPP, 
have 
developed 
training 
modules, 
which would 
be 
adapted 
for 
IDSMIP. 
One 
of 
the 
ADCP 
information 
campaign 
and 
training 
topics 
is 
water 
management, 
for which 
posters, 
brochures 
and 
a 
booklet 
have been prepared. 
However, 
in 
important 
areas 
such 
as 
WUA 
organization, 
WUA 
govemance, 
WUA 
conflict 
management, 
WUA 
financial 
management, 
WUA 
administration, 
and 
WUA 
O&M 
fee 
calculation 
and 
collection 
there have 
not 
been 
any 
major training efforts to 
date. 
From 
experience 
in 
other 
countries, 
these are 
the 
areas 
where 
training 
is 
most 
needed 
in 
order 
to 
develop 
competent 
and 
sustainable 
WUAs. 
Modules 
prepared 
for 
IDA 
projects 
in 
other 
countries 
would 
be 
shared 
with 
SAIC 
at 
the start 
of 
the project, 
to 
be 
adapted 
for 
use 
in 
the project. 
Based 
on 
the results 
of 
the 
training 
needs 
assessment, 
required 
training 
modules 
would 
be 
developed. 
It 
is 
expected 
that 
training 
modules 
would 
be 
prepared 
on 
subjects 
such 
as: 
WUA 
formation 
and registration; 
WUA 
organization 
and 
governance, 
including 
the 
important 
role 
of 
the 
WUA 
Council; 
WUA 
administration 
and 
the 
roles 
of 
the 
various 
staff 
members; 
ISF 
and 
methods 
of 
calculating; 
irrigation 
systems 
operations 
and water allocation; 
irrigation 
infrastructure 
maintenance 
and preparation 
of 
O&M 
plans; 
construction 
supervision; 
and 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation, and 
preparation 
of 
(annual) 
reports. 
The 
project 
would 
pay 
special 
attention 
to 
financial 
sustainability 
of 
WUAs, 
which 
in 
turn 
would 
ensure 
increased 
sustainability 
of 
the 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
systems. 
In 
this 
respect, 
modules 
are 
likely to 
be 
prepared 
on 
topics 
such 
as: 
personnel management, 
social 
security, 
taxes, 
insurance, 
etc; 
expenditure and 
income 
planning 
and 
budgeting; 
and bookkeeping 
and 
audit 
procedures. 
Raion-SU 
staff 
would 
provide 
a 
lot 
of 
the 
WUA 
training, 
but 
some 
training 
would 
be 
best 
done 
by 
private 
companies 
or 
NGOs 
under 
contract. 
This 
might 
include 
courses 
such 
as 
training 
WUA 
Accountants, 
WUA 
Managers and 
WUA 
Administrative 
Council 
members, 
and 
courses 
in 
conflict 
resolution, procurement and 
contracting, 
and 
auditing 
techniques. 
The 
use 
of 
ADCP 
master 
training 
advisors 
to 
provide 
some 
training 
would 
be determined 
when 
the 
detailed 
training 
program 
is 
being 
prepared 
under 
the 
proposed 
project.  The 
Ministry 
of 
Justice 
has 
established a 
Training 
Center 
in 
Baku, 
which 
would 
be available 
to 
provide 
training 
in 
legal 
subjects. 
While 
the 
Training 
Center 
has 
a 
small 
resident 
staff, the 
majority 
of 
the 
lecturers 
are 
short-term 
specialists such 
as 
retired 
judges, 
lawyers 
and 
- 
18- 
Page  23
other 
experts 
in 
various 
fields, 
who could 
be 
hired 
under 
the 
project. 
ADCP 
currently 
provides 
agricultural 
information 
and 
advisory 
services 
in 
Nakhchivan 
(including Babek 
and 
Sharur), 
and starting 
in 
July 
2003 the services 
would 
also 
be 
available 
in 
Aghjabedi, 
Beylagan 
and 
Imishli. 
This 
kind 
of 
extension 
assistance 
is 
essential 
to 
increasing 
agricultural 
production. 
It 
is 
therefore 
expected 
that 
the 
relevant Raion-SUs 
would 
work 
closely 
with 
ADCP 
staff. 
The latter 
would 
also 
be 
involved 
in 
setting 
up 
several 
small demonstration 
plots 
for 
crops 
and 
cropping 
systems 
in 
the various 
agricultural 
regions, 
to 
be financed 
under 
IDSMIP. 
This 
would 
sharpen 
the focus 
on 
irrigated 
agriculture 
and 
increased 
productivity. 
The 
project 
would work 
directly 
with 
an estimated 
280 
legally 
registered 
WUAs 
in 
the 
11 
project 
raions 
(there 
are 
currently 
about 
240 
WUAs 
in 
these 
11 
raions, 
and 
it 
has 
been 
estimated that 
another 
40 
would 
be 
legally formed 
during 
the project). 
Out 
of 
these, 
about 
45 
WUAs, 
with 
an 
average 
command 
area 
of 
1,250 
ha 
each, 
would 
be 
selected 
for 
rehabilitation 
(see 
next 
Component). 
For 
these 
45 
WUAs, 
a 
limited 
amount 
of 
equipment 
would 
be 
provided 
by 
the 
project. 
This 
is 
expected 
to 
include 
office 
fumiture, 
a 
computer, 
two-way 
radios, motorcycles, 
small 
canal 
maintenance 
machinery, and 
portable 
water measuring 
devices. 
The 
usefulness 
of 
each 
request 
for 
equipment 
from 
WUAs 
would 
be 
carefully 
analyzed 
by 
the 
SUs 
and 
the 
Project Implementation 
Unit 
(PIU). 
The 
benefiting 
WUAs 
would 
collect 
funds 
from 
their 
members 
for 
the 
replacement 
of 
the 
equipment. 
Raion-SUs 
would 
review 
each 
replacement 
fund 
proposal 
and 
monitor 
the 
collection 
of 
ISF for 
this 
purpose. 
Project 
staff 
themselves 
must 
fully 
understand 
the 
roles and 
functions 
of 
WUAs 
and 
what 
is 
needed 
to 
create 
viable, 
sustainable 
WUAs, 
before 
they 
are 
asked 
to 
go out 
and 
sell 
the 
concept 
to 
water 
users. 
Besides the 
in-country 
training 
program, 
it 
would 
be important 
to 
expose 
the 
main 
project 
staff 
and 
some 
WUA 
representatives 
to 
experiences 
in 
WUA 
development and 
transfer 
of 
irrigation 
management 
in 
other countries. 
It 
would 
be 
good 
to 
visit 
countries 
with 
technical 
and 
institutional 
conditions similar 
to 
those 
found locally. 
This 
could 
be 
organized 
so 
that a 
study 
group 
would 
spend 
several days 
in 
one 
WUA 
to 
get acquainted 
with 
the day 
to 
day operation 
of 
a 
successful 
WUA. 
This 
would 
provide 
insights 
to 
the 
requirements 
for 
accepting 
full 
responsibility 
for 
O&M 
and 
financial 
management 
of 
large 
service 
areas. 
A 
study 
tour 
for 
senior 
SAIC 
and project 
officials 
could 
be 
organized 
to Mexico 
and 
the US, 
where 
more 
mature 
WUAs 
can 
be 
found 
and 
are 
starting 
to 
be 
federated 
to 
take 
over responsibility 
for 
irrigation 
O&M 
upstream 
from 
the 
on-farm 
area. 
Component 
2 
- 
Irrigation 
and Drainage Infrastructure Rehabilitation 
(US 
$30.00 
million) 
During 
project 
preparation 
diagnostic 
surveys 
were 
carried out 
in 
nine 
project 
raions. 
The 
main 
technical 
findings 
of 
the 
survey 
work 
were: 
(i) 
on-farm 
systems are 
in 
poor 
condition 
with 
high 
water 
losses; 
(ii) 
canals 
are 
seriously 
affected 
by 
the 
heavy 
silt 
load 
in 
irrigation 
water 
due 
to 
non-functioning 
desilting 
at 
headworks; 
(iii) 
there 
is 
lack 
of 
operational 
hydraulic 
structures, 
and therefore 
no 
regulation 
or 
water 
measurement, 
and 
thus 
no 
incentive 
for 
efficient 
use 
of 
water; 
(iv) 
pump 
irrigation 
is 
seriously 
affected 
by 
poor quality 
of 
pumps 
and 
unreliable 
electricity 
supply; 
and 
(v) 
significant 
areas 
have 
been 
affected 
by 
salinity, 
and reclamation 
would 
be 
needed. 
In 
order 
to 
improve 
the 
performance 
of 
WUAs, 
they 
need 
to 
have 
infrastructure 
that 
allows 
efficient 
and 
reliable 
water 
distribution, 
so 
that 
farmers 
can 
receive water 
in 
the 
right 
amount, 
at 
the 
right 
time, 
and 
at 
the 
right 
place. 
To 
this 
end, 
the 
project 
would 
provide 
funds 
for 
selective 
rehabilitation 
of 
irrigation 
and drainage 
systems. 
The project 
would 
target 
some 
280 
WUAs 
in 
the 
11 
selected raions, 
of 
which 
about 
45 
of 
those that 
reach 
sufficient 
maturity 
would 
benefit 
from 
rehabilitation 
investments 
(hereafter 
referred 
to 
as 
rehabilitation 
WAS 
or 
R-WUAs). 
Rehabilitation 
would not only 
be 
at 
on-farm 
level, 
as 
it 
also 
has 
to 
be 
ensured 
that water 
supply 
to 
the 
WUA 
boundaries 
is 
reliable. 
Funds 
would 
thus 
-19- 
Page  24
be 
provided for: 
(i) 
rehabilitation 
of 
on-farm 
infrastructure 
on 
about 
56,000 
ha; 
(ii) 
rehabilitation 
of 
the 
Bahramtape 
headworks 
on 
the 
Araz 
River, 
which 
has 
stability 
issues 
requiring 
urgent 
attention, 
and 
which 
serves 
some 
140,000 
ha, 
including 
large 
areas 
in 
three 
of 
the 
projects 
target 
raions 
(Imishli, 
Saatli 
and 
Sabirabad); 
and 
(iii) 
rehabilitation 
of 
off-farm 
works 
associated 
with 
the 
on-farm works 
to 
ensure 
sufficient 
and 
timely 
water 
delivery 
to 
project 
WUAs. 
Off-farm 
rehabilitation 
investments, 
including 
hydroposts, 
would 
be 
restricted 
to 
about 
35 
percent 
of 
available 
rehabilitation 
funds 
to 
ensure 
that the 
bulk 
of 
investments 
are 
at 
the 
farm 
level, 
where 
the 
most 
serious 
deterioration 
has 
taken place 
during 
the 
past 
years. 
Based 
on 
various 
studies, 
it 
was 
determined 
that 
the 
average 
investment 
for 
on-farm 
rehabilitation 
can 
be 
estimated 
at 
US 
$300 
per 
ha. 
This 
would 
allow 
basic 
rehabilitation 
and 
modernization 
interventions 
to 
return 
a 
system 
to 
operational conditions.  The 
costs 
of 
rehabilitation 
works 
would 
vary widely 
across 
the 
project 
area, 
depending 
on 
the 
scale 
and 
nature 
of 
works required 
at 
each 
site. 
It 
was agreed 
with 
SAIC 
that the 
available 
funds 
would 
be 
used 
as 
efficiently 
as 
possible, 
so 
that 
the 
maximum 
number 
of 
WUAs 
can 
be 
served. 
The 
project 
would follow 
a 
program 
approach, 
where 
participating 
WUAs 
would 
be 
selected 
according 
to 
a 
number 
of 
criteria, 
including 
status 
of 
development 
of 
the 
WUA, 
training 
programs 
followed 
by 
WUA 
Council 
and 
management staff, 
and 
past 
collection 
rates 
of 
ISF 
(see 
Annex 
2). 
Rehabilitation 
plans 
would 
be 
prepared 
with 
maximum 
users 
participation. 
Typical 
rehabilitation 
works 
would 
include 
canal 
and 
drain 
cleaning, 
repair 
of 
canal 
lining, 
rehabilitation 
and construction 
of 
hydraulic 
structures, and 
rehabilitation 
of 
boreholes.  Increased 
use 
of 
weirs, 
rather than 
gated structures, 
would 
be 
an 
example 
of 
scheme 
modernization 
in 
order 
to improve 
the 
management 
of 
a 
system. 
Construction 
supervision 
and 
quality control 
would 
be 
provided 
by 
private 
engineering 
consulting 
firms, 
with 
oversight 
from 
PIU 
construction 
engineers, 
and 
WUA 
management 
staff. 
Implementation 
experience 
in 
the 
irrigation 
sub-sector 
shows 
that farmers 
participation 
in 
paying 
some 
of 
the 
investments 
is 
beneficial. 
Investments 
for 
rehabilitation 
are 
more 
likely 
to 
be 
successful 
when 
users 
not 
only 
participate 
in 
the 
identification 
of 
rehabilitation 
requirements, 
but 
also 
provide 
a 
contribution 
to 
the costs 
of 
the 
works. 
A 
contribution 
is 
not 
so 
much important 
from 
the 
standpoint 
of 
actually 
funding 
the 
rehabilitation 
works, 
but 
it 
is 
an important 
mechanism 
to 
involve 
members 
of 
the 
WUAs 
in 
the 
planning 
and 
design 
of 
works 
they 
see 
as 
important, 
by 
ensuring a genuine 
dialogue 
in 
determining 
on-farm 
priorities 
and 
a 
sense 
of 
ownership. 
The project 
would 
follow 
the 
concept 
of 
farmer-driven 
development, 
a 
participatory 
process 
in 
which 
irrigators (through 
WUAs) 
identify 
their 
needs 
and 
are 
involved 
in 
decision 
making, 
implementation, 
and 
monitoring. 
During 
the 
diagnostic 
surveys 
that were 
carried out 
during 
project 
preparation, 
farmers 
expressed 
a 
willingness 
to 
contribute 
financially 
towards 
rehabilitation, 
provided 
this 
could 
be 
done 
under 
easy 
repayment 
terms, 
and after 
the 
works 
have been completed. 
It 
was agreed that the 
project 
would 
include 
a small 
percentage repayment 
of 
ten 
percent, 
with 
a 
low-fixed 
interest 
rate. 
Repayment 
of 
the 
principal 
and 
interest 
would 
start 
in 
the 
fifth 
year 
after completion 
of 
rehabilitation, 
to 
be 
recovered 
as 
part 
of 
the 
ISF 
during 
four 
years. 
The 
WUA 
would 
be 
responsible 
for 
the 
collection 
of 
the 
funds. 
Failure to 
repay 
by 
a 
water 
user, 
would 
result 
in 
sanctions 
similar 
to 
the 
ones 
imposed when 
O&M 
fees are 
not paid 
in 
time, 
i.e. 
water 
delivery 
would 
be 
stopped. 
SAIC 
would 
consider 
the 
funds 
as 
an 
ISF, 
to 
increase 
the 
funding 
for 
O&M 
in 
the 
raions where the 
funds 
are 
collected. 
The 
Bahramtape 
headworks 
distributes 
water 
from 
the 
Araz 
river 
and 
Upper 
Karabakh 
canal 
to 
four 
main 
canals 
serving 
some 140,000 
ha 
of 
irrigated 
land 
in 
five 
raions. 
The 
headworks were 
completed 
in 
1950, 
with 
only 
routine 
maintenance 
and 
minor 
repairs 
being 
carried 
out 
over 
the 
following 
forty 
years 
until 
the 
end 
of 
Soviet support 
in 
1991. 
SAIC 
recognizes 
the 
need 
for 
urgent capital 
repairs, 
but 
it 
has 
been unable 
to 
take action, 
due 
to 
budgetary 
constraints. 
There have 
been 
no 
major 
repairs 
in 
the 
past 
decade, 
and the 
headworks 
are 
now 
in 
extremely 
poor 
condition, 
with 
an increasing 
risk 
of 
-20- 
Page  25
collapse 
in 
the 
event 
of 
a 
major 
flood. 
The 
project 
would 
provide 
the 
funds 
necessary 
to 
guarantee 
the 
safety 
and 
operation 
of 
the structure 
for 
the 
next 
decades. 
3. 
Project 
Management 
In 
order 
for 
SAIC to 
deliver 
adequate 
and 
timely 
volumes 
of 
water 
to 
the 
R-WUAs, 
the 
higher 
order 
systems 
would 
also have 
to 
be 
in 
operational condition. 
It 
would 
thus 
have 
to 
be 
ensured 
that 
main 
canal and drainage 
systems 
have 
sufficient 
capacity and 
ability 
to 
supply 
water 
to 
or 
drain 
water 
away 
from 
WUA 
areas. 
The 
project 
would 
finance 
selective 
rehabilitation 
of 
higher 
order 
systems. 
This 
would 
include 
the 
re-instatement 
of 
about 
1,000 
water measuring 
structures (hydroposts). 
The 
number 
per 
WUA 
would 
vary, 
but 
it 
has 
been 
determined that 
the 
average 
number 
is 
about 
four.  The 
construction 
of 
hydroposts 
would 
be part 
of 
the process 
of 
the 
re-introduction 
of 
volumetric 
water 
measurement, 
a 
conditionality 
of 
the 
financing 
of 
rehabilitation 
works 
for 
the 
R-WAS. 
System 
evaluation 
would 
also 
identify 
training 
needs 
of 
SAIC 
staff to improve 
the operation 
of 
systems. 
Necessary 
training 
courses 
to 
improve 
service 
and 
accountability 
would 
be 
developed, 
as 
needed. 
2.15 
I 
5.5 
I 
1.80 
I 
5.1 
The 
project 
would 
provide 
for 
necessary 
design 
and 
supervision 
services 
to 
ensure 
that 
all 
works 
are 
properly 
designed and 
carried out 
with 
sufficient 
construction supervision and 
quality 
control. 
Design 
and 
supervision 
of 
the 
rehabilitation works 
would 
be 
delegated 
to 
engineering 
consultants. 
Component 
3 
- 
Project 
Management 
(US 
$2.15 
million) 
With 
the 
similarities 
in 
implementation 
requirements 
for 
the 
ongoing 
Rehabilitation 
and 
Completion 
of 
Irrigation 
and Drainage 
Infrastructure 
Project, the 
existing PKJ 
would 
also 
be 
responsible, 
on 
behalf 
of 
SAIC, 
for 
the 
daily 
management, 
administration 
and 
coordination 
of 
the 
IDSMIP. 
The 
PIU 
would 
be 
strengthened 
with 
staff 
and 
equipment, 
to 
enable 
it 
to 
assume 
responsibility 
for 
the 
implementation 
of 
this 
project 
as 
well. 
The 
project 
would 
finance 
staff 
costs, 
goods 
and 
equipment, 
and 
part 
of 
the 
operational 
costs. 
Funds 
for 
annual 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation 
of 
selected 
project 
areas 
would 
also 
be provided. 
The 
annual 
audit 
of 
project 
accounts 
and 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
Environmental 
Management 
and 
Monitoring 
Plan 
would 
be 
financed 
as 
well.

2. 
Key 
policy 
and 
institutional 
reforms 
supported 
by 
the 
project: 
development. 
The 
proposed 
project 
would 
support 
Government's 
desire 
to 
promote 
the 
development 
of 
sustainable 
WAS 
and 
help 
them 
take 
care 
of 
the 
management 
and 
O&M 
of 
the infrastructure 
under 
their 
control.  This 
would 
facilitate 
Government's 
efforts 
to 
create a 
firm 
basis 
for 
the 
efficient 
allocation 
and 
use 
of 
water 
resources 
and 
efficient 
use 
of 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
systems 
in 
the country. 
Over 
550 
WUAs 
have 
been 
created 
in 
the 
country, 
which 
are 
at 
a 
very early 
stage 
of 
Appropriate 
institutional 
arrangements 
would 
be 
introduced 
to 
provide 
support 
to 
strengthen 
WUAs. 
This 
would 
be 
expected 
to 
be 
done 
through 
a 
combination 
of 
staff 
from 
government 
agencies 
and 
other 
agencies, 
such 
as 
private 
firms. 
Support arrangements 
would 
as 
much 
as 
possible 
be 
-21 
- 
Page  26
decentralized 
to 
raion 
level, to 
allow 
efficient 
decision 
making 
and 
improved 
responsiveness 
to 
the 
needs 
of 
WUAs. 
The project 
would 
incorporate 
mechanisms 
to 
assure 
that 
the 
WUA 
members 
have a 
major 
role 
to 
play 
in 
decision making 
concerning 
rehabilitation 
needs. 
They 
would 
also 
be 
involved 
in 
the 
decision 
making 
process 
for 
ISF 
setting, 
both 
for 
the 
internal 
fee 
and the 
fee 
to 
be 
paid 
to 
SAIC 
for 
the 
delivery 
of 
water 
to 
the 
WUA 
scheme. 
The project 
would 
also 
ensure 
that enabling 
legislation 
is 
in 
place 
to 
allow, 
inter 
alia, 
the 
voluntary 
establishment 
of 
WUAs, 
proper 
setting 
of 
ISF, 
full 
empowerment 
of 
WUAs 
in 
the 
management 
of 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
systems, 
and 
treatment 
of 
non-members 
in 
WUAs. 
The project 
would 
build 
capacity 
in 
management 
of 
investment 
projects. 
It 
would 
also increase 
the 
awareness 
of 
incorporating 
environmental and social 
concerns 
in 
project 
planning 
and 
design. 
Finally, 
it 
would 
improve 
the 
capacity 
to 
carry out 
monitoring 
and evaluation 
activities. 
3. 
Benefits 
and 
target 
population: 
of 
the country. 
The primary 
beneficiaries 
of 
the 
project 
would 
be private 
farmers, 
with 
average 
land 
holdings 
of 
2 
ha. 
A 
typical 
farming 
household 
comprises 
5 
people. 
The 
project 
would 
have 
a large geographical 
area, 
as 
it 
would 
cover 
11 
raions 
in 
several 
regions 
The 
main 
non-quantifiable 
project 
benefits 
would 
relate to: 
(i) 
institutional capacity 
building; 
and 
(ii) 
introduction 
of 
new 
concepts 
to 
change 
the 
mind-set 
among 
the 
rural 
population, 
including 
participatory 
approaches 
in 
decision-making 
related 
to 
rehabilitation 
of 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
systems, 
responsibility 
for 
operation 
and maintenance 
of 
the 
rehabilitated 
systems, 
and the 
need 
to 
pay 
high 
enough 
fees 
to 
make 
this 
happen. 
This 
would 
take 
place 
in 
every 
raion 
with 
irrigation 
in 
the 
country, 
and 
would 
provide 
the foundation 
for 
sustainability 
of 
the 
irrigation 
sub-sector. 
The project 
would 
provide 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
improvements 
to 
a 
total 
area 
of 
about 
56,000 
ha 
for 
45 
R-WUAs. 
The physical 
improvements, 
combined 
with 
more rational 
on-farm 
water 
management, 
would 
increase 
irrigation 
efficiencies and 
the 
timing 
and number 
of 
irrigations 
applied 
each 
season, 
with 
associated 
yield 
increases 
of 
up 
to 
45 
percent 
which 
would 
generate 
extra income 
for 
farmers.  Increased 
net 
incomes 
and 
savings 
from 
irrigation 
are 
expected 
to 
be 
sufficient 
to 
cover 
O&M 
costs 
of 
the 
WUAs 
and 
a gradually increasing 
part 
of 
the 
ISF for 
SAIC. 
Off-farm 
improvements 
would 
be 
required 
to 
improve 
water 
delivery 
to 
R-WAS, 
but 
would 
benefit 
other 
irrigation 
schemes 
as 
well. 
The 
extent 
of 
these 
indirect 
benefits 
cannot 
be 
quantified 
in 
advance, 
as 
their 
nature, 
location 
and 
area 
served 
would 
be 
determined 
during 
project 
implementation.  However, 
it 
is 
estimated that 
an 
area 
of 
at 
least 
110,000 
ha 
would 
benefit indirectly, through 
a 
more reliable 
water 
supply. 
The project 
would 
also 
rehabilitate 
the 
Bahramtape 
headworks, securing 
the 
water 
abstraction 
and 
reducing 
the 
sediment 
load 
of 
irrigation 
water 
to 
about 
140,000 
ha 
in 
five 
raions, 
including 
about 
89,000 
ha 
(63 
percent 
of 
total 
command 
area 
of 
the headworks) 
in 
three 
project 
raions, 
namely 
Imishli 
(27,000 
ha), 
Saatli 
(42,000 
ha), 
and 
Sabirabad 
(20,000 
ha). 
(The other 
two 
raions 
served 
by 
the 
headworks 
are 
Bilasuvar 
(39,000 
ha) 
and 
Jalilabad 
(12,000 
ha.) 
The 
immediate 
benefit 
would 
be 
a 
reduction 
of 
at 
least 
20 
percent 
in 
the current 
sediment 
volume entering 
the 
system, 
which would 
in 
turn 
reduce 
canal 
desilting 
maintenance 
from 
around 
10 
m3ha 
to 
8 
m3ha 
per 
year. 
The 
project 
would 
reinstate 
(or install) 
sufficient 
hydroposts 
(providing 
gate 
regulation 
and 
flow 
measurement) 
to 
serve 
about 
280 
WUAs 
in 
the 
11 
selected 
project 
raions 
to 
enable 
volumetric 
-22- 
Page  27
measurement 
of 
water 
delivery, 
as 
would 
be 
required 
under 
the 
revised 
legislation. 
This 
would 
benefit 
both 
farmers 
and 
SAIC, 
as 
it 
improves 
the 
incentive 
to 
be 
less 
wasteful 
in 
using 
irrigation 
water. 
More 
reliable 
water 
distribution 
and 
reduction 
in 
water 
losses 
would 
increase 
water 
availability 
and 
reliability 
to 
farmers, 
allowing 
an increased 
number 
of 
irrigations, and 
thus 
provide 
scope 
to 
reduce 
the 
irrigation 
interval, 
all 
aimed at 
reducing 
crop 
moisture 
stress. 
Besides 
increased 
crop 
productivity 
and 
profitability 
attributable 
to 
a 
sufficient 
and 
reliable 
supply 
of 
irrigation 
water, 
the 
effective 
area 
under 
irrigation 
could 
increase, 
depending 
on 
potential 
and 
demand. 
Unreliable 
water 
supply 
and 
management 
has 
made farmers risk-adverse 
and 
has 
led 
to 
a 
trend 
towards 
low 
value 
subsistence crops. 
The 
main 
benefits 
would 
initially 
include 
increased 
cropping 
intensity 
and yields. 
At 
a later 
stage, 
with 
restoration 
of 
confidence towards 
growing 
higher 
value 
crops, 
crop diversification, 
and 
subsequent 
employment opportunities 
in 
processing and 
marketing 
would 
be 
achieved, 
through 
a 
comprehensive development 
of 
the 
agricultural 
sector.

D. 
Project Rationale 
1. 
Project 
alternatives 
considered 
and 
reasons 
for 
rejection: 
rehabilitation 
of 
irrigation 
and drainage 
systems 
that 
have already 
been 
constructed. 
The 
No 
Action 
option 
would 
allow 
the 
existing 
systems 
to 
further 
degrade 
to 
a 
point 
where 
continued 
silting, 
installation 
collapse, 
and 
overgrowth 
of 
hydrophilic 
vegetation 
would 
essentially 
eradicate the 
water 
flow 
capacity 
of 
the 
irrigation 
systems. 
This 
would 
force 
most 
farmers 
to 
return 
to 
rainfed 
cropping 
patterns. 
With 
the 
high 
evaporatiodprecipitation 
ratio 
in 
much 
of 
Azerbaijans 
cultivable 
areas, 
this 
would 
spell 
the 
elimination 
of 
agriculture 
as 
a 
viable 
sector, 
with 
the 
exception 
of 
areas 
that 
benefit 
from 
adequate 
precipitation 
at 
higher 
elevations 
in 
the 
foothills 
of 
the 
Caucasus 
Mountains. 
Given 
the 
importance 
of 
the 
agriculture 
sector 
to 
farmers 
income 
and 
the 
welfare 
of 
Azerbaijans 
rural 
population 
in 
general, 
and 
to 
the 
food 
security 
of 
the 
country 
as 
a whole, 
the 
No 
Action option 
is 
not 
an 
acceptable alternative. 
This 
was also 
realized 
in 
the 
State 
Program 
on 
Poverty Reduction and Economic 
Development, 
which 
identifies 
public 
investment 
in 
the 
rehabilitation 
of 
irrigation 
infrastructure 
as 
a 
top 
priority. 
No 
Action 
Alternative. 
The 
natural 
state 
option 
does 
not 
exist, 
as 
the 
project 
concerns 
the 
Transfer 
of 
Irrigation 
Systems. 
Different 
options 
for 
the 
size 
of 
the 
command 
area 
to 
be 
managed 
by 
the 
WUAs 
have 
been 
considered. 
The 
WUAs 
could 
either 
take management 
responsibility 
for 
the 
command 
area 
of 
the 
former 
state 
and 
collective 
farms, 
which 
were 
well-bounded administrative 
areas, 
often 
coinciding 
with 
one 
or 
few 
lower 
order 
hydraulic 
units, 
or 
the 
management 
of 
at 
least 
part 
of 
the 
higher 
order 
irrigation 
system 
could 
also 
be 
transferred 
to 
larger 
WUAs 
andor 
federated 
WUAs. 
Considering 
the 
novelty 
of 
WUAs 
and 
users 
participation 
in 
irrigation 
management, 
it 
would 
be 
better 
to 
first 
of 
all 
develop 
strong and 
sustainable 
WUAs 
at the 
level 
of 
the 
former 
farm 
systems, 
the management 
of 
which 
is 
currently very 
poor. 
Only 
then should 
transfer 
of 
inter-farm 
and 
main 
systems 
be 
considered, 
which would 
also 
mean a 
considerable 
change 
in 
the 
mandate 
of 
SAIC. 
During 
the 
proposed 
project 
duration 
it 
is 
not 
advisable 
for 
SAIC 
to 
hand 
over larger-sized 
public 
infrastructure 
to 
an 
organization 
-25- 
Page  30
that 
would unlikely 
be 
capable 
of 
operating and 
maintaining 
it 
properly, 
therefore the 
option 
to 
transfer 
primary 
and 
secondary 
infrastructure 
to 
WUAs 
was 
rejected 
for 
this 
project. 
It 
would 
possibly be 
an 
important 
activity 
under 
future 
follow-up 
assistance. 
Sector Issue 
Sole 
Focus 
on 
On-farm 
Works. 
It 
was 
considered 
to 
allocate 
all 
funds 
available 
for 
rehabilitation 
to 
on-farm 
works, 
so 
that 
more 
WUAs 
could 
be 
assisted. 
Various 
surveys 
found 
that 
the 
off-farm 
system 
deterioration 
is 
considerable 
as 
well, 
which 
does 
not 
allow 
SAIC 
to 
deliver 
adequate 
volumes 
of 
water 
to 
WUAs. 
It 
was 
therefore decided that 
part 
of 
the 
available 
funds 
would 
be 
allocated 
for 
off-farm 
system 
rehabilitation 
and 
modernization. 
The project would, 
where 
possible, 
favor 
grouping 
of 
WUAs 
to 
maximize 
the 
impact 
and 
benefit 
of 
related 
off-farm 
investments. 
Off-farm 
rehabilitation 
investments, 
including 
the 
major 
headworks 
and 
hydroposts, 
would 
be 
restricted 
to 
around 
35 
percent 
of 
available 
rehabilitation 
funds 
to 
ensure 
that the larger 
share 
of 
investments 
would 
be 
at 
WUA 
level, 
where 
the 
most 
serious 
deterioration 
has 
taken place 
during 
the 
past 
years. 
Project 
Comprehensive 
Rehabilitation. 
Most 
of 
the 
irrigation 
and drainage 
systems 
have deteriorated 
considerably 
and 
could

Lessons 
learned 
and 
reflected 
in 
the 
project 
design: 
countries 
suggests 
that a 
project should 
have 
few 
components 
and 
be 
focused 
on 
one 
or 
few 
major 
activities, 
in 
view 
of 
the country's 
still 
limited 
implementation 
and 
coordination 
capacity. 
In 
addition, 
projects 
to 
be undertaken 
in 
rapidly 
changing 
circumstances 
and 
with 
limited 
reliable information 
should 
be 
designed 
to 
be 
suitably 
flexible 
at 
implementation.  Projects 
should 
foster 
the 
understanding among 
national 
staff 
of 
the need 
to 
ensure 
the 
participation 
of 
all 
stakeholders, 
particularly 
beneficiaries, 
during 
implementation. 
For 
this 
project, 
the 
latter 
would 
be 
key 
to 
the 
success 
of 
developing 
WUAs. 
The 
proposed 
project 
has 
only 
two 
major 
components 
and 
focuses 
on 
one sector 
only. 
It 
would 
follow 
a 
program 
approach, 
which would 
give 
flexibility 
to 
make 
some changes 
in 
project 
implementation. 
Also, 
a 
well 
operating 
PIU 
is 
already 
in 
place. 
Experience 
from 
ongoing project implementation 
in 
Azerbaijan 
and other 
Former 
Soviet 
Union 
Worldwide 
experience 
in 
the 
irrigation 
sub-sector 
suggests 
that 
in 
order 
to 
get 
away 
from 
the 
irrigation 
sector's 
vicious cycle 
caused 
by 
the 
lack 
of 
investment 
and 
maintenance, 
poor 
irrigation 
service, 
water 
user 
dissatisfaction, 
low 
rates 
of 
ISF 
collection, 
resulting 
in 
even 
less maintenance, 
projects 
should 
combine a 
mix 
of 
physical 
rehabilitation 
and 
improvement, 
user 
participation, 
improved 
financial 
performance, 
and 
attention 
to 
O&M. 
Effective irrigation 
projects should be 
as 
much 
concerned 
about 
the 
policy 
and 
institutional 
aspects 
of 
a 
project 
as 
about 
physical 
infrastructure. 
Implementation 
experience 
in 
the 
imgation 
sub-sector 
- 
for 
example documented 
in 
the Bank's 
Operations 
Evaluation 
Department (OED) 
sector 
reports 
- 
shows 
that 
projects 
should: 
(i) 
help 
countries 
and

selective investments 
for 
rehabilitation 
and improvements, 
with 
a 
carehl 
assessment 
of

to

all priority

to 
improved

(ii)

and water

(iii) 
help

develop

for 
managing water 
resources 
and

and management; 
(iv)

cost-sharing 
-27- 
Page  32
programs 
so 
that

have

and 
pay 
an

that

to

and 
(v) 
concentrate 
on 
management 
transfer before 
system 
improvements. 
Lessons 
from 
countries 
where

and

management 
transfer have 
taken 
place successfully 
(e.g.

best-practice) have 
shown 
that 
WUA 
support 
structures 
at

facilitate

changes, 
especially 
when

with

as

Such

could 
be 
part 
of

existing 
organization

of

outside 
agencies,

Only

to benefit

The

could

more 
and 
more 
to 
overall

and

The

would

at

state 
or

farm

Only

(secondary

in

the

In

As

Government

acceptable returns, 
with 
an 
EIRR 
of 
13 
percent 
(case 
(v)). 
2. 
Financial 
(see 
Annex 
4 
and 
Annex 
5): 
NPV=US$ 
17.1 
million; 
FRR 
= 
21 
940 
(see 
Annex 
4) 
Financial 
analysis centers 
on 
investigating 
the 
financial 
returns 
to 
farmers 
from 
the 
improved 
irrigation 
infrastructure, and 
in 
turn 
on 
the 
sustainability 
of 
WUAs. 
The 
WUAs 
would 
be 
dependent 
on 
farmers 
capacity 
to pay 
an 
irrigation 
service 
fee 
that 
is 
sufficient 
to 
cover 
management, 
operating 
costs, 
and 
maintenance 
of 
on-farm 
irrigation 
and drainage networks, 
as 
well 
as 
the 
irrigation 
service fee 
charged 
to 
the 
WUAs 
by 
SAIC.  Currently, the 
ISF 
collected 
by 
SAIC 
finances 
less 
than 
5 
percent 
of 
SAICs 
total 
irrigation 
and drainage 
O&M 
budget. 
The 
funds 
left 
with 
the 
WUA 
barely 
cover 
WUA 
salaries, 
much 
less system maintenance. 
With 
the 
project, 
total 
WUA 
service 
fees 
would 
have 
to 
rise 
substantially 
above the 
fees 
currently 
charged 
in 
order 
to 
ensure 
financial viability. 
Meanwhile, 
the 
ISF 
charged 
by 
SAIC 
should certainly 
not 
be 
reduced, 
on 
the 
contrary, 
it 
should gradually 
increase 
(see 
also 
Sustainability 
Section). 
After 
moving 
back 
to 
volumetric 
fee-setting, 
to 
at 
least 
maintain 
SAICs 
current 
revenue 
from 
service 
fees 
a 
volumetric 
fee 
of 
about 
AZM 
2 
per 
m3 
would 
be 
required 
(current 
average 
fixed 
fee 
is 
AZM 
12,000 
per 
ha, 
while 
the 
water 
delivery 
is 
about 
6,000 
m3 
per 
ha). 
Even 
at 
this 
low 
level 
of 
ISF, 
WUAs 
would 
need 
to 
charge 
their 
members 
on 
average 
around 
7 
times 
the current 
service fee 
to 
SAIC 
to 
ensure 
that 
they 
also cover 
their internal 
costs 
of 
operation 
and 
maintenance 
(estimated 
at 
US 
$15 
per ha 
on 
average), 
in

reimbursing 
SAIC for 
the 
ISF. 
In 
the 
long 
run, 
in 
order 
for 
SAIC 
to 
be 
able 
to 
fund 
20 
percent 
of 
its 
total 
O&M 
budget 
from 
the 
ISF, 
it 
would 
have 
to 
be 
increased 
to 
around 
AZM 
10 
per 
m3. 
Farm 
incomes 
of 
both 
middle-income 
and 
poor 
families 
were estimated 
in 
order 
to 
gauge

of 
increased service 
fees 
on 
farm 
finances. 
In 
the 
rehabilitated 
WAS, 
it 
is 
anticipated that 
systems 
would 
become 
fully 
operational, 
irrigation 
frequency 
would 
rise, 
and 
as 
a

farmers 
would 
benefit 
from 
significant

Analysis 
of 
a 
total 
of 
12 
farm 
models indicates that 
net 
farm

are 
anticipated 
to 
rise 
considerably, 
from 
US 
$100-260/family/year. 
At 
an

for SAIC 
of 
AZM 
2 
per 
m3, 
the 
required 
increase 
in 
WUA

would 
in 
no

exceed 
30 
percent 
of 
the 
expected increase 
in 
farm

and 
in

lower. 
For

represent 
5

or 
less 
of

and 
for

up 
to 
18 
percent 
of

cash earnings. 
At 
this

the 
charges 
should be 
readily 
affordable. 
Even

an eventual 
SAIC 
ISF 
of

10

m3, 
possibly

would 
in 
no 
case 
represent 
more 
than 60 percent 
of 
the 
increase 
in 
farm

from 
the 
project. 
Fiscal 
Impact: 
by 
US 
$35 
million 
in

would

an

US 
$4.1

About

of

Because

exempted 
In

borrowing 
- 
30 
-

from

land 
tax, 
and

as

it

in 
Government

as

However,

expected

it 
should

for

to

to project

According 
to

2002

for 
Azerbaijan, 
"rapid

of 
the 
oil

in 
Azerbaijan 
is 
expected 
to

in

in

to

Total 
Government revenues 
are 
expected 
to 
increase 
from 
AZM 
6,748 
billion 
in 
2002 
to 
AZM 
19,229 
billion 
by 
2010, 
with 
budget

Currently

of

water 
is

almost

by

from

Irrigation 
service 
fee 
revenues have 
contributed 
less 
than 5

to 
SAIC's 
total 
operational

Excluding 
O&M 
of 
pumps 
and 
wells, the 
fees 
cover 
only 
about 20 percent 
of 
current 
expenditures 
on

system maintenance. 
Total 
Government 
budgetary allocations 
for 
O&M 
of 
irrigation 
and

have 
amounted 
to 
about 
AZM 
150 
to 
170 
billion 
(US 
$30.6 
to 
34.8 
million) 
annually 
over 
recent 
years 
(this 
includes energy/electricity 
and 
pumping, 
but 
does 
not

not

associated 
with 
farm 
irrigation, 
such

for flood

works 
or

However, 
it 
is 
estimated 
that 
this 
figure 
would 
have 
to 
be 
more than 
doubled, 
to 
at 
least 
AZM 
350 
billion 
(US 
$71 
million), 
in 
order 
to 
adequately 
cover the 
required 
O&M 
expenditures. 
Expenditures 
through 
SAIC 
are 
the 
major 
channel 
of 
Government 
support 
to 
the 
agricultural 
sector 
(equivalent 
to 
2.7 
percent 
of 
the 
total 
State

in 
2001) 
and 
for 
maintaining 
levels 
of employment 
in 
the economy. 
To 
date, 
about 
two 
thirds 
of 
the budget 
allocation 
has 
been

to 
operating 
and

pumps. 
The 
design 
and 
age 
of 
the 
pumps 
are 
such 
that 
efficiencies 
are 
low, 
and the 
erratic 
power 
supply 
exacerbates 
repair 
requirements. 
The 
high 
operational 
cost 
has 
been 
justified 
in 
order 
to 
support 
livelihoods 
in 
rural 
communities 
which 
have 
virtually 
no

employment opportunities. 
Through 
the 
introduction 
of 
volumetric 
service 
fee 
charges, 
to 
be 
increased 
gradually, the actual 
cost 
of 
providing 
water can 
be 
calculated, 
and 
hopefully 
the 
efficiency 
of 
pumped 
systems 
improved. 
The 
project 
should 
not, 
of 
itself, 
incur 
great increases 
in 
either 
water 
abstractions/pumping 
or 
in 
other 
O&M 
costs. 
However, 
additional 
funding 
would 
be 
required 
to 
maintain 
the 
off-farm 
systems 
in 
the 
future, 
particularly 
if 
pumping 
continues 
to play 
a 
major 
part 
in 
the 
irrigation 
systems 
of 
the 
country. 
3. 
Technical: 
SAIC 
has 
sufficient high-quality 
staff 
at 
raion 
level, 
and 
has done 
a 
credible 
job 
during 
the 
past 
ten 
years 
in 
managing 
water, 
considering 
the 
fact 
that 
it 
has 
not had 
enough 
O&M 
budget 
for 
many 
years 
now.  The 
fact that 
off-farm irrigation 
systems 
are 
also 
deteriorating 
is 
not 
so 
much 
due 
to 
the 
technical 
capacity 
of 
SAIC, 
but 
is 
mainly 
a 
financial 
issue. 
There 
is 
confidence that 
when 
the 
financial 
situation 
of 
the agency 
improves, 
it 
will 
be 
able 
to 
properly 
operate 
and 
maintain irrigation 
and 
drainage 
infrastructure. 
The 
proposed 
project 
would 
mainly 
finance 
rehabilitation 
works 
and 
some 
modernization. 
The 
implementing 
agency's 
strength 
is 
in 
planning 
and 
maintaining 
irrigation 
and drainage 
infrastructure, 
and 
the 
works 
that 
would 
be 
implemented 
would 
unlikely 
present 
technical 
difficulties. 
As 
an 
example, 
under 
FPP and 
DIP 
the 
technical 
issues 
were generally 
well 
covered. 
SAIC 
and 
its 
PKJ 
have 
experience 
in 
supervising 
consulting 
firms 
that 
would 
be 
responsible 
for 
actual 
design 
and supervision. 
SAIC staff 
and 
local 
consultants have 
gained 
experience 
during 
preparation 
in 
conducting 
diagnostic 
surveys, 
which 
included 
walk-throughs 
to 
identify 
scope 
of 
works 
that 
are 
priority, 
cost 
-31 
- 
Page  36
effective 
and 
economically justified, 
and that 
would, 
where

future 
maintenance tasks 
and 
costs. 
This 
kind 
of 
investigation 
would 
be carried 
out 
for 
the 
project 
WUAs 
as 
well. 
During 
preparation,

was

to 
proper costing 
of 
unit 
rates, 
and 
the cost

used

A

Important

has

4. 
Institutional: 
4.1 
Executing 
agencies: 
agency 
is 
the State 
Amelioration 
and 
Irrigation 
Committee, 
which would 
implement 
project 
activities 
through 
its 
line 
departments 
and 
the 
PIU.  Under 
the 
ongoing 
RIDIP, 
the 
SAIC 
has 
gained relevant 
project implementation 
experience. 
SAIC 
management 
is 
committed to 
the 
project 
and 
has 
clear 
understanding 
of 
its 
objective and 
scope. 
SAIC 
has 
generally 
capable, 
experienced engineering 
staff 
at 
raion 
level, 
which 
have 
shown 
considerable 
interest 
in 
the 
activities 
proposed, 
and 
have 
actively 
participated 
during 
project 
preparation.  However, 
it 
is 
fundamentally 
an engineering-based 
organization 
with 
a residual 
supply-driven 
approach 
which 
is 
not 
yet 
well 
adjusted 
to 
the

approach that 
is 
proposed 
for

IDSMIP. 
Although 
hundreds

WUAs 
have

with 
the

of 
SAIC, 
its 
experience 
with

remains

and

largely 
on

Small

Units,

lack

and

These

by

assistance 
of

in

of

and group

and

The 
consultants 
would 
integrate

with 
the 
staff 
of 
the 
Central-SU through 
on-the-job training. 
Formal training 
activities 
and

planned 
under

project 
would 
further 
enhance 
the 
knowledge 
of 
SAIC 
and 
WUA 
Support 
Unit 
staff.

Unit,

SAIC 
Chairman.  Created

has

to

It

and 
has 
clear understanding 
of 
the

of

The

is

fully

with

In

to

envisaged

are

with
