4.3 
Procurement 
issues: 
A 
Country 
Procurement 
Assessment 
Review 
(CPAR) 
was 
carried 
out 
in 
early 
2002. 
The 
comprehensive 
assessment 
of 
the 
public 
procurement 
system 
included 
the 
legislative 
framework, the 
effectiveness 
of 
regulatory institutions, 
the 
strength 
of 
the 
enforcement 
regime, 
the 
capacity 
of 
its 
institutional 
and 
human 
resources, 
and 
the 
threat 
of 
corruption. 
The 
CPARs 
key findings, 
including 
risk 
assessment, 
the 
strengths 
and 
weaknesses 
of 
the 
public 
procurement 
system 
and a proposed 
action 
plan 
are 
summarized 
in 
the 
report 
issued 
in 
June 
2002. 
The 
CPAR 
concluded 
that 
while 
an 
appropriate 
and 
Basic 
institutional 
arrangements 
relating 
to 
the 
IDSMIP 
are 
already 
in 
place. 
The 
executing 
Day 
to 
day 
management 
of 
the 
project 
would 
be 
the 
responsibility 
of 
the 
Project 
Implementation 
- 
32 
- 
Page  37
competent 
regulatory 
framework 
is 
in 
an 
advanced 
state, 
capacity and 
resources 
of 
the 
procurement 
system 
remain 
weak.  Based 
on 
this 
analysis, 
the 
environment 
for 
conducting 
public 
procurement 
in 
Azerbaijan 
is 
assessed 
as 
a 
medium 
to 
high 
risk 
from 
a 
procurement 
point 
of 
view. 
The 
CPAR 
analyzed 
also the 
quality 
of 
the 
governments conduct 
of 
IDA-financed 
procurement 
in 
terms 
of 
adherence 
to 
fiduciary 
requirements 
and 
identified 
the 
following main 
problems 
affecting 
efficient 
procurement: 
(i) 
while 
there 
is 
a 
clear 
policy 
on 
taxation 
and 
custom 
duties 
for 
IDA-financed 
projects, 
the 
processes are 
not 
always 
known 
to 
tax 
and customs 
officers, and 
there 
is 
no 
clear 
procedural 
arrangements 
in 
place 
on 
how 
to 
apply the 
policy; 
and 
(ii) 
there 
are 
problems 
with 
technicalities 
of 
bid 
securities, and 
local 
banks 
are 
not familiar 
with 
issuing 
bid 
securities 
in 
accordance 
with 
IDA 
requirements. 
An 
Independent 
Procurement 
Audit 
of 
five 
IDA-financed 
projects 
in 
Azerbaijan 
(not 
including 
RIDIP),  conducted 
in 
May 
2002, 
identified 
the 
following 
common 
operational 
issues 
related 
to 
procurement: 
(i) 
lack 
of 
procurement 
planning 
resulted 
in 
ad 
hoc 
procurements, 
non-competitive 
procurement 
methods, 
resulting 
in 
high 
prices; 
(ii) 
insufficient 
procurement 
expertise and 
capacity 
in 
project 
PIUs, 
specifically 
with 
respect 
to 
conducting 
NCB 
and 
ICB; 
(iii) 
poor 
contract administration, 
especially 
of 
works 
contracts; 
and 
(iv) 
poor 
procurement 
record 
management. 
During 
the preparation 
of 
IDSMIP 
an 
updated procurement capacity 
assessment 
of 
the 
PIU 
was 
carried 
out. 
Based 
on 
this 
assessment, 
it 
is 
concluded 
that the 
capability 
of 
the 
PIU 
has 
been 
substantially 
built-up 
by 
the 
ongoing 
RIDIP. 
The 
projects 
procurement 
implementation 
capacity 
has 
been 
rated 
as 
Average 
risk, 
as 
it 
is 
not 
facing many 
of 
the 
issues 
and 
problems 
identified 
at 
country 
and 
project 
audit 
level.  Under RIDIP, 
the 
PIU 
has 
developed 
into 
one 
of 
the 
better 
performing 
procurement 
units, 
with 
adequate 
procurement 
management 
capacity and 
experience, 
and 
with 
familiarity 
with 
IDA 
procurement 
rules. 
It 
has 
performed 
satisfactory 
with 
the 
procurement 
of 
works, 
goods and 
consulting 
services 
using 
appropriate procurement 
methods. 
Most 
of 
the procurement 
actions have 
been 
reviewed 
by 
IDA 
supervision missions 
and 
no 
major 
problems 
were 
identified. 
Procurement under the proposed 
project 
would 
include 
mainly 
civil 
works, 
both 
ICB 
and 
NCB. 
In 
addition, 
there 
would 
be 
some 
goods procurement, 
which 
is 
expected 
to 
be 
mainly 
through 
ICB 
and 
International 
Shopping. 
Consultants 
would 
be 
hired 
through 
QCBS 
for 
larger 
packages, 
e.g. 
for 
design 
and supervision and 
WUA 
development, 
while individual 
consultants 
would 
also 
be 
hired 
for providing 
training to 
WUAs. 
Local 
firms 
would 
be 
recruited through 
Consultants 
Qualifications 
for 
smaller 
activities 
such 
as 
M&E 
studies 
and 
some 
of 
the 
SU 
training. 
The 
PIU 
has 
experience 
with all 
these 
methods 
of 
procurement and therefore 
no 
major 
issues 
that 
require 
special 
attention 
are 
anticipated. 
At 
the 
same 
time, 
in 
view 
of 
the 
anticipated 
increase 
of 
workload, 
an action 
plan 
has been prepared 
to 
strengthen 
PIUs 
capacity 
to 
administer procurement 
under 
IDSMIP 
in 
an 
effective 
and 
transparent way. 
This 
plan 
is 
summarized 
in 
Annex 
6. 
4.4 
Financial 
management 
issues: 
preparation and updated at 
appraisal. 
The 
existing 
PIU 
that 
would 
be 
responsible 
for 
the 
project 
financial 
management, 
including project 
accounting, 
reporting, 
administration 
of 
the 
Special Account and 
auditing 
arrangements, has 
developed 
sufficient 
capacity 
and 
experience 
in 
implementing 
IDA 
projects, 
having 
implemented 
the 
ongoing 
RIDIP. 
The existing 
financial 
management arrangements 
are 
considered 
adequate 
for 
the 
implementation 
of 
the proposed 
project. 
The 
ongoing 
project 
is 
already 
producing 
quarterly Project Management Reports 
(PMRs) 
that 
would 
be 
customized 
to Financial 
Monitoring 
Reports 
(FMRs) 
for 
the proposed 
project.  The 
FMRs 
would 
be 
considered 
to 
be 
the 
reports 
required 
under Section 
4.02 
of 
the 
legal 
agreement 
under 
the 
proposed 
IDSMIP. 
A 
financial 
management 
assessment 
was 
carried out 
for 
the 
proposed 
project 
during 
project 
- 
33 
- 
Page  38
The 
audit 
would 
be 
carried out 
by 
independent 
auditors 
in 
accordance 
with 
International 
Standards 
on 
Auditing 
(ISA) 
promulgated 
by 
the 
International 
Federation 
of 
Accountants 
(IFAC), 
and 
under 
terms 
of 
reference acceptable 
to 
the 
Association. 
The 
annual 
audit 
report 
including 
audited 
project 
financial 
statements, 
the 
Special 
Account 
and 
Statements 
of 
Expenditure 
(SOEs) 
would 
be 
provided 
to 
the 
Association 
within 
six months 
of 
the 
end 
of 
each 
fiscal 
year.  Based 
on 
the 
satisfactory performance 
of 
the auditors 
for 
the 
ongoing 
RIDIP, 
and 
for 
consistency 
and 
economic 
efficiency, 
it 
was agreed 
to 
use 
the 
same 
auditors. 
The 
CY 
2000 and 2001 audits 
for RIDIP 
were 
carried 
out 
well 
by 
the 
auditor, and 
did 
not 
reveal any 
problems 
with 
accounting. 
The draft 
Country Financial 
Accountability 
Assessment 
Report 
(CFAA) 
dated 
January 2003 
identified 
a 
number 
of 
weaknesses 
in 
the 
public 
financial 
management that 
require 
strengthening. 
The 
Structural 
Adjustment 
Credit 
(SAC-2) 
will 
facilitate 
policy 
dialogue 
in 
improving 
transparency 
and 
accountability 
in 
the 
public financial 
management, 
reducing 
conflict 
of 
interest and 
corruption 
in 
the 
business 
environment, 
and 
strengthening 
the 
banking 
sector.  These 
activities, 
including 
modernization 
of 
treasury operations 
and strengthening 
the 
capacity 
of 
the 
Chamber 
of 
Accounts 
and 
strengthening 
the 
commercial 
banking 
sector, 
will 
be 
supported 
by 
the 
ongoing 
IDA-financed Institutional 
Building 
Technical 
Assistance 
Credit 
(IBTA-2) 
and the 
Financial 
Sector 
Technical 
Assistance 
Credit, 
as 
well 
as 
with 
support 
from 
IMF, 
EU 
and 
USAID. 
The 
Country 
Portfolio 
Performance 
Review 
(CPPR) 
of 
January 
2002 
identified 
common 
financial 
management 
issues 
affecting implementation 
of 
IDA-financed 
projects. 
A 
Worlung 
Group 
was 
established 
to 
ensure 
common 
issues 
are 
appropriately 
addressed 
(delays 
in 
approving legal 
documents 
by 
the 
Government, arrears 
in 
counterpart 
funding 
and 
financing 
of 
duties 
and 
taxes). 
Within 
its 
Indicative 
Program 
for 
2004-2006, 
the 
EU/TACIS 
will 
assist 
the 
Government 
in 
the 
introduction and application 
of 
international 
accounting 
standards 
in 
the country. 
5. 
Environmental: 
5.1 
Summarize the 
steps 
undertaken 
for 
environmental 
assessment 
and 
EMP 
preparation 
(including 
consultation 
and 
disclosure) 
and 
the 
significant 
issues 
and 
their 
treatment 
emerging 
from 
this 
analysis. 
At 
the request 
of 
the 
Government 
of 
Azerbaijan, 
the 
Food 
and 
Agriculture 
Organization (FAO) 
assembled 
a 
multi-disciplinary 
team 
of 
international 
and 
national environmental 
experts 
to 
conduct 
the 
environmental 
assessment 
(EA) 
and 
prepare 
the 
environmental 
management 
and 
monitoring 
plan 
(EMMP).  The 
EA 
team 
had 
missions 
in 
Azerbaijan 
in 
June, 
September, 
and December 
2002, 
and 
February 
2003, 
to 
gather 
the 
baseline 
information 
for 
the 
EA, 
visit 
the 
various proposed 
project 
areas, 
view 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
conditions, and meet 
with 
national 
and 
local 
officials, 
representatives 
of 
non-governmental 
organizations, 
water 
user 
associations, farmers, 
and 
other 
beneficiaries 
of 
the 
project. 
During 
the February 
mission 
the 
EA 
team 
conducted 
public 
consultations 
in 
Baku 
and 
in 
Sabirabad 
with 
officials, 
NGOs, and 
project 
beneficiaries 
on 
the 
draft 
ENEMMP. 
Environmental 
Category: 
B 
(Partial 
Assessment) 
The 
EA 
confirmed 
the 
Category 
B 
designation 
for 
the 
project, 
finding 
no 
significant, 
irreversible, cumulative 
or 
long-term 
adverse 
impacts. 
In 
fact, 
the 
EA 
identified 
a number 
of 
positive 
impacts 
of 
the 
project 
(such 
as 
reduction 
in 
water 
losses, 
waterlogging 
and 
soil 
salinity; 
and 
enhanced 
water 
resources 
management). 
Potential 
negative impacts that were 
identified 
could 
be 
effectively 
prevented 
or 
reduced 
through application 
of 
appropriate 
preventive 
actions 
or mitigation 
measures. 
The 
potential 
negative impacts 
are 
described 
in 
Annex 
11, 
which 
presents 
the 
Executive 
Summary 
of 
the 
EA. 
The 
EA 
also 
confirmed 
the 
application 
of 
the 
safeguard 
policies 
on 
projects 
on 
international 
waterways 
and 
safety 
of 
dams, 
and 
examined 
but 
rejected application 
of 
the 
safeguard 
policies 
for 
natural 
habitats 
and 
pest 
management 
to 
the 
project. 
Finally, 
the 
EA 
determined that 
the 
project 
does 
not 
trigger 
any 
of 
the 
remaining 
safeguard 
policies 
involving 
forestry, 
cultural 
property, indigenous 
peoples, 
involuntary 
resettlement 
or 
projects 
in 
disputed 
areas. 
- 
34 
- 
Page  39
Although 
finding 
no 
significant 
adverse 
impacts 
from 
the project, 
the 
EA 
nevertheless 
recommended 
the 
following 
preventive 
and 
mitigative 
measures, 
supported 
by 
environmental 
monitoring, 
to 
address 
the 
few 
potential 
negative 
impacts 
identified: 
(i) 
environmental 
management 
guidelines 
and 
appropriate site 
management 
plans 
to 
minimize 
any 
damage 
caused 
by 
contractors 
during 
construction 
activities 
and 
ensure 
proper 
disposal 
of 
excavated sediment and 
coqtruction 
waste; 
(ii) 
proper 
management 
practices 
and 
water 
quality 
and 
quantity 
monitoring to 
address 
the 
potential 
decrease 
in 
water 
quality and 
risk 
of 
reduction 
in 
ecological 
flow; 
(iii) 
technical 
assistance 
to 
promote 
improved 
water and 
soil 
management 
practices 
to 
prevent any 
potential 
waterlogging, 
salinization, 
or 
erosion 
of 
soils; 
(iv) 
phasing 
of 
rehabilitation activities 
in 
sensitive 
areas 
and ecological 
monitoring 
to 
detect 
any 
potential 
adverse 
changes 
in 
the 
hydrology 
of 
sensitive 
wetlands; 
(v) 
extension 
services 
in 
environmental 
health 
practices 
and 
monitoring to 
prevent any 
increase 
in 
water-related 
diseases; 
(vi) 
technical 
assistance 
to 
strengthen water management 
practices 
by 
WUAs 
in 
order to 
minimize 
water 
user 
conflicts; 
and 
(vii) 
extension 
services 
in 
integrated 
pest 
and 
production 
management 
to 
reduce any 
potential 
future 
agro-chemical 
pollution 
resulting 
from 
increased 
use 
of 
fertilizers 
or 
pesticides. 
5.2 
What 
are 
the 
main 
features 
of 
the 
EMP 
and 
are 
they 
adequate? 
The 
EMMP 
identifies: 
(i) 
environmental 
screening 
and 
review 
procedures; 
(ii) 
capacity-building 
and 
training 
activities; 
and 
(iii) 
environmental 
monitoring 
programs that 
would 
be 
undertaken 
during 
project implementation 
to 
ensure 
that 
the 
environmental 
and 
social 
management 
principles 
and 
practices 
identified 
by 
the 
EA 
are 
incorporated 
into 
project activities 
and that 
any 
potential 
adverse 
environmental 
impacts 
are 
either 
prevented 
or 
minimized.  The 
EMMP 
has 
been 
fully 
budgeted, 
with 
an 
estimated 
allocation 
of 
about 
US 
$300,000 
over 
the 
life 
of 
the project. 
Environmental 
Screening 
and 
Review 
of 
Sub-Projects. 
The 
demand-driven nature 
of 
the 
project 
means 
that the 
exact 
number 
and 
location 
of 
the 
irrigation 
infrastructure 
rehabilitation 
sub-projects 
to 
be 
financed 
by 
the 
project 
have 
yet 
to 
be 
determined.  Therefore, 
in 
order 
to 
ensure 
that 
the 
appropriate 
preventive actions 
and 
mitigation 
measures 
are 
applied 
to 
specific 
sub-project 
rehabilitation 
sites 
on 
a 
case-by-case 
basis, 
the 
EMMP 
includes 
environmental 
screening 
and 
review 
procedures. 
The 
objective 
of 
these 
procedures 
is 
to review 
the 
individual 
infrastructure 
rehabilitation 
sub-projects 
for 
the 
purpose 
of 
identifying 
and 
addressing 
(preventing 
or minimizing) 
the 
site-specific 
potential 
adverse 
environmental 
impacts. 
Where 
potential 
impacts 
identified 
through 
this 
process 
are 
minor, they 
would 
be 
addressed 
using 
the 
appropriate preventive actions 
and 
mitigation 
measures 
specified 
in 
the 
EA. 
More 
significant 
impacts 
may 
require additional 
review 
by 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Ecology 
and 
Natural 
Resources 
and 
implementation 
of 
specifically 
identified 
preventive 
actions 
and 
mitigation 
measures. 
Capacity 
Building 
and 
Training. 
In 
order 
to 
ensure 
proper implementation 
of 
the 
environmental 
screening 
and 
review 
procedures 
and 
resulting preventive 
actions 
and 
mitigation 
measures, 
the 
project 
would 
include 
capacity 
building 
and 
training 
for 
environmental 
assessmentlmanagement 
through 
training 
of 
PKJ 
and 
SU 
technical 
staff. 
For 
this 
purpose, 
the 
project 
would 
provide 
short-term 
national 
and 
international 
experts 
to 
deliver 
a 
range 
of 
technical 
assistance 
and 
training 
on 
environmental 
issues 
to 
PIU 
management 
and 
staff 
and 
to 
central 
and 
field 
office 
staff 
in 
SAIC. 
The 
PIU 
would 
employ an 
environmental 
specialist 
to 
perform 
environmental oversight 
functions 
for 
the 
life 
of 
the 
project. 
The 
project 
also 
would 
ensure 
that 
WUAs 
participating 
in 
the 
rehabilitation 
component 
of 
the 
project 
each 
designate an 
environmental counterpart 
to provide 
a 
point 
of 
contact 
for 
the 
PIU 
environmental 
specialist, 
raise environmental 
awareness 
within 
the 
WUA, 
and 
ensure 
that 
environmental 
measures are 
observed 
in 
execution 
of 
any 
infrastructure 
rehabilitation works 
within 
the 
areas 
managed 
by 
the 
WUAs. 
Environmental 
Monitoring 
Program. 
The 
project 
would 
monitor 
the 
environmental and social 
indicators 
identified 
in 
the 
EAS 
analysis 
of 
preventive 
actions 
and 
mitigation 
measures. 
The 
purpose 
in 
monitoring 
these 
indicators 
is 
to 
determine, 
to 
the extent 
possible, 
the 
direct and 
indirect 
environmental 
- 
35 
- 
Page  40
and social 
impacts 
of 
project activities 
and, 
where 
necessary, 
make 
appropriate 
corrections to 
project 
activities 
in 
order 
to 
prevent 
or 
lessen adverse 
impacts. 
For 
example, 
under 
RIDIP, 
the 
PIU 
began 
an 
ecological 
monitoring 
program 
for 
the 
important 
lakes and 
wetlands 
in 
the 
project 
area 
(Lakes 
Ag-gel 
and 
Sarisu). 
This 
monitoring 
would 
be 
continued 
under 
the 
new 
project, 
as 
needed. 
Further, 
the 
PIU 
would 
administer 
and 
coordinate 
as 
necessary 
with 
SAIC, 
the 
MENR, 
and 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Health (MoH) 
the 
additional 
monitoring 
activities 
identified 
in 
the 
EMMP. 
The 
PIU 
environmental 
specialist 
would 
supervise these 
environmental 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation functions 
for 
the 
project 
and 
contract 
additional 
technical support 
as 
needed. 
The 
EMMP 
is 
considered 
part 
of 
overall project 
implementation 
and 
would 
be 
annexed 
to 
the 
Project Implementation 
Plan. 
The 
Development 
Credit 
Agreement 
has 
a 
covenant 
requiring 
execution 
of 
the 
EMMP 
by 
the 
Borrower.  The 
PIU 
would 
report 
on 
EMMP 
implementation 
in 
its 
regular 
project 
reporting 
to 
the 
IDA, 
and supervision 
missions 
would 
evaluate progress 
in 
its 
implementation. 
5.3 
For 
Category 
A 
and 
B 
projects, 
timeline 
and 
status 
of 
EA: 
Date 
of 
receipt 
of 
final 
draft: 
March 
3,2003 
The 
EA 
report 
has 
been translated 
in 
Azeri 
and 
is 
publicly 
available 
in 
SAIC. 
The 
Association 
was 
informed 
in 
a 
formal 
letter 
from 
SAIC 
of 
March 
20,2003  that the 
EA 
report 
can 
be 
submitted 
to 
the 
Infoshop, 
which 
was done 
the 
same 
day. 
The 
Ministry 
of 
Ecology 
and 
Natural 
Resources 
carried out an 
Environmental 
Expertise. 
The 
official 
written 
response 
of 
April 
19,2003 
was 
positive 
and 
the 
EA 
and 
EMMP 
were 
approved. 
The project 
would 
be 
fully 
supported 
by 
the 
MENR. 
5.4 
How 
have 
stakeholders been 
consulted at 
the 
stage 
of 
(a) 
environmental 
screening 
and 
(b) 
draft 
EA 
report 
on 
the 
environmental 
impacts 
and 
proposed environment 
management 
plan? 
Describe 
mechanisms 
of 
consultation 
that were 
used 
and 
which 
groups 
were 
consulted? 
Environmenlal 
Screening. 
The 
EA 
team began 
the 
process 
of 
consulting 
the relevant 
stakeholders 
and 
beneficiaries 
of 
the 
project 
during 
its 
three 
environmental 
screening missions. 
It 
held 
meetings 
with 
officials 
in 
the SAIC, 
the 
MENR, 
the 
MoH, 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Agriculture 
(MOA) 
and 
other 
national 
institutions; 
with 
local 
SAIC 
officials 
and 
WUA 
officers 
and 
members 
in 
a number 
of 
the 
project 
area 
raions; 
with 
representatives 
of 
national 
environmental and 
social 
NGOs; 
and 
with 
farmers 
and other 
beneficiaries 
of 
the 
project 
in 
the 
field. 
The 
list 
of 
stakeholders 
met 
during 
the 
environmental 
screening process can 
be 
found 
in 
Annex 
D 
of 
the 
ENEMMP 
report. 
Draft 
EA/EMMP. 
The 
EA 
team 
continued 
and 
expanded 
the 
consultation 
process 
during 
its 
fourth mission 
with 
more 
formal, 
publicly 
announced 
meetings 
in 
Baku 
and 
in 
Sabirabad 
with 
stakeholders 
and 
beneficiaries 
on 
the 
findings 
and 
recommendations 
of 
the 
draft 
ENEMMP. 
The 
Baku 
consultation 
included 
government 
officials 
from 
the 
SAIC, the 
MENR, 
and the 
MOA, 
as 
well 
as 
representatives 
from 
a number 
of 
national 
NGOs. 
The 
regional consultation 
in 
Sabirabad 
included 
a 
large number 
of 
local SAIC 
officials, 
WUA 
members, 
and 
local NGOs 
from 
a 
number 
of 
the 
project 
raions. 
The detailed 
minutes 
of 
these 
meetings 
and 
lists 
of 
participants 
are 
also 
contained 
in 
Annex 
D 
of 
the 
EAEMMP. 
5.5 
What 
mechanisms have 
been 
established 
to 
monitor 
and 
evaluate 
the 
impact 
of 
the 
project 
on 
the 
environment? 
Do 
the 
indicators 
reflect 
the 
objectives 
and 
results 
of 
the 
EMP? 
As 
noted 
above, 
the 
PIU 
would 
be 
in 
charge 
of 
administering 
and 
coordinating 
the 
monitoring 
program 
for 
the 
environmental and social 
indicators 
identified 
in 
the 
EAS 
analysis 
of 
preventive 
actions 
and 
mitigation 
measures. 
The 
EMMP 
includes a 
detailed 
and 
fully 
costed 
monitoring 
program. 
Monitoring 
these 
indicators 
should 
allow 
the 
PIU 
to 
determine 
the 
direct 
and 
indirect 
environmental and 
- 
36 
- 
Page  41
social impacts 
of project activities 
and, 
where 
necessary, 
take appropriate actions 
or 
make 
corrections 
to 
project activities 
in 
order 
to 
prevent 
or 
lessen 
any 
adverse 
impacts 
detected. 
6. 
Social: 
6.1  Summarize 
key 
social 
issues 
relevant 
to 
the 
project 
objectives, 
and specify 
the 
project's social 
development 
outcomes. 
Preparation 
of 
the 
IDSMIP 
made 
use 
of 
the 
social 
assessment 
and baseline 
survey 
for 
the 
IUDIP, 
and 
included 
a social 
diagnosis, 
carried 
out 
as 
part 
of 
the 
multi-sectoral 
diagnosis 
of 
former 
state 
and 
collective 
farms 
(now 
WUA 
areas). 
The 
findings 
are 
summarized 
in 
Annex 
12. 
This 
section 
provides 
a 
synopsis 
of 
major 
points 
that 
emerged 
from 
the preparation 
activities 
and 
discusses 
implications 
for 
the 
project. 
RIDIP 
Social 
Assessment. 
The 
social 
assessment 
for 
IUDIP, 
carried out 
in 
late 
1999, 
included 
two 
large 
irrigation 
zones 
in 
the 
Samur and 
KurdAraz 
areas, 
in 
which 
a 
survey 
was 
conducted 
of 
1,600 
households 
and 
staff 
of 
about 
400 
different 
entities 
(farms, 
non-agricultural 
and 
state 
enterprises, 
etc.). 
It 
was designed 
to 
understand a 
number 
of 
issues: 
(i) 
problems 
with 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
infrastructure 
and 
the 
contribution 
of 
unsatisfactory 
water 
supply 
to 
low 
agricultural 
production; 
(ii) 
the 
extent 
to which 
the 
reluctance 
of 
water 
users 
to 
pay 
for 
water use 
is 
related 
to 
the 
unreliability 
of 
irrigation 
service 
and 
inadequacy 
of 
supply, 
as 
well 
as 
ability 
to 
pay; 
(iii) 
the 
extent 
to which 
water 
users 
feel 
the 
need 
to 
be 
involved 
in 
operation 
and 
maintenance 
of 
irrigation 
infrastructure 
and 
water management; 
(iv) 
the 
willingness 
of 
water 
users 
to 
organize themselves; 
and 
(v) 
level 
of 
interest 
of 
users 
to 
take 
responsibility 
for 
system 
management. 
The 
results 
revealed 
that: 
(i) 
the 
principal 
problems 
with 
the 
existing 
irrigation 
systems 
supplies 
are 
unreliable 
and 
inadequate supplies and 
poor 
and 
deteriorating 
drainage; 
(ii) 
farmers 
would 
be 
willing 
to 
pay 
for 
better 
service; 
(iii) 
farmers 
are 
willing 
to 
be 
organized, 
but 
feel 
that 
they 
don't 
have 
the 
skills 
to 
organize; 
and 
(iv) 
farmers 
want 
to 
have 
more responsibility 
for 
decisions 
that 
affect 
their 
livelihood. 
RIDIP 
Baseline 
Survey. 
The 
RIDIP 
baseline survey, 
carried out 
in 
2002, 
included 
a 
random 
selection 
of 
485 
households 
in 
the 
two 
areas, 
including 
55 
control 
households 
outside 
the 
project 
areas. 
The 
survey 
focused 
primarily 
on 
agricultural activities 
and 
household 
budgets. 
Findings 
were 
similar 
to 
those 
of 
the 
earlier work, 
refined 
by 
an 
assessment 
of 
the 
impact 
on 
yields 
of 
reduced water 
supplies. 
IDSMIP 
Social Diagnosis. 
The 
Social Diagnosis 
was 
carried 
out 
in 
six 
WUAs 
that were 
assessed 
by 
a 
multi-disciplinary 
specialist 
team. 
In 
each 
site, 
the 
Social 
Diagnosis 
consisted 
of 
key 
informant 
interviews 
of 
officials 
of 
local 
self 
government, 
SAIC 
and 
the 
WUA; 
nine 
focus groups 
on 
different 
topics 
with 
different 
members 
of 
the 
communities; a 
series 
of 
Participatory 
Rapid 
Assessment 
exercises; 
and 
fifteen 
household 
interviews 
in 
a 
stratified 
sample. 
Among 
the 
principal 
findings 
of 
the 
Social 
Diagnosis 
are 
the 
following: 
(i) 
economic 
stratification 
is 
becoming more prominent 
in 
the 
WUA 
areas, 
based 
on 
access 
to 
well-irrigated 
land, 
among other 
factors; 
(ii) 
for 
the 
most part, 
the 
WUAs 
established 
over 
the 
last 
two 
years 
are 
led 
by 
members 
of 
the 
old 
state 
and 
collective 
farm 
management 
cadre; 
(iii) 
farmers have accepted 
the 
WUA 
concept 
but 
see 
them 
as 
administrative, rather 
than 
representative organizations; 
(iv) 
the incidence 
of 
payment 
of 
water 
tariffs 
has 
increased 
markedly 
and 
most 
farmers 
say 
they 
are 
willing 
to pay 
higher 
tariffs 
if 
they 
receive 
better 
irrigation 
service; 
(v) 
nonetheless, 
as 
much 
as 
35 
percent 
of 
WUA 
members 
in 
some 
communities 
may not 
be 
able 
to 
sustain 
much 
higher 
tariffs, 
at 
least 
until 
after 
they 
benefit 
from 
improved irrigation 
service; 
(vi) 
the 
level 
of 
information 
available 
to 
WUA 
members 
is 
very 
limited 
and 
directed 
only 
to 
male 
heads 
of 
household; 
(vii) 
the 
relationship between the 
WUA 
and 
local 
self-government 
is 
ambiguous, 
but 
not 
necessarily 
problematical; 
(viii) 
the 
status 
of 
Internally 
Displaced 
People 
(IDP) 
is 
ambiguous 
within 
communities 
and, 
despite 
the 
fact that they 
are 
irrigation 
water 
users, 
IDPs 
are 
virtually invisible 
to 
WUAs; 
and 
(ix) 
- 
37 
- 
Page  42
successful 
restructuring 
of 
existing 
WUAs 
along 
the 
lines 
envisioned 
in 
the 
project 
would 
require 
a 
major, 
sustained 
information 
campaign that 
reaches 
neighborhoods 
and 
individual 
households. 
Social 
Issues 
Based 
on 
the 
social 
analysis and other 
preparatory work, 
it 
is 
clear 
that 
five 
social 
issues are 
relevant 
to 
the 
project 
- 
poverty, equity, 
empowerment, 
gender, 
and social 
inclusion 
- 
either 
directly 
or 
indirectly. 
They 
would 
be 
addressed 
in 
the 
project 
through various 
means. 
Poverty. 
This 
is 
not 
a 
targeted, 
or 
focused 
poverty 
alleviation 
project, 
as 
such. 
Rather 
it 
would 
contribute 
to poverty 
reduction 
as 
an 
inclusive 
operation, that 
affects a 
broad group 
of 
people, 
increasing 
economic 
opportunities; and 
as 
an 
enabling 
operation 
that 
promotes 
local 
institutions 
that 
empower 
irrigated 
farmers and 
enhance 
their 
economic security. 
The 
target 
population 
includes 
all 
farmers 
with 
land 
in 
the 
irrigated 
areas 
of 
former 
farms 
in 
selected regions. 
The 
relative impact 
on 
different 
people 
would 
be 
marked, however.  Those 
who 
would 
benefit 
most 
are 
farmers 
on 
the 
margins 
of 
the system 
whose 
access 
to 
water 
has 
declined 
as 
the 
systems 
deteriorated.  These 
farm 
families 
receive 
no 
water 
or 
limited 
and 
irregular 
service, 
causing 
them 
to 
obtain 
limited 
yields 
or 
lose 
crops, 
depending 
on 
the 
extent 
to which 
they 
actually 
receive 
water. 
Some 
marginal 
areas 
have gone 
out 
of 
production 
or 
switched 
to 
rainfed 
crops, 
in 
the 
areas 
with 
best 
rainfall. 
The 
people 
who 
farm 
these 
areas 
are 
either 
already 
impoverished 
or 
suffer 
declining 
incomes 
as 
the 
situation 
worsens; 
rehabilitation 
would 
enable 
them 
to 
reverse 
this 
downward 
spiral.  Farmers 
in 
more 
favorable 
parts 
of 
the 
system 
also 
suffer 
from 
deteriorated infrastructure and 
poor 
water 
distribution 
management; 
physical 
and 
management 
improvements 
would 
reduce 
uncertainty 
and 
enable 
them 
to 
increase 
their 
incomes, 
as 
well. 
The 
recent 
baseline survey 
for 
the 
RIDIP 
demonstrated 
that 
farmers 
who 
receive 
adequate 
water 
supplies 
obtain 
average 
wheat 
yields 
of 
farmers three 
times 
as 
high 
as 
the farmers 
who 
get 
half 
of 
their 
water 
requirements. 
Equity. 
The 
privatization 
and 
restructuring 
of 
former 
farms 
aimed 
to 
distribute 
land 
assets 
equitably, based 
on 
a 
lottery 
system. 
Differences 
in 
land 
quality, location, 
family 
size, 
and 
farming 
skills 
inevitably 
were translated 
into 
a 
widening 
spectrum 
of 
family 
incomes. 
The 
discrepancy between 
high 
and 
low 
incomes 
was 
exacerbated 
by 
inequitable 
access 
to 
water, 
however, 
due 
both to 
a 
vacuum 
in 
the 
management 
of 
irrigation 
infrastructure 
and 
preferential 
access 
of 
former 
managers 
and 
other 
influential 
people. 
The 
social 
diagnosis 
revealed 
a 
common perception 
that 
the 
new 
WUAs 
hastily 
created 
over 
the 
last 
two 
years are 
dominated 
by 
a 
few 
farmers whose 
interests 
are 
given 
priority. 
The 
project 
would 
promote equity 
by 
helping 
to 
develop strong 
WUAs 
that 
are 
accountable 
to 
all 
of 
the 
membership.  Once 
they 
are 
able 
to 
guarantee 
equitable 
access 
to 
water, 
the 
WUAs 
would 
enable 
all 
capable farmers, 
not 
just 
the large and 
influential 
farmers, 
to 
improve 
their 
economic 
status. 
Empowerment. 
The 
impact 
of 
the 
privatization 
of 
former 
farms 
has 
been mixed. 
Most 
members 
of 
the 
former 
farm 
communities, 
including 
those 
not 
engaged 
in 
agriculture, 
welcomed 
the 
opportunity 
to 
have 
access 
to 
land 
to 
use 
for 
their 
own 
purposes, 
or 
lease 
to 
others. 
They 
soon 
discovered, 
however, 
that 
their 
aspirations were 
stymied 
by 
limited 
skills, 
as 
well 
as 
limited 
access 
to 
inputs 
ranging 
from 
technical 
information to 
regular water 
supplies. 
As 
systems 
deteriorated, 
access 
to 
water 
became 
more 
unreliable 
and 
inequitable, 
increasing 
both 
disparities 
and 
disillusionment. 
Respondents 
in 
the 
RTDIP 
social 
assessment 
strongly 
supported 
the 
idea 
of 
creating 
WUAs, 
but 
felt 
that 
they 
themselves 
were 
unable 
to 
create 
them. 
Consequently, 
SAIC's 
effort 
to 
create 
WAS 
met 
this 
need and 
was 
rapidly 
accepted. 
The 
many 
WAS 
that 
resulted 
from 
this 
top-down 
approach 
are 
limited 
in 
scope 
and 
competence and administrative, 
rather 
than 
representative; consequently 
they 
have 
little 
grass-roots 
support. 
The 
project 
seeks 
to 
empower water 
users 
on 
two 
levels: 
first, 
by 
restructuring 
and 
providing 
support 
to 
WUAs 
to 
enable 
them 
to 
be 
autonomous, representative, competent 
and 
accountable 
to 
their 
- 
38 
- 
Page  43
members, 
and 
to 
operate 
efficiently 
and transparently; 
and 
second, 
by 
instituting 
long 
term 
SAIC-WUA 
contracts 
to 
make SAIC 
responsible 
for 
meeting 
its 
water 
delivery 
obligations. 
These 
steps 
are 
necessary 
but 
not sufficient 
for 
sustainable 
agriculture 
in 
Azerbaijan, 
thus 
the 
project 
would work 
in 
areas 
where 
other 
projects 
are 
in 
place 
to 
address 
other constraints. 
Gender. 
The 
social 
diagnosis 
of 
sample farms 
focused 
on 
the 
influence 
of 
gender 
in 
the 
distribution 
of 
land 
and 
property 
and 
the 
creation 
of 
economically viable 
farming 
units, 
particularly 
those 
owned 
by 
female-headed households. 
The 
diagnosis 
concluded that 
the 
impact 
of 
privatization, 
in 
general, was greater 
on 
women than 
men, 
as 
women 
were 
the 
first 
to 
lose 
jobs 
due 
to 
downsizing, 
but 
gender 
differentials 
appear 
to 
be 
negligible 
in 
terms 
of 
the 
distribution 
of 
farm 
assets. 
Indeed, 
informal 
mechanisms 
emerged 
among extended 
families 
to 
help 
female-headed households 
manage 
their 
lands. 
Nonetheless, 
the 
social 
diagnosis 
revealed 
that 
information 
networks 
of 
women 
are 
much 
more 
limited 
than 
those 
of 
men, 
particularly 
regarding 
institutional 
changes, 
such 
as 
the 
creation 
of 
WAS, 
and 
their 
implications 
for 
increased water 
tariffs, 
and 
agricultural 
technologies. 
The 
project 
would 
thus 
target 
women 
as 
well 
as 
men 
in 
various 
information 
dissemination 
efforts 
in 
order 
to 
ensure that 
all 
members 
of 
the 
community 
are 
informed 
about 
the 
project 
and 
WUA 
activities,  especially 
those 
which 
have an 
impact 
on 
the 
family 
economy. 
Social Inclusion. 
The social 
diagnosis 
revealed 
growing 
economic 
polarization 
in 
the 
subject 
communities, 
significantly 
exacerbated 
by 
differential 
access 
to 
water 
based 
on 
influence 
and economic 
status. 
Until 
they 
receive 
reliable 
water 
supplies, 
as 
many 
of 
one-third 
of 
water 
users 
may 
be 
unable 
to 
pay 
increased service 
fees. 
This 
would 
present 
a 
challenge 
to 
WUAs 
that 
need 
to 
be 
sustainable, 
yet 
serve 
all 
water 
users, 
including IDPs 
and 
marginal 
farmers whose 
vulnerability 
is 
related 
to 
social factors 
and the 
location 
of 
their 
farm 
plots. 
First, 
WUAs 
that 
ensure 
equitable 
access 
to 
water 
can 
not only 
reduce 
economic 
polarization 
but 
also 
eliminate 
many 
of 
its 
consequences 
that 
take the 
form 
of 
social 
exclusion. 
This 
would 
not 
entirely 
eliminate 
the 
need 
for 
each 
WUA 
to 
decide 
up-front 
how 
to 
deal 
with 
members 
who 
are 
really too poor 
to 
pay 
increased service 
fees 
in 
an equitable, 
socially inclusive 
manner, 
however. 
Second, 
WUAs 
that 
effectively 
recognize 
the 
importance 
of 
all 
stakeholders, 
including 
IDPs, 
and integrate 
them 
into 
their 
activities, can 
,significantly 
increase 
the 
level 
of 
communication 
between 
IDPs 
and 
other 
members 
of 
the 
community and help bridge 
the 
gaps 
of 
the 
relative 
isolation 
that 
was 
described 
in 
the 
2002 
Poverty 
Assessment. 
Social 
Development 
Outcomes 
The 
anticipated social 
development outcomes 
of 
the 
project 
are 
related 
to 
the 
issues 
discussed 
above. 
Decreased 
Poverty. 
The 
project 
is 
expected 
to 
enhance 
the 
incomes 
of 
irrigated 
farmers 
by 
increasing 
the 
reliability 
of 
irrigation 
water 
delivery 
through 
improvements 
in 
water 
distribution 
and 
management 
by 
restructured, strengthened 
WUAs 
and 
rehabilitation 
of 
the 
physical 
infrastructure. 
All 
farmers 
are 
expected 
to benefit, 
but 
those 
who 
would 
qualitatively 
benefit 
the 
most 
are 
the 
farmers whose 
lands 
are 
located 
in 
marginal 
areas 
and 
currently suffer 
from 
the most unreliable, 
inadequate 
water 
delivery. 
Increased 
Equity. 
SAIC 
and 
WUAs 
are 
now 
most 
responsive 
to 
the needs 
of 
large and 
influential 
farmers. 
The 
project 
would 
help 
restructure 
and 
strengthen 
WUAs 
to 
separate 
governance 
and 
management, 
to 
make 
them 
representative 
and 
accountable, 
and 
to 
increase 
their 
managerial 
and 
technical 
competence. 
This 
would 
ensure 
that 
all 
water 
users 
receive equitable 
access 
to 
water and 
the 
needs 
of 
all 
members 
are 
met. 
- 
39 
- 
Page  44
Empowerment 
on 
Different 
Levels. 
Through 
project 
interventions, farmers 
would 
be 
empowered 
to 
exercise 
their 
skills 
productively 
under 
reliable 
irrigation 
regimes, 
enabling 
them 
to 
increase 
their 
production and 
incomes and 
to 
pay 
reasonable 
water 
service 
fees. 
Strong, accountable 
WUAs 
would 
be 
empowered 
to 
manage 
the 
on-farm 
irrigation 
systems 
effectively 
and 
efficiently to 
serve 
all 
farmers, 
and 
to 
demand 
that 
SAIC 
deliver 
the water 
supplies 
for 
which 
they 
have contracted 
and 
paid. 
SAIC would 
be 
empowered 
to 
carry 
out 
its 
traditional 
functions 
adequately 
and 
efficiently, 
as 
well 
as 
its 
new 
WUA 
support 
function. 
Gender Equity. 
The 
project 
would 
greatly 
enhance 
the 
level 
of 
information 
available 
to 
members 
of 
farm 
communities, 
WUAs 
and 
SAIC 
field 
staff. 
It 
would 
disseminate 
available materials and develop 
new 
materials 
and new information 
channels, 
as 
needed, 
to 
ensure 
that 
both 
men 
and 
women 
get the 
information they 
need 
for 
WUA 
families 
to 
make 
informed 
decisions 
regarding 
WUAs, 
water 
management 
and irrigation 
service 
fees. 
Increased 
Social Inclusion. 
Informed 
membership and 
representative, accountable 
WUA 
leadership 
would 
promote 
social 
inclusion 
by 
reducing 
economic 
disparities 
caused 
by 
inequitable 
access 
to 
irrigation 
water; 
enforcing 
rules 
and regulations 
equally 
on all 
members 
to 
eliminate 
suspicions 
of 
privileged 
treatment; 
developing 
consensus 
on 
ways 
to 
respond 
to 
the 
needs 
of 
poor 
WUA 
members, 
such 
as 
cross-subsidies, 
if 
appropriate; and 
including 
IDPs 
as 
active 
stakeholders 
in 
the 
WUA, 
reducing 
their 
marginal 
status 
and increasing 
the 
level 
of 
communication 
between 
them 
and other 
members 
of 
the 
community. 
6.2 
Participatory 
Approach: 
How 
are 
key 
stakeholders 
participating 
in 
the 
project? 
The project 
affects 
four 
distinct 
stakeholder 
groups, 
three 
of 
which 
have 
recognizable 
SUb-grOUpS. 
WAS. 
This 
can 
comprise a 
number 
of 
sub-groups, 
depending 
on 
the 
system: 
prominent 
members, 
characterized 
as 
"business 
farmers" 
in 
the Social Diagnosis; regular 
members; 
invisible 
members, such 
as 
IDPs 
and women; and managers. 
Each 
of 
these 
groups were 
consulted 
during 
project 
preparation, 
both 
directly 
through 
the 
diagnostic 
process, 
and 
indirectly 
through 
various 
field 
discussions. 
WUAs 
are 
the target 
units 
of 
the 
project, 
thus 
they 
would 
be 
directly 
involved 
in 
implementation, 
as 
well. 
Eligibility 
for 
rehabilitation 
funding 
would 
be 
based 
on 
demand 
and 
WUA 
performance, 
thus 
the 
project 
is 
designed 
to 
empower 
WUAs 
and 
strengthen 
their 
capacity 
to 
operate 
effectively. 
SAIC. 
The 
agency 
includes 
three 
sets 
of 
stakeholders 
who 
have been engaged 
in 
project 
design 
and 
would 
be 
responsible 
for 
implementation: 
staff 
in 
headquarters 
had primary 
responsibility 
for 
project 
design 
and 
would 
be 
responsible 
for 
implementation; 
one 
set 
of 
field 
staff 
in 
regional offices 
with 
responsibility 
for 
operating 
major 
structures, 
helped 
identify 
rehabilitation 
and 
reconstruction 
needs 
and 
would 
oversee 
the design 
and 
construction 
of 
the 
major 
infrastructure 
investments; 
and 
staff 
of 
the 
new 
WUA 
Support 
Units 
in 
raion 
offices 
would 
be 
supported 
as 
they 
define 
their 
new 
roles 
and 
assume 
responsibility 
for 
carrying them 
out. 
Local 
self-government. 
Officials 
of 
local 
self-government 
have 
participated 
in 
many 
discussions 
about 
the 
project, 
both 
in 
field 
visits 
and, 
more 
systematically, 
during

They 
have 
helped 
developing 
the 
project 
design 
through 
their 
insights 
regarding community 
structure and 
function 
and 
would 
act 
as 
silent 
partners 
during 
implementation. Meanwhile, 
as 
WUAs 
become stronger 
and 
more 
autonomous, 
they 
would 
each 
develop 
predictable 
relationships 
with 
local 
self-government, 
defining 
respective roles, 
responsibilities 
and 
boundaries.

Contractors.

The

are 
standard

The

engineers

would

to

with

as 
advisors and partners, even 
if 
SAIC 
issues 
the contracts.

Environmental

6.5 
How 
will 
the 
project monitor 
performance 
in 
terms 
of 
social 
development 
outcomes? 
The 
basic 
monitoring 
unit 
for 
social 
development outcomes 
is 
the 
WUA. 
The project 
would 
work 
intensively 
with 
WUAs 
in 
11 
raions. 
In 
each 
of 
these 
raions, 
some 
WUAs 
would 
eventually be 
eligible 
for 
rehabilitation 
support, based 
on

to 
the 
functioning 
of

WUA. 
Each 
WUA 
would 
carry out a 
multi-discipline 
diagnosis, 
patterned after 
those 
undertaken 
during 
project 
preparation. 
The 
staff 
of 
the 
Support 
Units 
would 
help 
WUA 
members 
carry out 
the social 
diagnosis, 
which would 
be 
used 
to 
develop 
a 
social/management 
agenda 
that 
clearly 
identifies 
relevant 
social 
issues 
in 
the 
WUA 
area, 
assesses 
their 
implications 
for 
the 
success 
of 
the 
WUA, 
sets 
appropriate objectives (social 
development outcomes) 
and 
sets 
benchmarks 
for 
monitoring 
and evaluating 
success. 
These benchmarks 
would 
then 
be 
aggregated 
and 
evaluated 
at 
the 
project 
level, 
as 
well. 
The

in 
the

a

and

program, 
provide 
technical 
assistance 
to

Units 
and 
WUAs 
to 
use 
it, 
and 
evaluate progress 
periodically 
through 
information 
updates 
and 
field 
assessments.

Assessment: 
refer to 
the 
text 
under

Safety 
ofDams: 
In 
1998, 
a 
dam 
safety 
assessment 
of 
Mingechevir 
and Shamkir 
dams 
was 
carried 
out, 
which 
concluded that 
the 
two 
dams are 
in 
good condition. 
A 
more 
detailed 
assessment 
was 
made 
by 
a 
team 
of 
Azeri 
specialists 
at Shamkir 
in 
1999 
and 
Mingechevir 
in 
2000, and 
the 
dams 
were 
again 
found 
to 
be 
in 
satisfactory condition. 
In 
MarchiApril 
2003 a 
follow-on 
dam 
safety 
assessment 
for 
Mingechevir 
and 
Araz 
Dams 
was 
carried out 
by 
a 
team 
of 
international dam 
specialists. 
The 
assessment 
did 
not 
reveal 
any 
major 
structural and managerial dam 
safety issues 
that 
would 
have 
to 
be 
addressed 
under 
the 
project. 
Projects 
in International 
Waters: 
Many 
of 
the 
project 
farms 
would 
be 
located 
within 
basins 
of 
international 
waterways, 
therefore 
OP 
7.50 
applies. 
Azerbaijan 
is 
the 
most 
downstream 
country 
for 
all 
international 
rivers. 
The 
proposed 
rehabilitation works 
under 
IDSMIP 
would 
not 
have 
a 
significant 
adverse change 
to 
the 
quality 
or 
quantity 
of 
water flows, 
while 
the 
works 
would 
not 
change 
the nature 
of 
the 
original 
systems 
or 
alter 
or 
expand 
their 
scope 
and extent 
as 
to 
create 
a 
new or 
different 
scheme. 
The 
proposed 
project 
would 
have 
no 
impact 
on 
the 
quality 
or 
quantity 
of 
the 
river 
flow 
in 
the 
upper 
riparian 
countries. 
There 
would 
also 
be 
no 
impact 
on 
the 
Caspian 
Sea, 
since there 
would 
not 
be a 
significant 
adverse change 
to 
the 
quantity 
of 
water 
flowing 
to 
the 
Sea. 
Similarly, 
water 
use 
in 
the 
riparian 
countries 
around 
the Caspian 
Sea 
would 
not 
affect 
the 
project.  The impact 
is 
thus 
expected 
to 
be 
negligible 
and 
subject 
to 
a 
waiver 
pursuant 
to 
paragraph 7 
(a)

OP 
7.50. 
A

with 
this

ECA 
VP's

have 
as 
eligibility

WUA

that

no

of 
new 
areas

F. 
Sustainability 
and 
Risks 
1. 
Sustainability: 
the 
proposed 
project. 
The 
present 
ISF 
does 
not 
ensure 
proper 
O&M 
of 
both 
the 
on-farm 
and 
off-farm 
irrigation 
systems. 
Fees 
only 
cover about 20 
percent 
of 
current 
expenditures 
on 
off-farm 
system 
maintenance, 
excluding 
operation 
of 
maintenance 
of 
pumps 
and 
wells. 
Including 
the 
high 
cost 
of 
pumped 
irrigation, 
the 
current 
ISF 
covers 
less 
than 
5 
percent 
of 
SAIC's 
total 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
budget. 
In 
the 
future, 
due 
to 
higher 
electricity 
tariffs 
and 
pump 
maintenance expenditures, 
the 
cost 
of 
pumped 
irrigation 
is 
expected 
to 
rise 
even 
higher. 
SAIC 
is 
aware 
that 
adequate 
and 
regular 
maintenance, 
as 
well 
as 
effective 
operation 
of 
the 
rehabilitated 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
infrastructure 
are 
critical 
for 
the 
sustainability 
of 
project 
benefits. 
The 
current 
fee 
setting 
at 
very 
low 
rates 
is 
part 
of 
government's 
policy 
to 
keep 
costs 
of 
agriculture and 
irrigation to 
farmers 
to 
a 
minimum 
until 
such 
time 
that 
they 
can 
afford 
to 
gradually 
start 
paying

implementing 
agency 
that 
the 
package 
would 
be 
issued 
to 
short-listed 
firms 
soon 
after 
the 
proposed 
project 
has 
been 
presented 
to 
the 
Board for 
approval. 
The 
PIU 
also has 
procurement 
documents ready 
- 
developed 
under 
RIDIP 
- 
for 
the 
procurement 
of 
vehicles 
and 
other 
goods. 
The 
procurement 
of 
vehicles 
and 
office 
equipment 
for 
PIU 
and 
SUs 
would 
also start soon 
after 
Board 
approval. 
A 
Project Implementation 
Plan 
has 
been 
drafted 
by 
the 
Implementing 
Agency, and 
will 
be 
finalized 
immediately 
after 
Board 
presentation.

Annex 
2: 
Detailed 
Project 
Description 
AZERBAIJAN: 
IRRIGATION 
DISTRIBUTION 
SYSTEM 
& 
MANAGEMENT 
IMPROVEMENT 
PROJECT 
Introduction 
Azerbaijan 
has 
nine 
agro-climatic 
zones 
which 
offer potential 
for 
highly 
diversified 
agriculture. 
Except 
for 
areas 
near 
the 
foothills 
and 
the 
south-eastern 
part 
of 
the 
country, 
agriculture 
is 
largely 
dependent 
on 
irrigation 
due 
to 
low 
rainfall. 
Most 
agricultural 
land 
is 
under 
arid 
or 
semi-arid 
conditions 
with 
average 
annual 
rainfall 
of 
200-300 
mm. 
Evapotranspiration 
is 
high 
and 
generally 
exceeds 
effective 
rainfall 
by 
a 
factor 
of 
five 
or 
more. 
Consequently, 
irrigation 
is 
essential 
for 
reliable 
crop 
production. 
Thus 
constraints 
affecting 
the 
irrigation 
sub-sector have 
a large 
impact 
on 
the 
agricultural 
sector. 
The 
total 
area 
equipped 
for 
irrigation 
is 
1.45 
million 
ha, 
representing 
90 
percent 
of 
all 
arable 
land 
in 
the 
country. 
There 
is 
a 
high 
reliance 
on 
pumped 
irrigation to 
abstract 
water 
from main 
canals, 
with 
about 
440,000 
ha 
receiving 
water 
through 
at 
least one 
level 
of 
pumping. 
Irrigation 
water 
is 
predominantly 
distributed 
through 
open 
canal 
systems 
and surface-applied 
to 
the 
land.  Wheat 
and 
fodder 
crops 
are 
irrigated 
mainly 
by 
border 
strips. 
Cotton, 
maize and 
vegetables 
are 
grown 
as 
row 
crops and 
irrigated 
by 
furrows. 
Drainage 
is 
essential 
in 
many 
areas, 
but 
is 
most 
critical 
in 
the 
flat 
low-lying 
Kura-Araz 
and 
Mugan-Salyan plains, 
totaling 
about 
760,000 
ha. 
The 
area 
actually 
irrigated 
has 
fluctuated 
considerably 
in 
the 
past 
decade, 
and 
was 
reportedly 
1.1 
million 
ha 
in 
2001. 
RegionlRaion

Note:

irrigation

in 
occupied regions. 
Status 
of Irrigation and 
Drainage Infrastructure 
Maintenance 
of 
the 
irrigation 
systems 
was 
largely 
abandoned 
for 
most 
of 
the 
1990s, 
due 
to 
falling 
budgets 
from 
SAIC, impoverishment 
of 
the population, 
and 
disorganization 
in 
management, 
especially at 
former 
farm 
level. 
With 
this 
collapse 
in 
maintenance, 
systems 
have 
deteriorated 
substantially. 
The 
farm 
gate 
of 
the 
former 
farms defines 
the 
demarcation 
point 
between 
off-farm 
infrastructure, 
the 
responsibility 
of 
SAIC, 
and 
on-farm 
infrastructure. 
Formerly 
there was one 
or 
more 
hydroposts 
to 
control 
and 
measure 
the 
flow 
of 
irrigation 
water 
into 
the 
former 
farm, 
but 
most 
are 
no 
longer 
functioning. 
Water 
is 
generally distributed 
to 
farmers 
through 
a 
gravity network 
of 
tertiary and 
field 
channels 
(pre-cast 
concrete 
canalettes, 
or 
more commonly 
earthen). 
There 
are 
few 
permanent 
flow 
control 
structures on-farm, 
and where 
they 
exist, 
are 
either 
not 
operating 
or 
in 
poor 
condition. 
The 
following 
table 
provides 
an 
indication 
on 
the 
condition 
of 
a 
sample 
of 
systems. 
It 
shows 
that large 
sections 
of 
canals 
are 
in 
need 
of 
rehabilitation 
or 
reconstruction. 
The 
main 
features 
of 
the 
current 
status 
of 
the 
systems 
are: 
-51 
- 
Page  56
most 
canals 
are 
unlined, 
and many, 
especially 
at 
farm-level,  have become 
seriously 
blocked 
with 
silt, 
impeding 
or 
preventing 
water 
flow. 
On-farm 
canals 
are 
in 
especially 
poor 
condition, 
with 
most 
requiring 
desilting 
and 
installation 
of 
control 
structures; 
diversion 
headworks 
are 
often 
corroded 
and 
in 
need 
of 
reinforcement 
or 
replacement, even 
for 
major 
structures, 
such 
as 
the 
Bahramtape 
intake 
structure 
on 
the 
Araz 
River; 
many 
energy 
dissipation 
structures 
and 
outfall 
areashasins 
are 
damaged; 
many 
steel 
gates 
and 
valves 
are 
inoperable; 
flow 
measuring 
structures 
are 
almost 
non-existent, 
even 
on main 
canals 
and 
headworks; 
keeping electrically-driven 
pumps 
functioning 
is 
problematic, 
as 
most 
are obsolete, 
having 
been 
operating 
for 
25 
years 
without 
replacement; 
many electrically-driven 
tubewells 
for 
irrigation 
are 
no 
longer 
functioning; 
and 
drainage 
systems 
were seldom 
well 
maintained, 
even 
during 
the 
Soviet period, 
and after 
ten 
years 
of 
neglect 
most collectors 
are 
choked 
with 
weeds 
and 
silt. 
At 
least 
60-70 
percent 
of 
the 
area 
served 
by 
drains 
requires 
rehabilitation. 
--------

643 
397 
7 
State 
Amelioration 
and 
Irrigation 
Committee 
Established 
in 
1993, 
SAIC 
replaced the 
former 
Ministry 
of 
Irrigation 
and 
assumed 
responsibility 
for 
managing 
the 
countrys 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
systems. 
This 
includes establishing 
national 
policy 
and 
legislation, 
planning 
ameliorative and 
irrigation 
measures, 
establishing 
technical 
standards 
and 
regulation, 
data 
collection 
and 
monitoring 
of 
irrigated 
lands. 
With 
cropped 
agriculture 
almost 
entirely 
dependent 
on 
irrigation, 
SAIC 
is 
of 
critical 
importance 
to 
the 
agriculture 
sector. 
SAIC 
has 
primary 
responsibility 
for 
executing and 
enforcing 
the 
Law 
on 
Amelioration 
and 
Irrigation 
(1996), 
the 
Water 
Code 
(1997), 
and 
other 
related legislation 
in 
the water 
sector. 
With 
its 
headquarters 
in 
Baku, 
SAIC 
has 
reportedly 
some 
22,000 
employees. 
About 
19,000 
staff 
are 
based 
in 
the 
districts, 
including 
some 
12,000 
laborers. 
The 
district staff 
has 
as 
main 
responsibility 
the 
O&M 
of 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
systems, 
but 
is 
engaged 
in 
other 
activities 
such 
as 
flood 
management. 
The raion 
offices 
are 
responsible 
for 
delivering 
irrigation 
water 
to 
farmers, 
maintaining 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
systems, 
operating 
pumping 
stations, 
and 
collecting irrigation 
service 
fees 
from 
WAS 
or 
individual 
farmers. 
SAIC 
has seven 
main 
directorates, 
including 
one 
for 
finance, another 
for 
administration, and a 
separate 
SAIC 
directorate 
for 
Nakhchivan. 
The 
five 
technical 
directorates 
include 
eight 
departments 
which 
cover 
design, 
construction, operation 
and 
maintenance, 
water 
resources 
and 
fee 
collection 
for 
all 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
systems 
in 
the 
country. 
The 
department 
which 
is 
responsible 
for 
- 
52 
- 
Page  57
O&M 
of 
major 
structures 
has 
special sections 
for 
some 
of 
the 
main 
canals, 
such 
as 
the 
Upper 
Karabakh 
and 
Upper 
Shirvan 
canals, 
which 
can traverse 
up 
to 
15 
raions. 
This 
department also 
oversees 
the 
60 
SAIC 
raion-level 
offices 
(rayvodkhoz 
- 
RVK). 
RVKs 
are 
responsible 
for 
O&M 
and capital 
repairs 
of 
the 
off-farm 
irrigation 
network, 
while main 
drainage 
collectors 
fall 
under 
the 
regional amelioration 
directorates. 
In 
view 
of 
the 
serious 
deterioration 
of 
on-farm infrastructure, 
in 
2000, 
SAIC 
made 
an 
assessment 
of 
on-farm 
infrastructure, 
and 
transferred 
responsibility 
for 
this 
to 
RVKs 
and 
amelioration 
directorates. 
However, 
without 
a 
substantial increase 
in 
resources 
to 
match 
the 
additional 
burden, 
the 
current 
on-farm situation 
remains unchanged. 
Work 
on 
creating a 
large 
number 
of 
WUAs 
was started 
in 
2000 
and 
given 
the 
limited 
amount 
of 
staff 
and 
financial 
resources 
invested 
the 
results have been 
impressive 
as 
at 
present 
a 
total 
of 
552 
WAS 
have been 
legally 
established 
in 
44 
raions, 
with 
387,000 
members 
and a 
total 
command 
area 
of 
644,000 
ha. 
The 
members' general 
level 
of 
knowledge 
about 
WUAs 
is 
limited 
and, 
in 
most communities, the 
WUAs 
are seen 
to 
be 
led 
by 
people 
who 
are 
not 
accountable 
to 
WUA 
members. 
Even 
though 
WUAs 
have already 
shown 
themselves 
to 
be 
quite 
effective 
in 
collecting 
water 
fees, 
they 
have 
been 
less 
effective 
in 
informing 
general 
membership 
about 
the 
purpose 
or 
use 
of 
the 
fees, 
which would 
become 
increasingly 
important 
as 
fees are 
raised 
over 
time. 
A 
WUA 
is 
thus 
mainly 
seen 
as 
an 
organization 
to 
distribute 
water 
and collect 
the 
ISF 
for 
SAIC, rather than a 
genuine 
farmer-led 
organization 
with 
the 
mandate 
of 
managing 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
infrastructure. 
Proposed 
Project 
Scope 
and 
Location 
The 
proposed 
project 
would 
have 
two 
primary 
activities: 
(i) 
assist 
Government 
in 
building 
capacity to 
provide training 
and 
support 
to 
WUAs, 
and 
provide 
this 
training 
and 
support 
to 
WUAs; 
and 
(ii) 
assist 
in 
the 
rehabilitation 
and 
improvement 
of 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
infrastructure. 
To 
achieve 
this, 
the 
project 
would 
have 
two 
major 
components, 
while 
a 
smaller 
third 
component 
would 
provide project 
management support. 
The 
project 
would 
provide 
some 
support 
to 
all 
raions, 
but 
it 
would 
provide more 
comprehensive 
institutional 
support 
to 
the 
11 
raions 
where 
rehabilitation 
activities 
would 
take place. 
It 
is 
expected 
that 
the 
following 
11 
raions 
would 
be 
covered: 
Aghjabedi, 
Babek, Beylagan, Geranboy, 
Guba, 
Imishli, 
Khachmaz, Saatli, 
Sabirabad, Sharur, 
and 
Zardab.  These 
raions 
have large 
irrigation 
areas, 
and 
they 
can 
be 
linked 
to other 
donor-assisted 
interventions 
in 
the 
sector. 
Several 
of 
the 
raions 
are 
benefiting 
from 
the 
ongoing 
RIDP, 
while 
FPP and 
ADCP 
are 
active 
in 
some 
of 
the 
raions 
as 
well.  The 
following 
table 
shows 
the 
current 
numbers 
of 
registered 
WUAs 
in 
the 
selected raions, 
and 
their 
respective 
irrigable 
areas. 
-

Development 
of 
Water 
Users 
Associations 
SAIC 
has 
recognized 
it 
is 
in 
their 
best 
interest 
to 
have 
WAS, 
both 
to 
improve 
on-farm 
irrigation 
management 
as 
well 
as 
to 
ensure 
collection 
of 
fees, 
which 
are 
expected 
to 
gradually 
become 
a larger 
percentage 
of 
SAICs 
budget 
for 
O&M. 
With 
very 
limited 
financial 
and 
staff 
support, 
SAICs 
Department 
of 
Water 
Management 
and Water 
Fee 
Collection 
has 
become 
directly 
involved 
in 
the 
formation 
and 
creation 
of 
WUAs. 
Since 
the 
basic concept 
of 
the 
WUAs, 
including 
the 
standard 
documents 
prepared 
under 
FPP, 
have been 
approved 
by 
the 
Agrarian 
Commission 
of 
the 
Cabinet 
of 
Ministers, 
SAIC 
has 
adopted 
these 
for 
the 
creation and 
registration 
of 
other 
WUAs 
in 
the 
country. 
As 
would 
be 
expected 
without 
any 
special 
training, 
it 
is 
not 
surprising 
that 
WUAs 
are 
not 
very 
effective. 
SAIC staff 
members have 
placed 
most 
of 
their 
efforts 
in 
helping 
WUAs 
complete the 
necessary 
forms 
for 
registration 
as 
well 
as 
signing 
contracts 
with 
SAIC 
for 
water 
delivery. 
Raion 
level 
SAIC 
staff 
also 
hold 
periodic 
meetings 
with 
chairmen and 
help 
resolve 
conflicts 
when 
necessary. 
- 
. 
, 
,...... 
. 
.... 
. 
.

3 
1 
1 
,.,,_A 
. 
~n 
order 
to 
estabiisn 
tne 
institutional capability 
to 
develop ana 
register 
in 
excess 
or 
wu-i 
,uuu 
WUAs, 
as 
well 
as 
to 
ensure that 
the 
Government has the 
long-term technical capacity 
to 
support 
these 
WUAs, 
the 
Chairman 
of 
SAIC 
has ordered 
in 
January 
2003 
the 
creation 
of 
WUA 
Support 
Units 
in 
SAIC 
in 
Baku 
as 
well 
as 
in 
each 
of 
the 
60 
SAIC 
Raion 
offices. 
This 
project 
would 
help 
fund 
and develop 
these 
offices 
with 
equipment, 
training, and operational 
funds. 
- 
54 
- 
Page  59
Experience 
with 
irrigation 
management 
transfer 
in 
former 
Soviet 
Union 
countries 
has 
demonstrated 
that 
the 
point 
of 
interface between the 
responsibility 
of 
the 
state 
irrigation 
agency 
and 
the 
former 
state 
and collective 
farms 
is 
an 
appropriate 
hydraulic 
level 
for 
transferring 
management 
responsibility, 
as 
the 
physical 
system 
as 
well 
as 
the 
management system 
are 
focused 
at 
that 
point. 
These 
former 
state 
and 
collective 
farm 
service 
areas 
were 
normally 
800 
to 
2,000 
ha 
in 
size, 
with 
an 
average 
of 
approximately 
1,250 
ha 
per 
farm. 
In 
other 
countries 
this 
has 
proven 
to 
be 
a 
manageable 
size 
for 
a 
WUA. 
It 
is 
large enough 
to 
have economies 
of 
scale 
with 
respect 
to fixed 
costs 
while 
small 
enough 
to 
be 
managed 
by 
the 
WUA 
management 
staff 
and 
governed 
by 
a 
Council 
representing 
the 
users 
in 
the 
area. 
WUAs 
smaller 
than 
this, 
normally 
based 
on 
the 
area 
of 
a 
single 
village, 
are 
often 
too 
small 
to 
be 
able 
to 
cover 
the 
fixed 
costs 
of 
maintaining an 
association 
and 
consequently have 
had problems 
with 
their 
financial 
situation. 
The 
Development 
of 
Water 
Users 
Associations Component 
would 
include 
several 
activities, 
designed 
to 
develop 
sustainable 
operating 
WUAs 
in 
the 
country. 
It 
would 
finance 
technical 
assistance, 
goods, 
training 
and 
study 
tours. 
The 
technical 
assistance 
would 
be 
used 
to 
develop 
training 
and support 
plans 
and 
provide 
initial 
training 
courses. 
Goods, such 
as 
office 
and 
training 
equipment, 
would 
be 
provided 
to 
strengthen 
the 
units 
involved 
in 
the 
training 
and support 
of 
WUAs. 
Participating 
WUAs 
would 
also 
be 
able 
to 
source goods, 
including 
office 
equipment, 
motor 
bikes, 
small 
maintenance 
machinery 
and 
field 
survey 
and 
monitoring 
equipment, 
to 
facilitate their 
operations. 
Training 
programs 
and 
study 
tours 
would 
be 
financed. 
Activity 
1: 
Development 
of 
Capacity 
to 
Train 
and 
Strengthen 
WAS 
Proper development 
of 
WUAs, 
with 
adequate 
member 
participation, 
would 
require 
a 
serious 
program 
of 
assistance, 
for which 
there 
is 
little 
experience 
in 
the country. 
The 
project 
would 
assist 
with 
the development 
of 
the 
newly 
established 
SUs, 
both 
within 
SAIC 
in 
Baku 
and Nakhchivan, and 
the 
SAIC 
Raion 
offices. 
The 
staff 
of 
these 
Units 
are 
civil 
servants 
and 
their 
salaries 
would 
be 
paid 
by 
Government. 
The project 
would 
distinguish 
between Raion-SUs 
in 
11 
raions where 
rehabilitation 
activities 
would 
take 
place, and other raions. 
The 
Central-SU 
in 
Baku, 
the 
Nakhchivan 
SU 
and 
the 
12 
(there 
would 
be 
two 
SUs 
in 
Sabirabad) 
in 
the 
raions where 
the 
rehabilitation 
of 
infrastructure 
is 
to 
be 
implemented 
would 
be 
supported 
with 
office 
equipment, vehicles, 
and 
part 
of 
the 
incremental 
operational 
funds. 
Both 
the 
Baku 
and 
Nakhchivan 
offices 
would 
be 
renovated, 
especially 
to 
establish 
training 
rooms 
with 
the necessary 
training 
equipment. 
The staff 
of 
the 
SUs 
would 
receive the 
training 
necessary 
to 
implement 
their 
WUA 
support 
and strengthening 
tasks. 
For 
this, 
national 
and 
international 
consultants 
would 
be recruited 
to 
develop 
the 
capacity 
within 
the SUs 
to 
establish, train, support 
and 
strengthen 
WUAs. 
The 
48 
SUs 
outside 
the 
immediate 
project 
area 
would 
receive 
some 
training 
support and a 
small allocation 
for 
office 
furniture 
and 
equipment. 
The 
Central-SU 
would 
have 
a 
staff 
of 
up 
to 
7 
members. 
In 
view 
of 
the 
country-wide 
training 
that 
would 
be 
provided, 
however, 
more 
staff may 
have 
to 
be 
added 
as 
the 
project 
progresses 
and 
the demands 
on 
the 
staff 
increase. Permanent 
slulls 
to 
be 
developed 
in 
the 
Central-SU 
would 
include: 
WUA 
Strengthening and Development, 
Training 
and Promotion, 
FinancialDSF 
Management, 
and 
Database 
Management. 
It 
could 
draw 
on 
local 
legal 
specialists 
for 
assistance 
with 
legal 
issues 
related 
to 
WUA 
development. 
The 
main 
tasks 
of 
the 
Central-SU 
would 
be 
to 
organize 
and 
implement 
the 
national 
WUA 
promotion 
program 
as 
well 
as 
to 
coordinate 
the 
WUA 
restructuring program 
and 
all 
training 
programs, 
both for 
the 
project 
WUAs 
and 
the 
larger 
set 
of 
WUAs 
in 
the 
country.  The project 
would 
provide 
support 
for 
the 
development 
and execution 
of 
the 
promotion 
and 
training 
programs.  Consultants 
would 
be 
hired 
to 
prepare 
promotion 
and 
training 
material, and 
funds 
would 
be 
available 
for 
the 
training 
of 
SU 
- 
55 
- 
Page  60
staff 
and 
WUAs. 
The Central-SU 
would 
also 
be 
responsible 
for 
maintaining 
the 
database 
of 
all 
WUAs 
in 
the 
country, 
for 
which 
questionnaires 
and 
report 
formats 
have 
to 
be 
developed. 
As 
information 
would 
be 
fed 
into 
Baku 
from 
Raion-SUs 
this 
would 
be an 
excellent 
source 
to 
track 
changes 
in 
WUAs 
over 
time. 
It 
would 
also 
allow 
the 
project 
to 
monitor 
impacts 
as 
WUAs 
in 
the 
project 
Raion-SUs 
are 
expected 
to 
demonstrate 
the value 
of 
more intensive 
WUA 
support, 
while 
WAS 
in 
the 
non-project 
Raions 
would 
have 
less 
intensive 
support and 
therefore 
are 
expected 
to 
progress 
at 
a slower 
rate. 
SAIC 
intends 
to 
monitor 
the 
technical 
and financial 
performance 
of 
WUAs. 
As 
long 
as 
the 
on-farm infrastructure 
belongs 
to 
the 
State, 
SAIC 
in 
conjunction 
with 
the 
WUAs 
would 
need 
to 
develop a 
program 
for 
monitoring 
the 
condition 
of 
the 
infrastructure. 
This 
would 
operate 
in 
tandem 
with 
the 
system 
of 
monitoring 
the 
level 
of 
funds 
collected 
by 
the 
WUA 
for 
O&M 
of 
the 
system 
under 
WUA 
management. 
The 
oversight

Activity 
2: 
WUA 
Strengthening 
The 
project 
would 
assist 
Azerbaijan 
with 
its 
first 
comprehensive attempt 
to 
institute 
a 
large, 
nation-wide

and

program 
for 
WAS. 
The 
program 
would 
have 
two 
components, one 
directed 
at 
sensitizing the 
users 
and 
the 
other 
directed 
at 
formal 
and 
informal 
training. 
The 
project 
would 
provide 
on-demand support 
to 
farmers 
that 
are 
interested 
in 
participating 
in 
WUAs. 
This 
would 
be 
done 
through 
promotional 
material 
and 
workshops. 
Information 
dissemination 
would 
be 
a 
priority 
of 
early 
stages 
of 
the 
project, 
following 
traditional information 
transmission 
mechanisms, 
as 
well 
as 
developing 
new 
ones 
that 
ensure 
that 
all 
stakeholders 
are 
reached 
and 
able 
to 
make 
informed 
decisions 
regarding 
restructuring 
and 
development 
of 
the 
WUA. 
The 
information 
campaigns 
would 
be 
accompanied 
by 
systematic

programs 
for 
existing 
WUA 
leadership, 
as 
well 
as 
aspiring, 
new

but 
would 
be 
able 
- 
57 
- 
Page  62
independently 
to 
assess

of 
leaders 
to

When

is 
in 
place, one

of 
Raion-SUs 
would 
be 
to help

to

Law 
on 
Amelioration 
and 
Irrigation, 
as 
public, non-profit 
associations 
rather than private, 
for-profit 
organizations 
as

are 
now. 
The 
current 
structure 
of 
WUAs 
has 
little 
chance

transforming 
into 
strong 
user 
organizations. 
The

proposed 
as 
part 
of 
project 
design 
proposes a 
new 
structure 
in 
which 
governance 
and 
management 
are separated. 
The 
governance 
part 
is 
a 
set 
of 
governing 
bodies 
filled 
by 
members 
on 
a 
voluntary 
basis. 
These 
individuals 
are 
farmers 
and 
must 
be 
farming 
in 
the 
system 
to 
qualify 
for 
membership 
and the 
right 
to 
hold 
a 
position. 
The various 
governing 
bodies 
of 
the 
association 
are 
expected 
to 
include: 
e 
General 
Assembly (or 
Representative 
Assembly) 
as 
its 
leading 
body 
which 
determines the 
main 
policies. 
The 
assembly 
is 
the supreme 
body 
of 
any 
WUA, 
and 
is 
composed 
of 
all 
members 
of 
the 
Association, 
or 
in 
the 
case 
of 
an Association 
with 
a representative system 
comprised 
of 
all 
representatives; 
Administrative Council 
that 
is 
responsible 
for 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
policies 
and 
decisions 
of 
the 
General 
Assembly. 
It 
is 
accountable 
to 
the 
General 
Assembly 
for 
the 
way 
in 
which 
it 
manages 
the 
activity 
of 
the Association, and 
would 
be 
comprised 
of 
five 
to nine 
members, 
one 
of 
whom 
will 
be 
the 
Chairperson; 
Audit 
Committee 
that

to 
inspect 
if

the

and 
Arbitration

for

e 
e 
e 
In

carrying 
out

of 
the

Day 
to 
day 
O&M 
activities 
of

WUA 
would 
be 
the 
responsibility 
of 
the 
hired 
manager, accountant 
and technical staff. 
Although

would

The

staff may 
not 
at 
the

serve

Successful

would 
require significant

The 
new 
structure 
must 
allow

to

by 
the

Council 
that

the members. 
At

WUA 
would 
also 
receive 
the 
irrigation 
infrastructure 
under 
long-term 
use. 
The 
development 
of 
WUAs 
is 
a 
continuing 
process 
that 
starts 
with 
promotion 
and 
education and 
continues 
after

The

would 
have 
to

and

These 
would

Unit 
in 
Baku. 
Once

are 
developed 
a

plan 
needs 
to 
be organized 
to 
sell 
water 
users 
on 
WUAs, 
and persuade 
them 
that 
a 
WUA 
is 
necessary 
if 
they 
are 
going to 
have 
reliable 
irrigation 
O&M 
at 
the

level, 
which 
of 
course 
requires

to 
pay 
the 
real

of

O&M

Generally

willing 
to 
pay more 
for 
irrigation 
O&M

but

the idea 
that 
O&M

provided 
by 
their 
own 
WUA 
may 
be

but

are 
better and 
more 
sustainable.

- 
Page  63
As 
soon

In

Units

the 
Council 
Members, 
how

They 
would

need 
to 
start 
training 
the 
water 
supply 
staff 
on 
techniques 
for 
proper 
maintenance 
and 
system 
operation. 
In 
addition 
the 
accountant 
and 
bookkeeper 
need 
to 
start 
learning 
how 
to 
keep an 
open 
set 
of 
books and 
develop procedures 
for 
establishing water 
charges 
and 
fee 
collection. 
During 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
project 
a large 
number 
of 
training 
courses 
would 
be 
conducted. 
These 
courses 
would 
both 
strengthen 
the 
SUs 
and 
participating 
WUAs. 
One 
of 
the 
first 
tasks 
of 
the 
Central-SU and 
its 
consultants 
would 
be 
to 
carry 
out 
a detailed 
training 
needs 
assessment, 
building 
on 
the 
initial 
assessment 
that 
was done 
during 
project 
preparation. 
At 
this 
stage 
it 
is 
difficult to 
estimate 
exactly 
how 
many 
training 
courses 
are 
required 
but 
a 
first 
estimate 
is 
that 
approximately 
140 
courses 
would 
be 
developed 
and 
conducted 
(the 
table 
on 
the 
following 
page 
provides 
an 
indication 
about the number and 
timing 
of 
courses). 
Training 
modules 
would 
be 
prepared 
jointly 
by 
the international 
experts and 
local 
trainers.

subjects 
include:

of

and book-keeping;

other projects,

would

During

to 
WUAs 
the

from 
the

would

with 
the

to 
ensure 
that 
course 
content 
of 
the 
training 
courses 
meets 
the 
needs 
of 
the 
project 
and the

Raion-SU staff 
would 
provide 
a

of 
the

training,

some

under

such

as

Other

includes

in

and

for

of

Project

have

for

The

of

to provide 
some 
training 
to 
Raion-SU

would

is

The

of

has 
established 
a 
training 
center 
in 
Baku, 
which would 
be 
available 
to 
provide training 
in 
legal 
subjects.

Study 
Tours 
to
