Hands-on Experience 
on 
WUA 
Operation. 
Besides 
the 
in-country 
training 
program, 
it 
would 
be 
important 
to 
expose the 
main 
project staff 
and 
some 
WUA 
representatives 
to 
experiences 
in 
WUA 
development and transfer 
of 
irrigation 
management 
in 
other 
countries, 
especially 
those 
with 
economic 
conditions 
similar to 
those 
found 
locally. 
This 
could 
best 
be 
done 
in 
a setting where 
a 
study 
group 
would 
spend several days 
in 
one 
WUA, 
to 
get 
acquainted 
with 
the 
day 
to 
day operation 
of 
a 
successful 
WUA. 
This 
would 
provide 
insights 
to the requirements 
for 
accepting 
full 
responsibility 
for 
O&M 
and 
financial 
management 
of 
large 
service 
areas. 
A 
study 
tours 
for 
senior 
project officials 
would 
be 
organized 
in 
Mexico 
and 
the 
US, where mature 
WUAs 
have developed. 
The project 
would 
work 
with 
a large number 
of 
WUAs 
in 
the 
11 
raions mentioned 
above. 
About 
45 
WUAs, 
with 
an average 
command 
area 
of 
1,250 ha, 
would 
be 
selected 
for 
rehabilitation. 
For 
these, 
a 
- 
60 
- 
Page  65
limited 
amount 
of 
equipment 
would 
be 
provided 
by 
the 
project. 
This 
is 
expected 
to include 
office 
furniture,  a computer, two-way 
radios, 
motorcycles, 
small 
canal maintenance 
machinery, 
etc. 
The 
benefiting 
WUAs 
would 
collect 
funds 
from 
their 
members 
for 
the replacement 
of 
the 
equipment. 
The 
usefulness 
of 
each 
request 
for 
equipment 
from 
WUAs 
would 
be 
carefully 
analyzed 
by 
the 
SUs 
and 
the 
Project Implementation 
Unit 
(PTU). 
The 
benefiting 
WUAs 
would 
collect 
funds 
from 
their 
members 
for 
the replacement 
of 
the equipment.  Raion-SUs 
would 
review 
each 
replacement 
fund 
proposal 
and 
monitor 
the 
collection 
of 
ISF 
for 
this 
purpose. 
Project 
Component 
2 
- 
US$30.00 
million 
Irrigation 
and 
Drainage Infrastructure Rehabilitation 
In 
order 
to 
improve 
the 
performance 
of 
WUAs, 
they 
need 
to 
have 
infrastructure 
that 
allows 
efficient 
and 
reliable 
water 
distribution, 
so 
that farmers 
can 
receive water 
in 
the 
right 
amount, 
at 
the 
right 
time, 
and 
at the 
right 
place. 
To 
this 
end, 
the 
project 
would 
provide 
funds 
for 
selective 
rehabilitation 
of 
irrigation 
and drainage 
systems. 
The project 
would 
target 
some 
280 
WUAs 
in 
the 
11 
selected raions, 
of 
which 
about 
45 
of 
those that reach 
sufficient 
maturity 
would 
benefit 
from 
rehabilitation 
investments 
(hereafter 
referred 
to 
as 
rehabilitation 
WAS 
or 
R-WUAs). 
Rehabilitation 
would 
not 
only 
be 
at 
on-farm 
level, 
as 
it 
also has 
to 
be 
ensured 
that water 
supply 
to 
the 
WUA 
boundaries 
is 
reliable. 
Funds 
would 
be 
provided 
for: 
(i) 
rehabilitation 
of 
on-farm infrastructure 
on 
about 
56,000 
ha 
(US 
$19.0 
million); 
(ii) 
rehabilitation 
of 
the 
Bahramtape headworks 
on 
the 
Araz 
River, 
which 
has 
stability 
issues, 
requiring 
urgent 
attention, 
and 
which 
serves 
some 
140,000 
ha, 
including 
large 
areas 
in 
three 
of 
the 
projects 
target 
raions 
(Sabirabad, 
Imishli 
and 
Saatli) 
(US 
$4.2 
million); 
and 
(iii) 
rehabilitation 
of 
off-farm 
works 
associated 
with 
the on-farm works 
to 
ensure 
sufficient 
and 
timely 
water 
delivery 
to 
project 
WAS 
(US 
$1.4 
million 
for 
hydroposts, and 
US 
$3.2 
million 
for 
other 
off-farm 
works). 
Off-farm 
rehabilitation 
investments, 
including 
the 
major 
headworks and 
hydroposts, 
would 
be restricted 
to 
around 
35 
percent 
of 
available 
rehabilitation 
funds 
to 
ensure 
that 
the 
bulk 
of 
investments 
are at 
the 
farm 
level, where 
the 
most 
serious 
deterioration 
has 
taken place 
during 
the 
past 
years. 
To 
ensure 
the 
project works 
with 
sustainable 
WUAs, 
the 
project 
must 
establish selection 
criteria 
for 
determining 
which 
WUAs 
to 
include 
in 
irrigation 
rehabilitation 
work. 
It 
is 
important 
that 
WAS 
demonstrate 
they 
are 
interested 
in 
maintaining 
the 
on-farm 
irrigation 
infrastructure. 
This 
requires they 
are 
willing 
to 
pay 
a 
reasonable service 
fee. 
This 
fee 
must 
include 
enough 
funds 
to 
support the 
WUAs 
on-farm 
O&M 
activities 
as 
well 
as 
make the 
required 
payments 
to SAIC. 
To 
identify 
WUAs 
that 
are 
considered 
good 
candidates 
for irrigation 
systems 
improvement 
a 
step-by-step process 
toward 
sustainability 
has 
been 
established. 
The 
project 
would 
adopt a 
generic 
and 
competitive 
approach, 
whereby 
all 
target 
WUAs 
would 
receive 
institutional 
support, 
but 
only 
those 
which 
meet 
prescribed 
criteria 
would 
be 
eligible 
for 
subsidized 
rehabilitation 
of 
their 
systems. 
As 
funds 
are 
limited, 
the 
project 
would 
adopt 
a 
first 
come, 
first 
served 
procedure, 
which would 
accelerate 
WUA 
response. 
The 
eligibility 
criteria 
are: 
(a) 
Status 
of 
development 
of 
the 
WUA: 
0 
0 
0 
0 
0 
0 
has 
been 
legally 
registered 
according 
to 
the 
amended 
Law 
on 
Amelioration 
and 
Irrigation, 
and has 
adopted a 
new 
charter; 
has 
long-term 
(20 
years) 
infrastructure 
use 
right 
and 
long-term 
(10 years) 
water 
right; 
has established 
the 
required 
governing 
bodies (General 
or 
Representative Assembly, 
Administrative 
Council, 
Audit 
Committee and 
Arbitration 
Committee); 
has 
recruited 
a 
manager 
and accountant 
as 
well 
as 
other staff; 
has 
established 
a 
set 
of 
policies 
under 
which 
the 
WUA 
is 
to 
operate; 
Signed 
water 
delivery 
contract 
with 
SAIC, 
based 
on 
water 
requirements; 
and 
-61 
- 
Page  66
a 
has 
established 
a 
realistic 
O&M 
plan, 
irrigation 
service 
fee, 
and a 
step-by-step 
procedure 
for 
payment 
of 
this 
fee 
by 
the 
members. 
(b) 
management 
and 
accounting; 
WUA 
Council 
and management 
staff 
have 
followed 
basic 
training 
courses, 
including 
(c) 
80 
percent 
of 
intemal 
water 
fees 
and 
at 
least 
80 
percent 
of 
ISF 
contract 
with 
SAIC 
during 
the 
past 
two 
years; 
Past 
collection 
rates 
of 
ISF. 
The participating 
WAS 
are 
expected 
to 
have 
paid 
at 
least 
(d) 
ten 
percent 
of 
the investment 
costs. 
The 
plan 
should 
be 
economically viable 
with 
a rate 
of 
return 
of 
at 
least 
12 
percent. 
WUA 
members 
agreement 
on 
rehabilitation 
plan 
and 
costs, 
and 
agreement 
to 
contribute 
The 
project 
would 
follow 
the 
concept 
of 
farmer-driven 
development, 
a 
participatory 
process 
in 
which 
irrigators (through 
WAS) 
identify 
their 
needs 
and 
are 
involved 
in 
resource 
allocation, 
decision 
making, implementation, and 
monitoring.  The 
decisions 
concerning the 
priorities, 
works 
required and 
scale 
of 
investments 
would 
lie 
largely 
with 
farmers, 
and 
the 
project 
would 
be 
responsive 
to 
these 
requirements.  Procedures 
for 
identifying 
and 
implementing 
interventions 
would 
ensure 
a consultative 
and 
transparent process 
to 
determine 
priorities, 
costs, 
benefits 
and the users' 
responsibility 
for 
limited 
cost 
recovery. 
Increased 
farmer 
involvement 
from 
the 
outset 
would 
engender 
a 
sense 
of 
ownership, 
and 
a 
willingness 
to 
maintain 
and 
take 
care 
of 
the 
improvements. 
This 
process 
marks 
an 
important 
change 
from 
the 
former reliance 
on 
centralized 
financing 
and control. 
During 
the diagnostic 
surveys, farmers 
expressed 
a 
willingness 
to 
contribute 
financially 
towards 
rehabilitation 
costs, 
provided 
this 
is 
available 
under 
easy 
repayment terms, 
and 
after 
the 
works 
have 
been 
completed. 
It 
was agreed 
that the 
project 
plan 
for 
a 
small 
percentage repayment 
of 
ten 
percent, 
with 
low-fixed 
interest 
rate, 
based 
on 
inflation, 
but 
not 
to 
exceed 
10 
percent.  Repayment 
would 
be 
over a 
four 
year 
period, starting 
in 
the 
fifth 
year 
after 
the 
completion 
of 
rehabilitation 
works. 
The 
funds 
would 
be collected 
as 
ISF 
and 
used 
by 
SAIC 
for 
increased 
O&M. 
Based 
on 
various 
studies, 
it 
was 
determined that 
the 
average 
investment 
for 
on-farm 
rehabilitation 
can 
be 
estimated 
at 
US 
$300 
per 
ha. 
This 
would 
allow 
basic 
rehabilitation 
interventions 
to 
retum 
a 
system 
to 
operational 
conditions, 
which typically would 
include 
canal and 
drain 
cleaning, 
repair 
of 
canal 
lining, 
rehabilitation 
and 
construction 
of 
hydraulic 
structures 
(including 
cross regulators, 
division 
boxes, 
drop 
structures, 
gates, 
and 
flow 
measurement 
devices), 
and 
rehabilitation 
of 
boreholes. 
The 
costs 
of 
rehabilitation works 
would 
vary 
widely 
across 
the 
project 
area, 
depending 
on 
the 
scale 
and 
nature 
of 
works required 
at 
each 
site. 
It 
was agreed 
with 
SAIC 
that 
the available 
funds 
would 
be 
used 
as 
efficiently 
as 
possible, 
so 
that the 
maximum 
number 
of 
WUAs 
can 
be 
served. 
Costs 
have been calculated 
on 
the 
basis 
of 
unit 
prices 
from 
recently 
awarded 
civil 
works 
contracts 
for 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
works. 
Some 
of 
these 
unit 
costs are 
slightly 
higher 
than 
those 
used 
by 
SAIC 
for 
planning 
purposes, 
which 
may 
not 
be 
fully 
based 
on 
the current market conditions. 
For 
example, 
SAIC's 
unit 
rate 
for 
earthworks 
is 
US 
$0.30, 
while 
the analysis 
carried 
out 
during 
project preparation 
determined 
that the average 
market 
cost 
is 
Us 
$0.50 
per 
m3.

The 
Bahramtape 
headworks 
distributes 
water 
from 
the 
Araz 
river 
and 
Upper 
Karabakh 
canal 
to 
four 
main 
canals 
serving 
some 
140,000 
ha 
of 
irrigated land 
in 
five 
raions. 
The 
headworks 
were 
completed 
in 
1950, 
with 
only 
routine 
maintenance 
being 
carried 
out 
over 
the 
next 
forty 
years 
until 
the 
end 
of 
Soviet 
support 
in 
199 
1. 
There have been 
no 
repairs 
in 
the 
decade 
since, 
and the 
headworks 
are 
now 
in 
poor 
condition, 
with 
an increasing 
risk 
of 
collapse 
in 
the 
event 
of 
a 
major 
flood. 
SAIC 
recognizes 
the 
need 
for 
urgent 
capital 
repairs, 
but 
it 
has been 
unable 
to 
take action, 
due 
to 
budgetary 
constraints. 
The 
headworks 
has 
six 
main 
gates, 
and 
the 
flow 
through 
two 
gates at 
the 
right 
end 
has 
caused 
scouring 
in 
the 
immediate 
outfall 
area. 
This 
is 
due 
to 
poor 
hydraulic 
design, 
and 
there 
is 
clear 
evidence 
(cracks 
in 
the 
concrete) 
that the foundations 
of 
the 
supply 
channel 
to 
the 
sedimentation basin 
are 
being 
undermined. 
A 
high 
wall 
(1 
5 
m), 
bordering 
the 
river, 
forms part 
of 
this 
channel 
and 
appears 
close 
to 
- 
64 
- 
Page  69
collapse. 
It 
is 
also 
probable 
that 
the 
foundations 
of 
the 
main 
diversion 
structure 
might 
have become 
weakened 
by 
voids, 
but 
this 
would 
require 
detailed investigation. 
The 
two 
end 
gates 
are 
kept 
either 
partially 
or 
fully 
closed 
to 
reduce 
flows 
and 
minimize 
the 
risk 
of 
further 
scouring. 
However, 
in 
the event 
of 
a large 
flood, 
it 
would 
be 
necessary 
to 
open 
all 
gates, 
which 
could 
jeopardize the 
whole 
structure. 
All 
of 
the 
gates 
in 
the 
sedimentation 
basin 
on 
the 
right 
bank 
are 
in 
an advanced 
state 
of 
decay.  Some 
efforts 
have 
been 
made 
to 
reinforce 
the foundation, 
but 
without 
noticeable effect. 
The project 
would 
provide 
the 
funds 
necessary 
to 
guarantee 
the 
safety 
and 
operation 
of 
the 
structure 
for 
the 
next 
decades. 
In 
order 
for 
SAIC 
to 
deliver 
adequate 
and 
timely 
volumes 
of 
water 
to 
the 
R-WAS, 
the 
higher 
order 
irrigation 
system 
would 
have 
to 
be 
in 
fully 
operational condition. 
It 
would 
thus 
have 
to 
be 
ensured 
that 
main 
canal 
and 
drainage 
systems 
have 
sufficient 
capacity and 
ability 
to 
supply 
water 
to 
or 
drain 
water away 
from 
WUA 
areas. 
The 
project 
would 
finance 
selective 
rehabilitation 
of 
higher 
order 
systems, 
addressing 
specific 
bottlenecks 
such 
as 
limited 
repairs 
to 
abstraction 
structures 
(river 
diversions 
or 
pump 
stations), 
canal 
cleaning 
and 
reconstruction 
of 
hydraulic 
control 
structures. 
The 
off-farm 
works 
would 
also 
include 
the reinstatement 
of 
about 
1,000 
water 
measuring 
structures (hydroposts). 
This 
would 
be 
part 
of 
the 
process 
of 
the 
reintroduction 
of 
volumetric 
water 
measurement, 
a 
conditionality 
of 
the 
financing 
of 
rehabilitation 
works 
for 
the 
project 
WAS. 
The project 
would 
provide 
for 
necessary 
design 
and supervision 
services 
to 
ensure 
that 
all 
works 
are 
properly 
designed 
and carried 
out 
with 
sufficient construction 
supervision 
and 
quality 
control. 
Design 
and supervision 
of 
the 
rehabilitation 
works 
would 
be 
delegated 
to 
engineering 
consultants. 
It 
is 
envisaged 
that 
there 
would 
be 
a 
separate 
contract 
with 
an international consulting 
firm 
to 
design 
the 
rehabilitation works 
for 
Bahramtape 
weir. 
Another contract 
would 
be 
awarded 
for 
the 
design 
and 
supervision 
of 
the 
on- 
and 
off-farm 
works. 
It 
is 
expected 
that a 
lot 
of 
this 
work 
can 
be carried 
out 
with 
maximum local 
consulting 
firm 
participation.  The 
PIU 
would 
also 
have 
the 
option to 
hire 
some 
short-term 
international 
inputs 
to 
assist 
for 
example 
in 
the development 
of 
modem hydraulic 
structures.

Project Management 
With 
the 
similarities 
in

and

Irrigation

the

The

M&E 
and

The

The 
PIU 
would be

all

project. 
This 
would 
include 
ensuring that procurement 
meets 
all 
IDA 
procurement 
procedures. 
Financial 
reports 
for

project 
would 
be

by

PIU 
accountant 
using 
data 
provided 
by 
the 
offices 
in 
Baku 
and 
the Raions. 
The

accountant 
would 
be 
responsible 
for 
the 
financial 
management 
system 
including 
auditing 
and 
other 
internal 
controls. 
The 
engineer 
and 
supervisors 
would 
ensure 
that the technical 
aspects 
of 
the 
engineering 
work 
are 
carried out professionally. 
These supervisors 
would 
countersign, 
along 
with 
the 
Raion-SU 
engineer, 
the 
design and 
supervision 
consultant 
engineer, 
and 
WUA 
representative 
for 
on-farm 
works, 
to 
authorize 
payments 
for 
completed 
civil 
works. 
The 
PIU 
would 
also 
be 
responsible 
for 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation 
of 
projects activities. 
It 
would

and

which would allow 
the 
PIU 
to 
track project 
- 
65 
- 
Page  70
success 
by 
measuring benefits 
in

sites. 
It

to pinpoint 
locations

is 
having 
difficulties. 
The

Funds

The

of 
Benefits

Overview 
The 
economic 
benefits 
of 
the 
project 
would 
result 
from 
investments 
in: 
(i) 
rehabilitation 
of 
on-farm 
irrigation 
infrastructure 
for 
some 
56,000 
ha 
of 
service 
area 
of 
WUAs 
in 
the 
11 
project 
raions; 
(ii) 
selective 
rehabilitation 
of 
related 
off-farm 
infrastructure 
in 
the 
1 1 
project 
raions; 
and 
(iii) 
rehabilitation 
of 
the 
Bahramtape headworks. 
In 
addition, 
components 
to 
strengthen 
WUAs 
and 
to 
develop 
the 
capacity 
of 
SAIC 
to train 
and support 
WUAs 
would 
result 
in 
improved 
management 
of 
irrigation 
infrastructure 
and 
increased 
timeliness 
and 
efficiency 
of 
water 
supply, 
and 
would 
enhance 
the 
sustainability 
of 
the 
investments. 
Project 
costs 
result 
from 
the 
costs 
of 
infrastructure 
design, 
supervision, 
and construction 
or 
rehabilitation; 
from 
the 
provision 
of 
equipment 
and

renovation 
for 
WUA 
SUs 
and 
equipment 
for 
WUAs 
themselves; 
and 
from 
the 
hiring 
of 
specialists 
and

for 
training 
and 
overall 
project 
management. 
An 
economic analysis was 
carried 
out 
separately 
for 
the 
WUA 
rehabilitation 
sub-component, 
for 
the 
repair 
to 
the 
Bahramtape headworks, 
and 
for 
the 
project 
as 
a 
whole (including 
the costs 
of

in

and

of 
WUAs, 
as 
well 
as 
the

of 
hydroposts). 
The 
analysis 
is 
based 
on 
a

of

benefits 
attributable 
to 
the 
project 
by 
comparison 
of

with

(WIP)

and 
the 
without 
project 
(WOP) 
scenario. 
Sensitivity 
analysis 
was 
also 
conducted 
to 
gauge 
the effects 
of 
changes 
in 
the 
main 
assumptions. 
Overall 
Economic 
Internal 
Rate 
of 
Return 
(EIRR) 
An 
economic analysis was 
carried 
out 
on 
project 
investments that 
resulted 
in 
an 
EIRR 
for 
the 
overall 
project 
of 
2 
1 
percent. 
Separate analyses 
which 
compared the 
benefits 
and 
costs 
of 
investments 
in 
rehabilitation 
of 
WUA 
irrigation 
infrastructure 
and Bahramtape 
resulted 
in 
EIRRs 
of 
25 
percent 
and 
39 
percent, 
respectively. 
The 
sections 
below 
provide 
summaries 
of 
each 
of 
the 
three 
analyses, 
including 
information 
on 
the 
models, calculations, assumptions 
and 
results.

WA 
Rehabilitation 
The 
project 
would 
rehabilitate 
some 56,000 
ha 
of 
service 
area 
of 
WUAs 
in 
11 
raions 
as 
well 
as 
required 
complementary 
off-farm 
works. 
This 
assumes 
that 
the 
costs 
of 
on-farm 
rehabilitation 
would 
average 
US 
$300/ha. 
The 
incremental 
benefits 
of 
these 
investments 
as 
compared 
to 
the 
WOP 
scenario 
would 
derive 
from 
three 
sources: 
(i) 
an increase 
in 
yields 
due 
to 
improved irrigation 
water 
distribution 
and 
delivery to 
farmers; 
(ii) 
an increase 
in 
the 
area 
irrigated; 
and 
(iii) 
a 
change 
in 
cropping 
patterns 
towards 
more lucrative 
cash crops. 
Under 
the 
base 
case 
scenario 
described 
below, 
the 
NPV 
of 
incremental 
benefits, net 
of 
incremental 
costs 
of 
water delivery, 
is 
US 
$23.2 
million. 
Without 
the 
Project: 
With 
the 
supply 
of 
irrigation 
water 
becoming 
less 
reliable 
and 
patchier, 
an 
increasing 
proportion 
of 
the 
command 
area 
(1.5 
percent 
per 
year 
resulting 
in 
a compounded 
17 
percent 
by 
Year 
12) 
would 
cease 
to 
be cultivated and 
the 
proportion 
of 
cash 
crops, 
which 
require regular irrigation, 
would 
decrease (5 
percent 
per 
year) 
to 
be 
replaced 
by 
winter 
wheat and fodder. 
Yields 
and 
input 
use 
would 
rise 
under 
the 
influence 
of 
the 
Agricultural 
Development and 
Credit Project and 
other 
ongoing 
agricultural 
development 
initiatives, 
but 
rather 
slowly 
(between 
0.5 
percent and 
1 
percent 
per 
year). 
With 
the 
Project: 
Investment 
in 
rehabilitation, 
together 
with 
support 
to 
WUA 
management, 
would 
result 
in 
increased 
efficiency 
of 
water 
distribution 
such that farmers 
would 
receive 
on 
average 
4 
irrigations 
annually 
instead 
of 
around 2 
irrigations 
on 
average 
currently. 
In 
the context 
of 
improved 
water 
management 
by 
the 
WAS, 
and 
the 
re-introduction 
of 
volumetric 
charges 
for 
water, increases 
in 
water 
abstractions 
by 
the 
WUAs 
benefiting 
from 
rehabilitation 
would 
be 
limited 
to 
about 10 percent 
over 
current levels. 
All 
parts 
of 
the 
system 
would 
be 
brought back 
into 
operation, 
and 
10 percent 
of 
the 
command 
area 
would 
be 
returned 
from fallow to 
agricultural 
production 
as 
a 
result. 
Crops 
would 
benefit 
from 
improved 
water 
supply, 
resulting 
in 
increases 
in 
yields 
and 
input 
application 
over 
the 
WOP. 
Yields 
would 
reach 
levels 
between 
15 
percent 
and 
46 
percent 
higher 
than 
WOP 
depending 
on 
the 
crop.  There 
would 
also 
be 
a 
change 
in 
the 
cropping pattern 
away 
from 
winter 
wheat 
and fodder towards 
more 
lucrative 
cash 
crops, 
such 
as 
fruits, 
vegetables 
and cotton. 
The 
assumptions 
on 
changes 
in 
cropping 
patterns 
are 
conservative. 
They include 
a 
decrease 
in 
the 
share 
of 
wheat 
in 
the 
area 
cropped 
from 
69 
percent 
to 
65 
percent 
after 12 
years, 
a

of 
fodder 
from 
11

to 
10 
percent, 
and 
increases 
in 
the

of

and

from 
3

4 
percent, 
4

5 percent and 
8

10 percent, 
respectively.  Incremental 
costs 
of 
WUA

and 
of 
O&M 
of 
the 
system 
under 
WUA 
management 
would 
amount 
to 
an average 
of 
US 
SlYha. 
Bahramtape 
Headworks 
Rehabilitation 
Approximately 
US 
$4.2 
million 
have been 
estimated 
for 
rehabilitation 
of 
Bahramtape headworks, 
including modification 
of 
design 
as 
necessary. 
The 
benefits 
from 
the 
works 
and 
a 
consequent 
rationalization 
of 
its 
operation 
would 
include: 
(i) 
a 
reduction 
in 
the 
quantities 
of 
silt 
entering 
the 
irrigation 
network; and 
(ii) 
more importantly, 
the 
prevention 
of 
the 
collapse 
of 
the headworks and the 
consequent loss 
of 
all 
of 
the attendant 
irrigation 
system. 
Without 
the 
Project: 
The 
irrigation 
system 
would 
continue 
to 
silt 
up 
as 
at 
present, 
thereby 
reducing 
the 
flow 
of 
water 
to 
farmers, 
and 
causing 
high 
maintenance 
costs. 
More 
significantly, 
if 
the 
headworks 
failed 
(as 
they 
are 
expected 
to 
do 
in 
serious 
flood 
conditions) 
some 
140,000 
ha 
of 
command 
might 
fall 
out 
of 
operation. 
Allowing for 
the 
fact that 
up 
to 30 
percent 
of 
the 
current command 
area 
may 
not 
actually be irrigated 
at 
present, 
the 
net 
benefit 
to 
farmers 
from 
cropping 
this 
area 
is 
estimated 
at 
about 
US 
$20 
million 
annually. 
This 
estimate 
is 
based 
on 
the current 
average 
cropping 
pattern, 
agricultural 
practices, and 
fee 
structure, 
and 
is 
equivalent 
to 
an 
average 
of 
US 
$145/ha 
for 
the 
full 
command 
area. 
Deducting 
an 
economic cost 
of 
pumping 
of 
US 
$8O/ha 
on 
35 percent 
of 
the 
area 
and 
US 
$10/ha 
for 
- 
69 
- 
Page  74
current 
O&M 
costs, 
still 
results 
in 
an 
annual 
net 
economic 
benefit 
of 
about 
US 
S 
15 
million 
annually (or 
just 
over 
US 
$100/ha). 
With 
the 
Project: 
As 
a result 
of 
the 
rehabilitation, 
it 
has 
been 
assumed 
that 
silt 
entering 
the 
irrigation 
system 
would 
be 
reduced 
by 
an 
average 
of 
2 
m3/ha/year over 
the command 
area 
of 
140,000 ha. 
The 
NPV 
of 
this 
cost 
saving 
would 
be 
around 
US 
$1 
million 
and 
would 
not 
alone 
be 
sufficient 
to 
justify 
the costs 
of 
the works. 
In 
contrast, 
the 
benefit 
of 
averting 
the 
collapse 
of 
the 
headworks 
and 
the 
subsequent 
loss 
of 
irrigation 
to 
the 
140,000 
ha 
of 
command 
is 
substantial. 
If 
the 
headworks 
were 
to 
collapse at any 
time 
during 
the 
next 
20 
years, 
the 
consequent loss 
of 
the 
value 
of 
agricultural 
production 
cited 
in 
the 
WOP 
would 
easily 
exceed 
the 
costs 
of 
carrying 
out 
the 
works. 
Working 
on 
the assumption 
that a collapse 
of 
the 
headworks 
would 
occur 
in 
201012011, 
resulting 
in 
the 
equivalent 
of 
the 
loss 
of 
3

of

production, 
the 
rate 
of 
return 
on 
the

of 
Bahramtape 
would 
be 
39 
percent. 
This 
does 
not

of 
the

of

to 
the

and

Project 
Economic Analysis 
Combining 
the

of

of 
investments 
in 
WUA 
and 
Bahramtape

with

(including

as 
well

results 
in 
an

of 
21 
percent 
for

as 
a 
whole. 
Financial 
Analysis 
Financial 
analysis centers 
on 
investigating 
the 
financial 
returns 
to 
farmers 
from 
the 
improved 
irrigation 
infrastructure, 
and 
in 
turn 
on 
the 
sustainability 
of 
WUAs. 
The 
WUAs 
would 
be 
dependent 
on 
farmers 
capacity 
to 
pay 
irrigation 
service 
fees 
sufficient 
to 
pay 
to 
cost 
of 
management, operation, 
and 
maintenance 
of 
the 
irrigation 
networks. 
Net 
farm 
earnings range 
from 
AZM 
2.2 
milliodyear 
to 
just 
over 
AZM 
12 
milliodyear, 
the range 
depending 
not 
only on holding 
size 
and ownership 
of 
livestock, 
but 
also 
on 
potential 
for 
cash 
crop 
development. 
Farm 
incomes 
of 
both 
middle-income and 
poor 
families 
were 
estimated, 
with 
net 
farm 
earnings 
of 
middle-income 
families 
calculated 
at 
around 
AZM 
10 
milliodyear 
(US 
$2,00O/year) 
and 
those 
of 
poorer families 
at around 
AZM 
4 
milliodyear 
(US$ 
8OOlyear). 
Poor families 
all 
have 
farm 
earnings 
below 
the 
absolute 
poverty 
line 
(taken 
as 
AZM 
7.2 
milliodyear for 
a 
family 
of 
5). 
Farm 
earnings 
are 
supplemented 
by 
pension 
and 
wage payments, 
but 
these 
are 
still 
often insufficient 
to 
raise 
the 
families 
above 
the 
poverty 
line. 
About 
half 
of 
the 
net 
farm 
earnings 
of 
middle-income families 
are 
deemed 
to 
be received 
in 
cash, 
while for 
poorer families 
this 
proportion 
is 
typically 
only 
about 
a 
quarter. 
Land 
taxes 
and current water 
charges 
normally 
constitute a 
small 
proportion 
of 
net 
farm 
income, 
but 
for 
poorer families 
they 
can 
represent 
20 
to 
30 percent 
of 
the 
cash earnings. 
With 
the 
project, 
total 
charges 
for 
water 
by 
WUAs 
would 
rise 
substantially 
above 
the water 
fees 
currently 
charged 
(AZM 
10,325 
to 
18,438 
per 
ha or 
about 
US 
$2 
to 
3.75 
per 
ha). 
The 
average 
cost 
of 
WUA 
O&M 
is 
estimated 
at 
around 
US 
$15 
per 
ha. 
In 
addition, 
since 
SAICs  current 
ISF 
is 
set 
at 
a 
level 
which 
covers 
less 
than 
5 
percent 
of 
the 
estimated 
cost 
of 
operating 
and 
maintaining 
the 
off-farm 
irrigation 
network, 
it 
should 
not 
be 
reduced. 
To 
at 
least 
maintain 
SAICs  current 
revenue 
from 
water 
fees, 
a 
volumetric 
fee 
of 
about 
AZM 
2 
per 
m3 
would 
be 
required. 
WUAs 
would 
need 
to 
charge 
around 7 
times 
this 
current service 
fee 
to 
ensure 
that 
they 
cover 
their 
costs 
of 
operation and 
maintenance 
as 
well. 
In 
the 
rehabilitated 
WAS: 
It 
is 
anticipated that 
systems 
would 
become 
fully 
operational, 
-

frequency

Sensitivity analysis 
/

values 
of

(i>

of

against the 
dollar 
after 
2006; 
(ii)

in 
area 
cropped 
for 
the

(iii)

6 percent 
high

and

assumed

(iv) 
a

and 
(iii); 
and

of 
(i) 
and 
(ii). 
Even

of

results 
in 
acceptable 
returns.

Annex 
6(A):  Procurement  Arrangements 
AZERBAIJAN: IRRIGATION DISTRIBUTION SYSTEM 
& 
MANAGEMENT IMPROVEMENT 
PROJECT 
Procurement 
Procurement 
of 
goods 
and 
works 
would 
be 
done 
in 
accordance 
with 
the 
Bank's 
Guidelines 
Procurement under 
IBRD 
Loans 
and 
IDA 
Credits 
(issued 
in 
January 
1995, 
revised 
January 
and 
August 
1996, 
September 
1997, 
and 
January 
1999). 
The 
Borrower 
would 
use 
the 
Bank's 
standard 
bidding 
documents 
for 
goods and works, 
including 
standard 
evaluation 
reports. 
Consulting 
services 
and technical 
assistance 
would 
be 
procured 
in 
accordance 
with 
the 
Guidelines 
Selection 
and 
Employment 
of 
Consultants 
by 
World Bank Borrowers 
(January 
1997, 
revised 
September 
1997, 
January 
1999, 
and 
May 
2002). 
The 
Bank's Standard 
Request 
for 
Proposals 
and Forms 
of 
Consultants' 
Contract 
would 
be 
used. 
A 
General Procurement 
Notice 
(GPN) was 
published 
on 
April 
30,2003 
in 
issue 
605 
of 
the 
U.N. 
Development 
Business 
and 
also 
in 
a 
national 
newspaper. 
Procurement 
Responsibilities 
At 
the 
country 
level, 
a comprehensive analysis 
of 
the 
public 
procurement 
system 
in 
Azerbaijan, 
including 
legal 
and 
regulatory 
framework, 
institutional 
capacity, enforcement regime, was 
carried 
out 
during 
the 
Country 
Procurement Assessment 
Review 
(CPAR) 
in 
2002. 
The 
CPAR 
emphasizes 
the 
advanced 
state 
of 
the 
procurement 
legislation 
and 
the existence 
of 
an 
appropriate and 
competent 
regulatory 
framework.  Azerbaijan's 
public 
procurement 
law 
is 
based 
on 
the 
UNCITRAL 
model, 
and 
is 
generally 
satisfactory. 
There 
is 
a State 
Procurement 
Agency 
with 
appropriate 
duties, 
and 
which 
is 
active 
in 
ensuring 
compliance 
with 
the 
public 
procurement 
law. 
However, the 
capacity 
and 
recourses 
of 
the 
procurement 
system 
need 
to 
be 
strengthened substantially, 
and 
there 
is 
still 
little 
experience 
in 
the 
country 
with 
competitive procurement. 
The 
CPAR 
assesses 
Azerbaijan 
as 
a 
"medium 
to 
high 
risk" 
country 
from 
a 
procurement 
point 
of 
view. 
Responsibility 
for 
all 
procurement 
would 
rest 
with 
the 
PKJ, 
which 
is 
already 
in 
place 
with 
qualified 
national 
experts, 
recruited 
on 
competitive 
basis, 
for 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
ongoing RIDIP. 
An 
assessment 
of 
the 
PIU's capacity 
to 
conduct procurement 
for 
the 
proposed 
IDSMIP 
as 
well 
was 
carried 
out 
in 
December 2002. 
The 
PIU 
has 
been 
operational 
for 
over 
two 
years 
and 
has 
performed 
well. 
The 
assessment 
addressed 
the 
legal 
aspects, 
procurement 
cycle 
management, 
organization and functions 
of 
the 
procurement 
team 
in 
the 
PIU, support and 
control 
systems, 
record 
keeping, 
and staffing. 
The 
staff 
of 
the 
PKJ 
includes 
a 
full-time 
procurement 
specialist, 
who 
has 
received 
training 
in 
Bank 
procurement 
guidelines and 
applications, 
and 
acquired 
good 
experience 
in 
procurement 
under 
the 
RIDIP. 
The 
PKJ 
Director, 
who 
received procurement 
training 
in 
Turin, 
has 
also experience 
in 
procurement, 
and 
provides 
oversight 
to 
the 
procurement 
team. 
Procurement 
activities 
are 
generally satisfactory 
and 
no 
major 
problems 
were 
identified 
during 
the 
implementation 
of 
RIDIP 
so 
far. 
The 
procurement 
assessment 
rated 
the 
PIU 
procurement 
capacity 
as 
"Average 
risk", 
as 
it 
is 
not 
facing many 
of 
the problems and 
issues 
identified 
at country 
level 
by 
the 
CPAR. 
Following 
a 
recommendation 
of 
the 
procurement 
assessment, 
recruitment 
of 
one 
procurement 
assistant 
has 
already taken 
place, 
initially to work 
on 
RIDIP 
procurement, 
but, 
subject 
to 
satisfactory 
performance, 
to work 
on IDSMIP 
procurement 
in 
future. 
Procurement 
staff 
would 
be 
shared 
between 
the 
two 
projects, 
each 
with 
primary 
responsibility 
specific 
either 
to RIDIP 
and 
IDSMIP, 
but 
able 
to provide 
back-up support 
as 
needed. 
In 
collaboration 
with 
the 
PIU 
an action 
plan 
has 
been prepared 
to 
strengthen 
PWs 
capacity 
to 
administer 
procurement 
for 
the 
proposed 
IDSMIP. 
The 
plan 
includes: 
(i) 
the 
recruitment 
of 
an additional 
- 
73 
- 
Page  78
procurement 
staff 
before Credit 
effectiveness (already completed); 
(ii) 
a procurement 
book 
containing 
a 
full 
set 
of 
the 
latest 
Bank 
standard procurement documents 
would 
be 
provided to 
the 
PIU 
before 
effectiveness; 
(iii) 
the 
project launch 
workshop, 
which 
is 
scheduled 
for 
September 
2003, 
would 
have 
a 
session 
on 
procurement 
principles 
and 
arrangements; 
(iv) 
the 
Task 
Team 
Leader (TTL), 
who 
is 
a PAS, 
would 
review project 
procurement 
activities 
during 
every supervision mission, 
in 
order 
to 
provide 
guidance 
and support 
to 
the 
PIU 
in 
carrying 
out 
the 
procurement 
plan 
and activities; 
(v) 
the 
PIU 
would 
establish 
a 
computerized 
monitoring 
system, 
similar 
to 
the 
one 
for 
RIDIP, before 
the 
first 
procurement 
takes 
place; 
and 
(vi) 
a 
minimum 
of 
one 
in 
every 
five 
contracts 
representing 
different 
procurement 
methods (not subject 
to 
prior 
IDA 
review) 
would 
be reviewed 
on 
an 
ex-post basis 
during 
project 
implementation. 
The 
TTL 
would 
get 
assistance 
from 
a 
senior 
Bank 
procurement 
specialist 
for 
this. 
Information 
regarding 
procurement 
administration 
would 
be collected 
and recorded 
in 
the 
quarterly 
progress reports. 
The 
information 
would 
include: 
(i) 
cost estimates 
for individual 
contracts 
and 
the 
total 
project; 
(ii) 
timing 
for 
procurement 
actions, 
including 
contract 
completion 
times; 
and 
(iii) 
an 
updated procurement 
plan. 
The 
PIU 
prepares satisfactory 
quarterly Project 
Status 
Reports 
for 
RIDIP, 
incorporating Project 
Management 
Reports (PMR). 
A 
similar format 
of 
reporting 
would 
be 
followed 
for 
IDSMIP. 
The 
project 
would 
work 
with 
many 
WUAs, 
who 
would 
be 
involved 
in 
prioritizing 
design 
interventions, and supervising 
implementation 
of 
the 
rehabilitation 
works 
within 
the 
command 
areas 
of 
their 
management. 
All 
procurement responsibilities would, 
however, 
rest 
with 
the 
PIU. 
Procurement 
Methods 
(Table 
A) 
The 
investment 
part 
of 
the 
project 
would 
include 
procurement 
of 
works, goods 
and 
services. 
A 
detailed 
procurement 
plan 
is 
included 
in 
the 
Project 
Implementation 
Plan. 
It 
is 
estimated 
that 
about 142 
IDA 
financed 
contracts 
would 
be 
generated 
during 
project 
implementation, 
of 
which 
60 
would 
be 
for 
procurement 
of 
works, 
42 
contracts 
for 
procurement 
of 
goods, 
and 
40 
for 
procurement 
of 
consulting 
services. 
The 
procurement 
arrangements 
are 
described 
in 
the 
next 
paragraphs, 
which 
also 
indicate 
the 
applicable 
thresholds, 
which 
are 
very similar 
to 
the 
ones 
recommended 
in 
the 
CPAR. 
The 
aggregate 
amount 
for 
each 
procurement 
method 
is 
given 
in 
table 
A. 
Civil 
Works. 
The project 
would 
carry 
out 
rehabilitation 
works 
of 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
systems 
in 
11 
raions 
of 
Azerbaijan. 
Civil 
works 
would 
include 
rehabilitation 
of: 
(i) 
off-farm 
infrastructure, 
including 
headworks, 
primary 
and 
secondary 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
canals, 
and 
hydraulic 
structures; 
and 
(ii) 
on-farm 
infrastructure, consisting 
of 
tertiary and 
lower 
order 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
networks, 
including 
structures. 
International 
Competitive 
Bidding 
(ICB). 
All 
civil 
works 
estimated 
to 
cost 
US$ 
600,000 
or 
more 
per 
contract 
would 
be procured according 
to ICB. 
This 
includes 
large works 
such 
as 
the 
rehabilitation 
of 
Bahramtape headworks, 
with 
a 
cost estimate 
of 
US 
$4.2 
million. 
Other 
ICB 
contracts 
estimated 
to 
cost 
US 
$1.3 
million 
in 
aggregate 
would 
include 
rehabilitation 
of 
some 
of 
the 
larger 
off-farm 
systems. 
In 
the 
comparison 
of 
bids 
for 
civil 
works procured though 
ICB, 
a 
domestic 
preference 
would 
apply 
in 
accordance 
with 
the 
provisions 
of 
the 
procurement guidelines. 
National 
Competitive 
Bidding 
(NCB). 
Works 
estimated 
to 
cost 
between 
US 
$50,000 
and 
US 
$600,000 
would 
be 
procured through 
NCB. 
It 
is 
anticipated 
that 
45 
WUAs 
would 
participate, 
with 
an 
average 
on-farm investment 
of 
US$375,000, 
with 
a range 
from 
about 
US 
$150,000 
to 
US 
$600,000. 
WUAs 
would 
be 
selected 
depending 
on 
the 
status 
of 
their 
development, 
and 
according 
to 
a number 
of 
- 
74 
- 
Page  79
criteria 
that 
have 
been detailed 
in 
the 
Project Implementation 
Plan.  Sub-projects 
benefiting 
the 
WUAs 
would 
be implemented 
under 
close 
supervision 
of 
the 
PIU 
and 
respective 
WUA 
management. 
Rehabilitation 
of 
some 
of 
the 
off-farm 
systems 
and 
the 
hydroposts 
would 
also 
be conducted 
through 
an 
estimated 
12 
NCB 
contracts. 
Given 
the 
anticipated small contract 
values 
and 
the 
dispersed 
location 
of 
the 
sub-projects, 
ICB 
is 
not 
expected 
for 
on-farm rehabilitation 
works. 
Most 
of 
the 
civil 
works 
contracts 
for 
WUA 
command 
areas 
would 
be 
relatively 
simple and straightforward, 
comprising 
mainly 
canal and 
drainage cleaning, 
repair 
of 
canal 
lining, 
and 
rehabilitation and construction 
of 
hydraulic 
structures. 
Experience 
of 
the FPP 
has 
shown 
that 
there 
is 
a 
sufficient 
number 
of 
local 
contractors 
with 
adequate 
capacity 
to 
carry 
out 
such 
kind 
of 
works. 
The 
performance 
of 
national 
contractors under 
RIDIP 
has 
also 
generally been 
satisfactory. 
Sufficient 
interest 
and 
capacity 
is 
envisaged 
to provide 
a 
competitive 
environment. 
ECA 
sample 
NCB 
bidding 
documents 
would 
be 
used, 
with 
which 
the 
PIU 
is 
familiar. 
The 
procedures 
to 
be 
followed 
for 
NCB 
would 
be 
those 
set 
forth 
in 
the 
Public 
Procurement 
Law 
of 
Azerbaijan. 
Clarifications 
required 
for 
compliance 
with 
the 
provisions 
of 
the 
Guidelines 
for 
Procurement 
under 
IBRD 
Loans 
and 
IDA 
Credits 
has 
been 
provided 
in 
a 
supplemental 
letter 
on 
NCB 
procedures. 
The 
letter 
has 
been 
annexed 
in 
the PIP. 
Minor 
Works 
(Mv. 
Works 
valued 
at 
less 
than 
US 
$50,000 
per 
contract 
would 
be procured 
on 
the 
basis 
of 
at 
least 
three 
price 
quotations. 
The 
invitation 
to 
quote 
shall include 
a 
detailed description 
of 
the 
works, 
including 
basic specifications, the 
required 
completion 
date, 
a 
basic 
form 
of 
agreement 
acceptable 
to 
the Association, 
and 
relevant 
drawings, 
where applicable. 
The award 
of 
contract 
shall 
be 
made 
to 
the contractor 
who 
offers 
the 
lowest 
price 
quotation 
for 
the 
required 
works, 
and 
who 
was 
the 
experience 
and 
resources 
to 
complete the 
contract 
successfully. 
MW 
is 
expected 
to 
be 
used 
for 
office 
renovations. 
Goods. 
The 
goods 
to 
be 
financed 
in 
the 
project 
would 
include 
vehicles, 
motor-cycles, 
office 
equipment, 
environmental 
monitoring 
equipment, 
furniture, 
and various 
small 
equipment 
for 
WUAs 
(e.g. 
office 
equipment, 
small 
maintenance 
machinery, 
and 
field 
testing 
equipment). 
For 
goods packages 
estimated 
to 
cost 
more 
than 
US 
$100,000 
each, 
ICB 
procedures 
shall 
be 
used. 
For 
the 
purchase 
of 
goods 
to 
be 
awarded 
through 
ICB, 
the 
Borrower 
may 
grant 
a 
margin 
preference 
of 
15 
percent 
to 
qualified 
domestic manufacturers, 
in 
accordance 
with 
the 
Guidelines referred 
to 
above. 
International 
shopping 
(IS), 
based 
on 
comparison 
of 
quotes 
from 
at 
least three 
suppliers 
from two 
eligible 
countries, 
shall 
be 
used 
for 
contracts estimated 
to 
cost 
between 
US 
$50,000 
and 
US 
$100,000 
per 
package. 
Alternatively 
IAPSO 
of 
the 
United 
nations 
could 
be 
used 
to 
source 
such 
goods. 
National 
Shopping 
(NS) 
would 
be 
used 
for 
contracts 
for 
goods estimated 
to 
cost 
less 
than 
US$ 
50,000 
per 
contract, 
to 
be 
awarded 
on 
the 
basis 
of 
three 
written price 
quotations 
from 
local 
suppliers. 
The 
procurement 
of 
goods 
for 
the 45 
WUAs 
(US 
$1,941,000 
in 
aggregate) 
would 
be

by 
the 
PIU 
through 
a number 
of 
packages 
throughout the 
project 
duration. 
The 
composition 
of 
the 
packages 
would 
to 
a 
certain

on 
the 
demand 
of 
the 
WUAs, 
but 
is 
expected 
to 
include 
equipment 
such 
as 
computer, 
motorcycle, 
two-way 
radio, 
field 
testing 
devices, 
small 
canal

machinery, 
and 
office 
furniture. 
Goods 
to 
be 
procured 
for 
the 
qualifying 
WUAs 
would 
be 
consolidated 
to 
the extent practicable 
into 
a 
number 
of 
bid 
packages 
or 
lots 
depending 
on 
their 
composition 
to 
achieve 
economy 
of 
scale 
and

It 
is 
foreseen 
that 
this

would

during

implementation through 
50 
percent 
IS 
and 
50 
percent 
NS, 
depending 
on 
the

of

The

plan 
for 
goods 
would

every

for

and approval.

Consultant 
Services. 
Additional 
PIU 
staff 
would 
be 
recruited 
on 
a 
competitive 
basis 
as 
individual 
consultants, 
initially 
for 
one 
year, 
with 
the 
possibility 
of 
extension 
upon 
satisfactory 
performance. 
National individual 
consultants 
would 
be 
recruited 
to 
provide 
specialized technical 
assistance 
in 
the 
areas 
of 
legislation, 
community mobilization, environmental monitoring, 
training 
and 
information 
dissemination. 
Intemational 
short-term 
consultants 
would 
be recruited 
by 
SAIC 
and 
PIU 
for 
specialized 
design 
tasks 
(e.g. 
modem 
hydraulic 
structures).  Such 
individual 
specialists 
would 
have 
specific 
skills 
needed 
for 
short 
periods 
of 
time 
during 
the 
first 
years 
of 
the 
project. 
All 
individual 
consultants 
would 
be 
selected 
in 
accordance 
with 
Part 
V 
of 
the Consultant Guidelines. 
National 
consultants, 
including 
PIU 
staff, cannot 
be 
civil 
servants. 
Design 
and 
supervision 
services 
for 
the 
on-farm 
and 
off-farm 
rehabilitation works 
would 
be 
procured through 
QCBS.  Considering that most 
of 
the 
field 
investigations, 
surveys,

activities 
are 
relatively 
easy, 
active 
participation 
of 
national consulting 
firms 
as 
sub-consultants 
is 
anticipated. 
There 
would 
be 
a 
separate 
QCBS contract 
for 
design 
and 
supervision 
of 
Bahramtape 
weir, 
which 
requires specialized 
expertise. 
A 
Technical 
Assistance 
(TA) 
team 
to provide 
technical 
expertise 
in 
WUA 
development 
would 
be 
procured through 
QCBS. 
It 
is 
important 
that 
these 
services commence soon 
after 
project 
effectiveness. 
The 
terms 
of 
reference have already been 
drafted 
and 
the 
approved 
Request 
for 
Proposals 
is 
expected 
to

early

2003. 
The

16,2003. 
Three

Consultants' 
Qualifications.

Incremental Operating 
Costs. 
The project 
would 
finance incremental operating 
costs 
of 
about 
US 
$1,250,000 
(US 
$930,000 
to

by 
IDA), 
consisting 
of

for

These

would 
include: 
(i) 
operation and 
maintenance

and 
other 
costs agreed 
with 
the 
Association. 
Each 
Support 
Unit 
would 
open a 
bank 
account 
in 
the 
local 
branch 
of 
the 
International 
Bank 
of 
Azerbaijan 
or 
other 
commercial 
banks 
that 
would 
be 
capable 
of 
executing 
foreign 
exchange and 
local 
currency 
transactions 
promptly 
and 
capable 
of 
issuing 
prompt 
monthly 
bank 
statements, 
while 
charging 
reasonable 
fees 
for 
their 
services. 
The 
PIU 
would 
transfer 
60-day 
advances 
to 
the 
local 
bank 
accounts 
on 
the basis 
of 
an estimate 
provided 
by 
the 
Support 
Units. 
Typically 
the 
advance 
would 
not 
exceed 
US 
$400 
per 
Support 
Unit, 
and 
would 
be 
for 
expenditures 
as 
defined 
under 
Training 
and Incremental Operating 
Costs 
in 
Schedule 
1 
of 
the 
Development 
Credit 
Agreement. 
The 
advances 
would 
be reconciled 
once 
every 
two 
months 
or 
when 
80 
percent 
of 
the 
funds 
have 
been 
utilized, 
at 
which 
time 
the Support 
Units 
would 
provide 
the 
PIU 
with 
receipts and 
estimates 
for 
the 
next 
60-days 
operational 
budget. 
Since the 
Borrower 
would 
be 
empowered 
to 
manage 
and 
account 
for 
funds 
which 
are 
advanced 
from 
the 
Special Account, 
it 
would 
be 
necessary 
to 
ensure 
that 
minimum 
safeguards 
are 
put 
in 
place. 
The 
PIU 
would 
have 
the 
overall responsibility 
for 
maintaining 
proper 
books 
of 
accounts 
for 
the 
project 
funds, 
allocate 
funds 
to 
the decentralized locations 
based 
on 
identified 
and 
justified 
implementation 
needs, 
and 
closely 
supervise 
and 
monitor 
this 
decentralized 
flow 
of 
funds. 
The 
PIU 
has 
already 
designed 
a 
standard 
reporting 
format, 
to 
be 
able 
to 
get 
consistent 
financial reporting 
from 
all 
the Support 
Units. 
The 
PIU 
would not 
make 
hrther 
advances 
to 
the 
Support 
Units 
until 
proper accounting 
has 
been 
received 
fiom 
them. 
Financial 
Covenants. 
The 
PIU 
would 
maintain 
an 
adequate 
financial 
management system 
and 
furnish 
to 
IDA 
quarterly 
FMRs 
on 
the 
financial, 
monitoring 
and procurement 
management 
activities. 
As 
indicated 
above, 
independent 
extemal 
auditors under terms 
of 
reference 
acceptable 
to 
IDA 
would 
audit 
the 
Project 
Financial 
Statements, 
the Special 
Account 
and 
disbursements 
made 
on 
basis 
of 
SOEs, 
and 
submit 
the audit 
report 
to 
IDA 
no 
later than six 
months 
after 
the 
end 
of 
each year audited. 
Supervision 
Plan. 
The Association 
would 
conduct 
financial 
management 
supervision every 
six 
months 
to monitor 
progress 
of 
project 
implementation. 
The 
financial 
management 
supervision 
would 
pay 
particular attention 
to: 
(i) 
disbursements 
and 
financial 
management arrangements 
including review 
of 
SOEs; 
(ii) 
review 
of 
project's 
financial monitoring 
reports and audited 
financial 
statements; 
and 
(iii) 
review 
of 
implementation 
of 
progress 
of 
remedial 
actions recommended 
in 
the 
Auditors 
Management 
Letters.

Additional 
Annex 
11 
: 
Executive 
Summary of 
Environmental Assessment 
AZERBAIJAN: IRRIGATION DISTRIBUTION 
SYSTEM 
& 
MANAGEMENT 
IMPROVEMENT 
PROJECT 
Project 
Description 
At 
the 
request 
of 
the 
Government 
of 
Azerbaijan, 
the 
IDA 
prepared 
an 
Irrigation Distribution 
System 
and 
Management Improvement 
Project (IDSMIP).  The 
IDSMP 
would 
support 
the 
development 
of 
Azerbaijans 
Water 
User 
Associations 
(WUAs) 
and 
finance 
rehabilitation 
of 
associated 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
infrastructure. 
The 
infrastructure improvements 
would 
be 
made 
on 
a demand-driven 
basis 
with 
WUAs 
that 
meet 
certain 
eligibility 
criteria 
in 
the 
project 
area 
raions 
in 
Azerbaijan 
(Aghjabedi, 
Beylagan, 
Geranboy, 
Guba, 
Imishli, 
Khachmaz, Saatli, 
Sabirabad, 
Zardab) 
and 
in 
the 
Autonomous 
Republic 
of 
Nakhchivan 
(Babek, Sharur). 
Some 
associated 
off-farm 
infrastructure improvements 
(including 
the 
rehabilitation 
of 
Bahramtape headworks) 
would 
be 
made 
to 
ensure 
effective delivery 
of 
water 
to 
the 
rehabilitated on-farm 
systems. 
Environmental 
Assessment 
(EA) 
The 
EA 
has been prepared 
for 
the proposed 
project 
in 
order 
to 
ensure 
that 
it 
incorporates sound 
environmental 
management 
principles 
and practices 
and 
complies 
with 
World 
Bank 
environmental 
safeguards 
as 
well 
as 
with 
Azerbaijani 
environmental 
requirements. 
The 
EA 
examined proposed 
project 
activities, 
the 
environmental 
policy, and 
the 
legal 
and 
institutional 
framework 
in 
Azerbaijan. 
It 
also 
examined 
the 
existing situation 
in 
the 
project 
area 
to 
identify 
the 
environmental and 
social 
impacts 
of 
the 
proposed project, 
both 
positive 
and negative, 
and 
specify appropriate 
preventive 
actions 
and 
mitigation 
measures 
to 
prevent, 
eliminate 
or 
minimize 
anticipated 
adverse 
impacts 
and 
to 
ensure 
the 
projects 
compliance 
with 
Azerbaijani 
legislation. 
Furthermore, 
the 
EA 
proposed 
certain 
institutional 
arrangements, 
capacity-building 
and 
monitoring 
activities 
to 
ensure 
that the 
prevention 
and 
mitigation 
measures 
are 
implemented. 
The 
EA 
has 
been 
prepared 
by 
a 
team 
of 
international environmental 
experts 
from 
the 
UN 
Food 
and 
Agriculture 
Organization 
(FAO), 
worhng 
together 
with 
the 
Environmental 
Specialist 
of 
the 
Project Implementation 
Unit 
(PIU) 
and 
national environmental 
experts. 
Consultations 
The 
EA 
team 
began 
consulting 
stakeholders 
and beneficiaries 
of 
the 
proposed 
project 
during 
its 
first 
three missions. 
It 
met 
with 
officials 
in 
the 
SAIC, 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Ecology 
and 
Natural 
Resources 
and other 
national 
institutions; 
with 
local 
SAIC 
officials 
and 
WUA 
members 
in 
the 
raions; 
with 
environmental NGOs; and 
with 
farmers 
and 
other 
beneficiaries. 
Public 
consultations 
were 
organized 
during 
the 
fourth 
mission 
in 
Baku 
and 
Sabirabad 
to 
inform 
project 
beneficiaries 
and stakeholders 
of 
the 
findings 
and 
recommendations 
of 
the 
EA, 
discuss 
and 
improve 
understanding 
of 
the 
potential 
impacts 
of 
the 
project, 
review 
the 
proposed 
mitigation 
measures 
to 
ensure 
their 
adequacy, 
appropriateness 
and 
acceptability, 
and 
receive and incorporate 
comments 
from 
consultation 
participants. 
Main 
Anticipated 
Positive 
Impacts 
Improved 
Agricultural 
Productivity. 
Infrastructure 
rehabilitation 
should 
enhance 
productivity 
by 
both 
improving irrigation 
water 
delivery 
and 
bringing 
fallow 
lands 
back 
into 
production. 
Strengthened 
WUAs 
should 
improve 
water resourceslsoil 
fertility 
management 
and 
promote 
consistent 
operation and 
maintenance 
of 
irrigation 
and 
drainage structures. 
- 
90 
- 
Page  95
Increased 
Farm 
Income, 
Alleviation 
of 
Poverty. 
Assuming 
improvements 
in 
agncultural 
marketing 
and 
ago-industries 
as 
well 
as 
access 
to 
credit 
and extension services 
over 
time, 
increased 
agricultural 
productivity 
and 
return 
to 
cash crops 
should 
lead 
to 
increases 
in 
farm 
family 
incomes, 
improved 
employment, and a 
reduction 
in 
rural 
poverty. 
Improvement 
in 
Health. 
Drainage 
system 
improvements 
would 
reduce the 
areas 
of 
standing 
water where 
disease 
vectors 
multiply. 
Improved 
operation 
and maintenance 
of 
these systems 
should 
encourage farmers 
to 
improve 
health conditions 
in 
the 
rural 
areas. 
Reduction 
in 
Water 
Losses, 
Soil 
Salinity, and 
Waterlogging. 
The 
rehabilitation 
of 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
systems 
would 
reduce 
water 
losses, 
improve control 
of 
groundwater 
levels, 
and 
prevent 
soil 
salinization and 
waterlogging. 
Improved 
Water Resources 
Management. 
By 
strengthening 
WAS, 
encouraging better water 
measurement, 
enhancing 
irrigation 
service fee 
collection, 
and 
eliminating 
illegal 
abstractions, 
the 
IDSMIP would 
promote 
better 
control 
on 
water 
use 
and contribute 
to 
more 
rational 
water 
management. 
Potential Negative 
Impacts and 
Recommended 
Preventive 
and 
Mitigation 
Measures 
Construction Impacts. 
Mostly 
on-site, small-scale 
impacts 
on 
access 
and 
movement, 
agricultural 
activities, and vegetation and 
wildlife. 
Bahramtape headworks 
rehabilitation may 
involve 
additional 
construction 
impacts: 
water and 
soil pollution; 
disruption 
of 
the 
hydrological 
system, 
irrigation 
water 
supply 
and 
fisheries; 
damage 
to 
the 
river 
ecosystem 
or 
the surrounding 
landscape. 
Recommended 
Measures. 
Environmental, 
health and 
safety 
covenants have been 
incorporated 
in 
the 
bidding 
documents 
of 
construction 
contracts; 
environmental 
management 
guidelines 
for 
construction 
contractors; 
site-specific environmental 
management 
plans and 
related 
staff 
training; 
restrictions 
on 
the 
use 
of 
heavy 
machinery 
at 
ecologically 
sensitive 
sites. 
Management 
of 
Dredged 
Sediment 
and 
Concrete 
Debris. 
Indiscriminate 
dumping 
of 
sediments 
may 
cause 
loss 
of 
agricultural 
land, 
erosion 
and 
resilting 
of 
the 
canals. 
Contaminated 
sediments 
may 
adversely 
affect 
the 
natural 
environment and 
accumulate 
in 
crops.  Concrete 
debris 
and 
metal 
scrap, 
if 
dumped 
indiscriminately, 
would 
damage 
landscape 
and 
cause 
pollution. 
Recommended 
Measures. 
S 
ediment 
sampling 
prior to 
canal 
dredging; 
proper 
disposal 
of 
contaminated material; 
use 
of 
non-contaminated 
sediments 
for 
field 
levelling 
or 
as 
fill 
material; a 
waste management 
plan 
for 
concrete/metal waste 
to 
identify 
proper 
reuse 
and disposal opportunities. 
Articles 
would 
be 
incorporated 
in 
the 
technical specifications 
of 
the standard 
bidding 
documents. 
Decreased 
Downstream 
Water 
Quality. 
Main 
risks 
are 
increased drainage 
from 
saline 
soils 
and 
inadequate management 
of 
pumping 
station 
waste 
and, 
in 
the 
long-term, 
increases 
in 
ago-chemical 
use 
induced, 
indirectly 
and gradually, 
by 
productivity 
improvements and 
return 
to 
cash crops that 
the 
IDSMIP 
is 
expected 
to 
bring. 
Chemical over-application 
may 
be 
done 
by 
farmers unaware 
of 
risks 
and 
subsequent 
water 
pollution. 
Recommended 
Measures. 
Expose farmers 
through agricultural 
extension 
services 
to 
proper 
irrigation 
and 
other 
agricultural 
practices, 
Integrated 
Pest 
and 
Production 
Management 
(IPPM), 
proper 
agro-chemical 
application and 
storage methods, 
as 
well 
as 
awareness 
raising 
on 
the 
risks 
of 
obsoletebanned chemicals 
use. 
Guidance 
on 
proper 
pumping 
station 
management 
would 
be 
provided 
to 
pump 
operators. 
Reduction 
in 
Downstream 
River 
Flows. 
The project 
would 
not significantly 
increase 
irrigation 
water withdrawals. 
Present 
abstraction 
levels 
may, 
in 
dry 
years, 
conflict 
with 
ecological 
minimum 
flows. 
- 
91 
- 
Page  96
Recommended 
Measures. 
Installing 
river 
flow 
measuring 
equipment 
at 
headworks; 
promotion 
of 
water-conserving 
irrigation 
techniques; 
and a 
review 
of 
ecological 
minimum 
flow 
requirements 
to 
be 
initiated 
at 
the 
national 
level. 
Impacts 
on 
Agricultural 
Soils. 
Topsoil 
erosion 
due 
to 
inappropriate 
irrigation 
methods; 
canal 
erosion 
due 
to 
poor 
desigdconstruction; 
waterlogging 
and 
soil 
salinization 
caused 
by 
over-watering and 
poor 
subsurface drainage; 
leaching 
of 
nutrients 
and 
reduction 
in 
soil 
fertility 
caused 
by 
irrigation 
retum 
flows. 
Recommended Measures. 
Farmers 
would 
be 
encouraged 
to level 
land; 
reline 
canals susceptible 
to 
erosion 
and rehabilitate 
associated 
hydraulic 
structures; 
remove 
unauthorized 
connections 
to 
canals/drains; 
improve 
water 
distribution, 
management 
and 
irrigation 
practices; 
clear 
field 
drainage 
before 
each 
irrigation 
season; 
and introduce crop 
rotation 
cycles.  Sub-surface 
water 
level 
and 
quality 
monitoring 
to 
be carried 
out 
by 
WUAs. 
Reduction 
of 
Water Levels 
in 
Sensitive Wetlands. 
Any 
participating 
project 
site 
located 
close 
to 
sensitive 
wetland 
habitats 
(Lakes Ag-gel, Boz-Gobu, 
Mehman 
and 
Sarisu) 
might 
adversely 
affect 
the 
wetland 
ecosystems 
if 
they 
reduce 
the 
amount 
of 
water 
flowing 
or 
draining 
into 
them. 
Recommended 
Measures. 
Continue 
the 
FUDIP 
ecological wetland 
monitoring 
program, 
as 
needed; 
phase 
IDSMIP 
activities 
so 
that 
infrastructure 
on 
any 
WUA 
adjacent 
to 
sensitive 
wetlands 
would not 
be rehabilitated 
before 
adequate 
monitoring information 
is 
available; 
expert 
panel 
to review monitoring 
results 
and 
decide 
on 
mitigation 
measures 
if 
needed. 
Water 
Pollution 
on 
Sensitive Wetlands. 
Water 
salinity or ago-chemical 
pollution 
could 
have 
negative impacts 
on 
the 
shallow 
wetland 
habitats. 
Recommended 
Measures. 
Monitor 
the 
quality 
of 
inflowing 
drainage water; 
if 
needed, 
establish 
natural 
vegetation 
filters 
in 
the 
tail 
end 
of 
secondary 
drains 
or 
small, 
uncultivated 
buffer 
zones 
along 
the 
edges 
of 
field 
drains. 
Reduction 
of 
On-farm 
Habitat. 
Rehabilitation 
of 
irrigation 
infrastructure 
would 
eliminate 
some 
of 
the 
mini-wetlands occurring 
in 
drainage canals 
and 
may 
damage 
vegetation 
along 
irrigation 
canals, 
potentially affecting 
habitats 
of 
rarelprotected 
species. 
Recommended 
Measures. 
Clean 
drainage canals 
only 
where 
this 
is 
needed 
to 
prevent salinity/waterlogging; 
perform 
site 
assessment 
before construction 
to 
identify 
any 
important 
habitatslsites located 
alongin 
the 
canals 
proposed 
for 
rehabilitation 
and 
define 
appropriate site-specific 
preventive/mitigation 
measures. 
Irrigation 
Water-related 
Health 
Problems. 
Health 
impacts 
are 
primarily 
positive, 
but 
the 
availability 
of 
more 
irrigation 
water 
in 
the 
canals 
and 
collectors 
may 
also 
lead to 
increases 
in 
disease 
vectors, 
with 
associated 
risks 
where 
irrigation 
water 
is 
used 
as 
drinking 
water. 
Recommended 
Measures. 
Ensure 
that 
farmers 
discontinue any water 
retention 
practices that 
provide 
breeding 
areas 
for 
mosquitoes; 
train 
farmers 
in 
proper 
vector 
control 
techniques 
and 
safe 
drinking 
water 
practices. 
Water User 
Conflicts. 
There 
is 
potential 
for 
conflicts 
between 
WAS 
and 
among 
WUA 
members over 
actual 
or 
perceived 
inequities 
in 
water 
distribution. 
Recommended 
Measures. 
Establish 
a 
transparent, equitable 
and corruption-free 
system 
of 
water 
allocation 
and 
distribution; 
strengthen 
WAS 
to 
address 
equitable 
water 
management 
and 
use 
conflict 
resolution 
strategies, 
and prevent 
illegalhnplanned 
connections 
to 
canalddrains. 
Environmental 
Screening 
and 
Review 
The 
demand-driven 
nature 
of 
the 
IDSMIP 
means 
that 
the exact 
number 
and 
location 
of 
the 
infrastructure 
rehabilitation 
sub-projects 
to 
be 
financed 
have yet 
to 
be 
determined. 
In 
order 
to 
ensure 
that 
- 
92 
- 
Page  97
the 
appropriate 
preventive 
and 
mitigation 
measures are 
applied 
to 
specific 
sub-project 
rehabilitation 
sites 
on 
a 
case-by-case basis, 
the 
EA 
proposes 
environmental 
screening 
and 
review 
procedures. 
The 
initial 
screening 
would 
cover 
all 
of 
the 
infrastructure 
rehabilitation 
sub-projects. 
A 
sub-project 
would 
not 
be 
eligible 
if 
it 
involves: 
(i) 
construction 
of 
significant 
new 
irrigation 
or 
drainage 
systems; 
(ii) 
a 
significant 
increase 
in 
the 
withdrawal 
of 
river 
water; 
or 
(iii) 
conversion 
of 
important natural 
habitat 
into 
agricultural 
use. 
Furthermore, 
an 
environmental 
screening 
checklist 
would 
be 
completed 
on 
each 
sub-project 
by 
the 
WUA 
concerned 
to 
identify 
potential environmental 
impacts, 
suggest 
appropriate 
preventive and 
mitigation 
measures, 
and 
trigger, 
where 
appropriate, 
further 
environmental 
review 
by 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Ecology 
and 
Natural 
Resources. 
The 
PIU 
would 
assist 
in 
the 
preparation 
of 
checklists, 
approve 
them, 
supervise 
and 
monitor 
their 
implementation, 
and assist 
in 
possible 
ministry 
review. 
Monitoring 
Monitoring 
of 
the environmental 
and 
social impacts 
of 
the 
project 
would 
be 
performed 
by 
the 
PIU 
and 
the 
WAS. 
The 
PIU 
would 
be 
responsible 
for 
implementation 
of 
general 
and site-specific 
mitigation 
measures, 
and 
monitoring 
of 
impact 
on 
wetlands, 
construction 
impacts, sediment sampling, 
subsurface 
water 
level 
and quality, and 
soil 
salinization. 
The 
WUAs 
would 
be 
responsible 
for 
monitoring 
on-farm 
erosion 
and 
illegal 
connections, 
as 
well 
as 
assisting the 
PIU 
in 
monitoring 
on-farm 
subsurface 
water and 
soil 
salinization. 
An 
indication 
of 
the proposed environmental 
monitoring 
parameters, 
as 
well 
as 
the 
responsibility 
and 
schedule 
for 
monitoring 
them, 
is 
shown 
in 
the 
table 
on 
the 
next 
page. 
Capacity 
Building 
Environmental 
capacity 
building 
and 
agricultural 
extensionitraining 
are 
included 
in 
the 
project to 
ensure 
effective implementation 
of 
the 
mitigation 
measures. 
Environmental 
capacity 
building 
would 
include: 
(i) 
an 
environmental 
specialist 
in 
the 
PIU; 
(ii) 
support 
to 
the 
environmental specialist 
through 
technical 
training 
and environmental 
consultants; 
(iii) 
environmental 
training 
to 
PIU 
management 
and 
staff; 
(iv) 
training 
of 
environmental 
counterparts 
in 
participating 
WAS; 
(v) 
environmental 
training 
for 
SAIC 
central 
and 
field 
office 
staff, 
mainly 
through 
the 
IDA-funded 
Urgent Environmental 
Investment 
Project; and 
(vi) 
environmental 
support 
to 
SAIC 
through national 
experts. 
The

the

Project, 
which 
is 
already 
being 
implemented 
in 
many 
of 
the 
areas 
to 
be 
covered 
by 
the 
IDSMIP.
