Additional 
Annex 
12: 
Executive Summary 
of Social 
Assessment 
AZERBAIJAN: 
IRRIGATION DISTRIBUTION SYSTEM 
& 
MANAGEMENT IMPROVEMENT 
PROJECT 
The 
purpose 
of 
this 
annex 
is 
to 
provide 
supplemental 
background 
and 
rationale 
for 
the 
social 
analysis 
presented 
in 
the 
body 
of 
the 
text 
in 
Section 
E.6, 
and 
to 
discuss 
Social Impact 
Monitoring.  The 
annex 
draws 
on 
the 
findings 
of 
the 
social 
and 
institutional 
assessment 
undertaken 
during 
project 
preparation, the 
social 
assessment 
for 
RIDIP, 
baseline 
survey 
for 
RIDIP, 
other 
IDA, 
Government 
and 
UNDP 
reports, 
and 
the 
field 
observations 
of 
project 
consultants 
and 
IDA 
Staff. 
Background 
and 
Justification 
of 
the 
IDSMIP 
The 
IDSMIP 
aims 
not 
only 
to 
improve 
local 
livelihoods 
by 
rehabilitating irrigation 
systems 
but 
also 
and 
more importantly, 
to 
build 
the 
social 
and 
institutional 
capacity 
for 
the 
sustainable 
operation 
and 
maintenance 
of 
irrigation 
infrastructure. 
The transition 
process 
in 
Azerbaijan 
has 
been 
marked 
by 
poor 
attention 
to 
the 
social 
and technical 
infrastructure 
that supports 
agricultural production. 
Because the 
main 
focus 
was 
on 
farm 
enterprises 
rather than communities, 
agricultural 
crop 
production 
received 
greater 
attention than 
the 
ownership 
and 
management 
of 
common 
property, social 
assets 
and 
infrastructure. 
The 
focus 
on 
privatization 
and 
the 
farming 
unit, 
and 
the 
neglect 
of 
infrastructure and 
the 
farming 
community, 
has 
had 
a negative social 
impact 
on 
the 
whole 
rural 
sector. 
The 
collapsing 
irrigation 
infrastructure 
is 
one 
consequence 
of 
the 
transition 
process 
that 
has 
clearly 
negative 
consequences 
for 
local 
development. 
The 
direct 
social objective 
of 
IDSMIP 
would 
be 
to 
improve 
local 
livelihoods 
by 
increasing 
agricultural 
productivity.  Agriculture 
is 
the 
main 
household 
livelihood 
strategy 
and 
the 
main 
determinant 
of 
rural 
income; 
poor 
irrigation 
infrastructure 
is 
one 
of 
the 
main 
limits 
to 
agricultural 
growth. 
The 
Household 
Budget 
Survey 
(2001) 
and the 
UNDP 
Human 
Development Report 
(2002) 
all 
indicate that 
agriculture provides 
a 
critical 
safety 
net 
against 
poverty. 
The 
diagnostic 
study 
undertaken 
for 
this 
project 
established 
that 
agricultural 
income 
in 
cash 
and 
in-kind 
is 
the 
biggest 
source 
of 
income 
for 
all 
wealth 
groups. 
For 
poorer 
households (about 
35 
percent 
of 
the sample) 
agriculture 
was 
essentially 
subsistence 
production, 
while 
many 
of 
the 
wealthier 
households 
derive 
most 
of 
their 
cash 
income 
from 
agricultural 
production. 
Focus groups 
held 
during 
the diagnostic 
study 
established 
that 
the 
conditions 
for 
agriculture 
were 
felt 
to 
be improving; 
a 
conclusion 
confirmed 
by 
Azerbaijan's 
Human 
Development 
Index, 
which 
has 
reported 
growth 
in 
the 
agricultural 
sector since 
1998. 
In 
sum, 
the 
IDSMIP 
would 
provide 
critical 
assistance 
to 
a 
sector 
that 
not only 
constitutes 
the 
main 
safety 
net 
against 
poverty 
in 
the 
country, 
but 
also 
offers 
most 
rural 
people 
the 
main 
opportunity 
for 
growth 
and 
investment. 
Indirect 
social benefits that 
could 
be 
expected 
from IDSMIP 
include 
increased 
employment and 
an 
increase 
in 
the 
value 
of 
land.  Increased 
employment 
is 
one 
of 
the 
key 
strategic 
aims 
of 
the 
State 
Program 
on 
Poverty 
Reduction 
and 
Economic 
Development. 
Both 
employment 
generation 
and 
an 
increase 
in 
the 
value 
of 
land could 
be 
expected 
to 
bring 
benefits 
to 
poorer 
members 
of 
local 
communities 
who currently 
lease 
out 
their land 
and 
may 
not 
have 
sufficient 
capital 
to 
make 
immediate 
use 
of 
improved 
irrigation. 
See 
below 
for 
a 
further 
considerations 
of 
the 
social 
risks 
of 
the 
project 
and 
suggestions 
for 
how 
these 
could 
be mitigated. 
The 
second 
social objective 
of 
the 
IDSMIP 
would 
be 
to 
develop 
the 
institutional 
capacity 
for 
farmer 
managed 
irrigation 
systems. 
This 
would 
involve 
turning 
over the 
authority 
and 
responsibility 
to 
manage 
irrigation 
systems 
from 
government 
agencies 
to 
water 
user 
associations 
(WAS). 
Legislation 
- 
95 
- 
Page  100
and 
institutions 
being 
developed 
under 
IDSMIP 
would 
provide 
the 
framework 
for 
WUAs 
to 
define 
what 
irrigation 
services 
would 
be 
provided and 
the 
authority 
to 
arrange 
the 
provision 
of 
those services. 
This 
process 
would 
involve 
a re-orientation and 
re-definition 
of 
the 
role 
of 
SAIC, 
which 
has 
had 
little 
experience 
in 
taking 
account 
of 
local 
demand. 
It 
would 
also 
involve 
a 
significant 
effort 
to 
build 
the 
capacity 
of 
WUAs, 
as 
there 
is 
currently 
little 
experience 
of 
inclusive, 
transparent 
and democratic 
decision-malung. 
The 
little 
experience 
of 
autonomous 
decision-making 
that there 
has 
been 
so 
far 
at 
the 
local level 
has 
tended 
to 
reproduce 
old 
authority 
structures. 
The 
development 
of 
WUAs, 
although 
inevitably 
a 
slow 
process, 
would 
not 
only 
provide 
effective 
irrigation 
management, 
but 
would 
also 
provide 
an 
example 
of 
local 
collective 
action 
and 
negotiation 
between 
different social 
groups. 
This 
would 
contribute 
to 
the 
wider 
objective 
of 
building 
sustainable 
local institutional 
and 
governance 
structures 
for rural 
development 
that 
are 
based 
on local 
needs 
and 
priorities. 
International 
experience 
of 
WAS 
suggests 
that 
their 
success 
depends 
heavily 
on 
the extent 
to 
which 
their 
members 
consider 
them 
to 
be 
representative, equitable, 
efficient, 
transparent 
and 
accountable. 
The 
experience 
from 
many 
different 
countries 
has 
led to 
the 
conclusion 
that 
WUAs 
should 
have 
an 
institutional 
framework 
based 
in 
law, 
involve 
a 
separation 
of 
managerial 
and 
executive 
powers, 
establish 
transparent 
financial 
and 
accounting 
procedures 
and 
clearly define 
the 
rights 
and 
responsibilities 
of 
membership. 
This institutional framework 
constitutes 
a 
new way 
of 
doing 
things 
in 
Azerbaijan 
for which 
there 
is 
currently 
no 
local 
capacity. 
This 
would 
involve 
a 
major 
exercise 
in 
training 
on 
technical and 
institutional 
aspects 
of 
WUA 
management 
and 
general 
awareness 
raising 
and capacity 
building 
to 
ensure that members 
understand 
their 
rights 
and 
the 
financial implications 
of 
decisions taken. 
Water 
users, 
in 
particular 
those 
with 
different 
irrigation-related 
priorities 
and 
financial 
capacities, 
are 
likely 
to 
have 
different 
needs. 
Capacity 
building 
work 
must 
therefore 
be 
directed 
at the 
grassroots 
and 
not 
rely 
on 
the 
mediation 
of 
existing 
village 
leadership. 
The 
following 
are 
some 
of 
the 
poverty 
and 
equity 
related 
issues 
that 
should 
be 
taken 
into 
account 
in 
this 
respect. 
Poverty 
and 
Capacity 
to 
Pay 
-- 
Social 
and 
Institutional 
Issues 
In 
most 
of 
the 
WUAs 
included 
in 
the social 
diagnosis, 
people 
reported 
that 
parts 
of 
the 
irrigation 
command 
areas are 
uncultivated 
due 
to 
a 
lack 
of 
adequate 
irrigation 
and 
drainage. 
Owners 
of 
these 
plots 
have 
limited 
opportunities 
to 
make 
use 
of 
the 
land 
themselves 
or to 
lease 
them. 
These 
vulnerable 
families 
must 
adopt 
diversified 
income generating 
strategies, 
which may 
limit 
their 
ability 
to 
make 
good 
use 
of 
the 
land 
when 
the 
irrigation 
systems are 
rehabilitated. 
Other families 
have 
irrigated 
1ands.but are 
vulnerable 
due 
to 
the inadequate 
and 
unreliable 
irrigation 
service 
they 
receive. 
While 
all 
farmers, 
including 
poor 
ones, 
expressed 
a 
willingness 
to 
pay 
irrigation 
service 
fees 
(ISF) 
in 
principle, 
the 
diagnostic 
study 
suggests 
that 
perhaps 
35 
percent 
of 
all 
water 
users 
might 
have 
problems 
paying 
higher 
fees 
and 
contributing upfront to 
rehabilitation. 
This 
finding 
is 
supported 
by 
the 
economic 
report 
of 
the diagnostic 
studies, 
as 
well 
as 
more broadly 
by 
the 
2001 
Household Budget 
Survey. 
The problem 
is 
not only 
one 
of 
poverty 
but 
also 
of 
cash 
flow; 
even 
middle-income 
farmers 
tend 
to 
have 
few 
sources 
of 
regular 
cash 
income.  However, 
for 
poorer 
households 
the 
increased 
ISF 
may 
entail 
cashing 
in 
on 
capital 
assets 
and 
drawing 
on 
their 
pensions 
and 
social benefits. 
Evidently 
one 
of 
the 
main 
reasons 
for 
low 
agricultural 
incomes 
is 
irrigation 
and 
the 
capacity 
to 
pay 
of 
even 
poor 
farmers 
should 
increase 
during 
the course 
of 
the 
project. 
However 
mechanisms 
for 
farmer 
contributions 
and 
for 
scheduling 
ISF 
payments 
should 
be 
cognizant 
of 
both 
immediate 
and 
longer-term 
constraints 
on 
cash 
flow. 
Further, 
efforts should 
be 
made 
to 
ensure that 
the 
capacities 
of 
the 
poorer 
farmers 
are 
considered 
in 
deciding 
on 
rehabilitation priorities. 
- 
96 
- 
Page  101
Equity 
and 
Local 
Stakeholder Relationships 
-- 
Major 
Challenges 
The 
social 
diagnosis 
was 
not 
able 
reliably to quantify 
the 
distribution 
of 
wealth 
in 
the 
study 
WAS, 
due 
to 
the 
normal 
reluctance 
of 
respondents 
to 
discuss 
their 
source 
and 
size 
of 
income, 
as 
well 
as 
the 
subsistence character 
of 
the 
local 
economy. 
The 
analysis 
of 
household 
budgets 
of 
different 
income 
groups 
revealed a 
significant 
gap 
between income 
groups, however, 
but 
the 
polarization 
of 
income 
is 
somewhat 
cushioned 
by 
the 
fairly 
just 
distribution 
of 
initial 
landholdings 
and 
the 
reciprocity 
arrangements 
between relatives. 
In 
many 
cases, 
this 
has 
meant that 
poor 
families 
unable 
to 
cultivate 
their 
own 
land 
receive a 
small rental 
income 
or 
share 
of 
the 
crop 
others 
cultivate 
on 
their 
lands. 
There 
are 
signs 
that 
these 
relations 
are 
changing and that 
there 
is 
an 
emerging group 
of 
people 
locally 
called 
business farmers, 
who 
are 
distinguished 
by 
their 
relatively 
large 
landholdings 
and 
livestock 
ownership. 
Currently 
some 
of 
these 
farmers 
are 
able 
to 
operate 
successfully 
because 
they 
have 
private 
diesel 
pumps 
to 
ensure 
their 
own 
irrigation 
supply, 
and 
others have 
direct 
water 
contracts 
with 
SAIC. 
In 
most 
instances, the 
success 
of 
these 
farmers 
is 
linked 
to 
and 
reinforced 
by 
opportunities 
in 
local 
leadership structures 
and 
access 
to 
capital 
from 
non-agricultural 
employment that 
was 
invested 
in 
local 
agriculture. 
Similarly, 
under 
conditions 
of 
short 
water 
supply 
and 
poor distribution 
patterns, 
adequate 
water 
supplies 
reflects 
linkages 
to 
local 
leadership. 
The 
project 
would 
most 
likely 
contribute 
to 
an 
increase 
in 
the 
value 
of 
land 
for 
both 
rent 
and 
sale. 
This 
could 
be a 
positive 
development 
for 
poor 
farmers 
while 
they explore other 
livelihood 
strategies 
or 
gather the 
capital 
necessary 
to 
cultivate 
the 
land. 
However 
there 
is 
likely to 
be 
a 
difference 
in 
the 
interests 
of 
poorer 
farmers whose 
main 
benefit 
from 
the project, 
at 
least 
in 
the 
early 
phase, 
may 
be 
increased 
rental 
income and 
employment, 
and 
the 
interests 
of 
the 
business 
farmers described. 
The 
latter 
may 
be more 
interested 
in 
cheap 
land 
and labor; 
the 
differences 
in 
priorities 
that 
may 
also 
strain relations 
of 
reciprocity 
between 
extended 
families. 
These 
interests 
may 
be 
further 
divided 
if 
- 
as 
often 
seems 
to 
be 
the 
case 
- 
the 
poorer 
farmers 
also 
own 
land 
at 
the 
tail-end 
of 
the 
system. 
Most 
WUAs 
would 
include 
some 
people 
who 
would 
not 
be 
able 
to 
pay 
irrigation 
fees 
and 
may 
be 
eligible 
for 
exemptions. 
The 
project should 
insist 
that 
exemptions 
from 
payment 
for irrigation 
are 
decided 
by 
the 
WUA 
in 
a 
consultative fashion 
in 
order 
to avoid 
engendering resentment 
and 
conflict. 
The 
description 
above relates 
to 
a 
trend, 
but 
in 
fact 
there 
are 
too many 
variables 
to 
be 
able 
to 
predict quantitatively 
the 
costs and 
benefits 
of 
an 
investment 
in 
irrigation 
for 
different 
groups. 
Therefore, 
it 
is 
critical 
for 
the 
process 
of 
restructuring 
WUAs 
be 
used 
as 
an opportunity 
for 
open 
and 
fair 
negotiation 
between 
different 
interest 
groups 
regarding 
operating 
rules, 
exceptions, sanctions 
and other 
common 
issues. 
Kinship 
networks 
have been an 
important 
source 
of 
security 
during 
the 
transition 
period. 
They 
constitute 
an important 
resource 
for 
group 
formation 
because 
they 
are 
characterized 
by 
relative 
trust 
and 
reciprocity. 
By 
their very 
nature, 
however, 
they 
are 
exclusive, 
rather 
than 
inclusive, 
and 
the 
project 
must 
ensure 
that they 
do 
not 
become 
an organizing 
feature 
of 
the 
WUA. 
Information 
is 
likely 
to 
be 
used 
strategically 
by 
those 
in 
leadership positions, 
this 
would 
mean that 
the 
restructuring 
and 
re-registration 
process 
must 
start 
with 
an 
information 
and 
awareness 
raising 
campaign that 
is 
explicitly 
targeted 
to 
reach 
all 
WUA 
members. 
Even 
if 
long-term 
trends 
in 
the 
agricultural 
sector 
may 
result 
in 
the 
consolidation 
of 
land 
ownership and 
agricultural 
production, 
it 
is 
important 
for 
access 
to irrigation not 
be 
allowed 
to 
be 
exploited 
by 
some 
WUA 
members 
to 
gain 
access 
to 
resources. 
Currently, 
it 
is 
clear that 
poorer 
households and 
women 
have 
little 
understanding 
of 
the 
rules 
of 
the 
game 
under 
which 
local 
and national 
institutions, 
including 
SAIC 
and 
WUAs 
operate, whereas 
wealthier 
households 
are 
familiar 
with 
them. 
The 
social objectives 
of 
the 
IDSMIP 
described 
above can 
only 
be 
achieved 
if 
all 
water 
users 
gain 
this 
institutional 
understanding that 
would 
enable 
them 
to 
realize 
full 
value 
from 
WUA 
membership. 
- 
97 
- 
Page  102
In 
some 
communities, refugees (IDPs) also 
constitute 
an 
additional 
challenge 
for 
WUAs. 
IDPs 
do 
not 
receive 
land 
use 
rights, 
but 
they 
lease 
private 
or public 
land, sometimes 
under 
special 
arrangements 
that 
are 
not 
understood 
by 
other 
members 
of 
the 
community, 
and 
work 
as 
laborers. 
Unlike 
other 
members 
of 
the 
communities 
studied, 
IDPs do 
not 
have 
water 
contracts 
with 
SAIC; 
rather, 
their 
water 
fees 
may 
be 
included 
in 
lease 
contracts. 
Whatever 
the 
specific 
circumstances, however, 
the 
social 
diagnosis 
identified 
the 
lack 
of 
integration 
of 
IDPs, 
and 
perceptions that 
they 
receive 
special 
benefits 
not 
available 
to 
other 
members 
of 
the community, especially 
the poor, 
as 
potential 
sources 
of 
conflict 
if 
they 
are 
not 
addressed. 
Representative, accountable 
WUAs 
would 
serve 
as 
an important 
mechanism 
for 
social 
inclusion 
of 
IDPs, 
as 
well 
as 
other 
poor 
households. 
Due to 
the special 
status 
of 
IDPs, 
they 
are 
likely 
to 
be open 
to 
charges 
of 
either 
unjust 
privilege 
or 
discrimination 
by 
other water 
users over such 
issues 
as 
timely 
water 
supply, 
fee 
collection 
and 
sanctions. 
According 
to 
the 2002 
Poverty 
Assessment, 
IDPs 
have a 
higher 
level 
vulnerability 
than other 
social 
groups, 
due 
to 
the 
fact that 
transfers account 
for 
over 
half 
of 
their 
incomes. 
They 
are 
not 
necessarily 
the 
poorest 
members 
of 
communities, 
however.  Experiences 
from 
NGOs 
working 
with 
IDPs 
indicate 
that 
they 
are 
often 
interested 
in 
rehabilitation 
of 
irrigation 
infrastructure 
and 
in 
providing 
labor 
for 
this 
purpose, 
presumably 
because 
the 
land 
they 
have 
been 
allocated 
is 
likely 
to 
be 
the poorest 
quality. 
Nonetheless, 
their 
ability to 
pay 
for 
increased 
water 
fees 
may 
be 
highly 
linked 
to 
subsidy 
levels. 
IDPs 
are 
disadvantaged 
compared 
to 
established residents 
in 
their 
communities along 
a 
number 
of 
dimensions, 
including 
the 
following: 
owning 
the 
land 
they 
cultivate 
(26 
percent, 
compared 
to 
87 
percent); 
having 
irrigated 
land 
(54 
percent, 
compared 
to 
88 
percent); 
complaining 
about 
poor land 
quality 
(48 
percent, 
compared 
to 
30 
percent); 
using 
their 
land 
for cultivation 
(36 
percent, 
compared 
to 
60 
percent); 
owning 
cows 
(27 percent, 
compared 
to 
63 
percent); 
and 
owning 
sheep 
(8 
percent, 
compared 
to 
20 percent). 
The 
role 
and 
rights 
of 
IDPs 
in 
WUAs 
should 
be 
left 
to 
each 
WUA 
to 
decide 
during 
the 
reconstruction 
period 
that 
precedes 
re-registration. 
Given 
the 
uncertain 
status 
and 
land 
rights 
of 
IDPs, 
the 
project 
can 
not 
be 
expected 
to 
prescribe specific 
legislation relating 
to 
IDPs 
or 
issue 
institutional 
instructions 
to 
WUAs. 
Nonetheless, 
the 
presence 
of 
IDPs 
in 
WUA 
areas, 
and 
the 
challenges 
they 
pose 
for 
the successful management 
of 
WUAs 
reinforces the 
importance 
of 
capacity 
building 
during 
the 
early 
stages 
of 
WUA 
development, 
to 
ensure 
that 
their 
plans 
are 
well 
thought 
out 
comprehensive 
and that 
they 
are 
strong 
enough 
to 
be 
able 
to 
address 
different 
local 
priorities 
in 
a 
fair, 
equitable 
and 
transparent 
manner. 
WUAs 
and 
Empowerment 
-- 
An 
Unfinished 
Agenda 
Over 
the 
last 
three 
years, 
SAIC 
management 
recognized the 
potential 
value 
of 
WUAs 
in 
carrying 
out 
its 
own 
mandate, 
as 
well 
as 
their 
value 
to 
members. 
More 
recently, 
SAIC 
management 
initiated 
a 
restructuring 
process 
that 
will 
profoundly 
affect 
its 
role 
over 
time. 
First, 
SAIC 
regional 
staff actively 
promoted 
the 
concept 
of 
WUA 
and 
helped 
create 
over 
550 
WUAs 
in 
less 
than 
three 
years. 
This 
was 
followed 
by 
the 
creation 
of 
a 
special 
unit 
in 
SAIC's headquarters 
with 
responsibility 
for 
water 
management and 
revenue 
collection. 
This 
unit 
has 
been 
strengthened 
through 
its 
participation 
in 
project 
preparation 
and 
would 
be 
provided 
further 
support 
as 
it 
assumes 
responsibility 
for 
project 
implementation. 
Most 
recently, 
SAIC 
established 
a 
three-person 
WUA 
Support 
Unit 
in 
each 
regional 
office 
with 
responsibility 
for 
direct 
interface 
with 
WUAs 
under 
the supervision 
of 
the central 
unit. 
The 
project 
would 
train 
(essentially re-train) 
staff 
of 
the 
WUA 
Support 
Units 
and 
provide 
initial 
technical 
support 
to 
establish 
the 
credibility 
and 
effectiveness 
of 
the 
units 
during 
early 
stages 
of 
the 
project 
when 
WUAs 
are 
being 
restructured and registered under 
new 
legislation. 
The 
findings 
of 
the 
social 
diagnosis 
provide 
very 
strong 
justification 
for 
these 
major institutional 
changes. 
- 
98 
- 
Page  103
From 
a social 
perspective, 
it 
is 
evident that 
on 
the 
local 
level, 
there 
is 
extreme 
and widespread 
frustration 
with 
the 
deterioration 
of 
irrigation 
infrastructure and 
institutional 
vacuum 
regarding on-farm 
water 
management. 
In 
some 
instances 
water 
is 
paid 
for 
in 
advance 
but 
not 
delivered 
at 
all, 
or only 
a 
fraction 
of 
the 
expected 
water 
was 
delivered. 
In 
many 
instances 
people 
do 
not 
cultivate 
their 
land 
at 
all 
because 
they 
can 
not 
be 
sure 
of 
receiving irrigation; 
in 
other 
cases, 
farmers 
invest 
in 
inputs 
and 
lost 
the 
crop 
because 
of 
inadequate water.  Respondents 
in 
the 
social 
diagnosis also expressed 
uncertainty 
about 
the 
amount 
of 
water 
needed 
for 
various 
crops 
and most said that 
they 
relied 
on 
the 
ditch-riders 
to 
regulate 
the 
water per 
crop.  Overall, 
respondents 
were 
unsure 
of 
how 
to 
explain poor 
irrigation 
and 
offered 
explanations that 
range 
from 
SAIC 
incompetence 
to 
dirty 
drains, 
blocked 
collectors and 
illicit 
water 
diversion. 
Few 
people understood 
the 
logic 
of 
either 
the 
new or 
old 
water 
fee 
payment 
system 
or 
the 
regulations 
governing irrigation. 
In 
short, 
the 
limited 
general 
understanding 
of 
both 
technical 
and 
institutional 
aspects 
of 
the 
irrigation 
system 
is 
a 
major contribution 
to 
poor 
management. 
SAIC 
created 
WUAs 
to 
respond 
to 
these 
issues, 
but 
the 
top-down 
approach 
has 
not 
succeeded 
in 
reaching many 
members 
of 
the 
WUAs. 
The 
rules 
and 
rights 
of 
WUA 
membership 
are 
unclear, 
thus 
most 
households 
do 
not 
identify 
themselves 
as 
members. 
The 
stakeholding 
of 
the 
WUA 
in 
irrigation 
should be 
representative 
of 
its 
members, 
but 
this 
relationship 
is 
not 
yet functional. 
Partly, 
this 
is 
due 
to 
technical 
reasons; 
a 
significant 
number 
of 
WUA 
members 
do 
not 
receive 
sufficient 
water 
or 
have decided 
not 
to 
irrigate 
altogether, 
thus 
they 
have 
no 
contracts 
with 
the 
WUA. 
This 
unclear 
relationship 
between 
the 
WUA 
and 
its 
members 
is 
also due 
to 
the 
organizational structure 
of 
the 
WUAs. 
There 
are 
no 
clear 
guidelines 
for 
the management structure 
and 
for 
the 
rights 
and 
responsibilities 
of 
the 
members. 
In 
some 
WUAs, 
households 
form 
groups 
and 
only 
one member 
signs 
a contract 
with 
the 
WUA 
in 
order 
to 
simplify 
the 
process; 
in 
others, 
all 
members 
contract 
individually. 
In 
all 
of 
the 
WUAs 
included 
in 
the 
social 
diagnosis, 
it 
was 
evident 
that 
average 
water 
users have 
no 
clear understanding 
of 
the 
structure 
of 
their 
WUA 
or 
their 
status 
as 
members. 
WUAs 
should 
be 
the 
main 
stakeholders 
in 
irrigation, 
as 
representatives 
of 
water 
users, 
but 
the 
social 
diagnosis 
concluded that 
their 
capacity 
is 
still 
very 
weak 
for 
a 
number 
of 
reasons: 
e 
0 
e 
e 
lack 
of 
technical 
and 
management experience 
in 
irrigation; 
poor irrigation 
and 
drainage 
infrastructure, 
which 
not 
only 
makes 
delivery 
difficult 
but 
also 
complicates measurement 
and pricing; 
large 
numbers 
of 
members 
are 
inexperienced 
farmers; 
an 
uncertain 
policy 
and 
legal 
environment 
for 
irrigation, 
particularly 
over 
questions 
of 
rights 
and ownership; 
changes 
in 
the 
system 
of 
payments; 
members 
feel 
that 
vested 
interests 
determine 
the 
supply 
and 
delivery 
of 
water; 
frequent 
conflicts 
over water 
distribution; 
difficulties collecting 
water 
fees 
and 
difficulties imposing 
sanctions 
and 
fines; 
limited local 
understanding 
of 
the 
function 
of 
the 
WUA 
and 
the 
rights 
of 
members; 
and 
limited 
local 
participation 
in 
the operation 
of 
the 
WUA. 
Water 
fees are 
a 
major 
problem. 
Local 
opinions 
vary 
about 
the 
equity 
of 
the 
fixed 
rate 
per 
hectare 
that 
was 
adopted 
in 
June 
2002 
as 
a pragmatic adaptation 
to 
the 
reality 
that 
neither 
WUAs 
nor 
SAIC 
is 
able 
to monitor 
cropping 
patterns 
or 
measure 
actual 
delivery. 
However 
it 
clearly 
does 
not 
provide 
an incentive 
to 
farmers 
to 
economize 
on 
water 
use 
and 
has 
already 
resulted 
in 
conflict.  Under 
this 
system, 
farmers 
receiving 
one 
irrigation 
are 
required 
to 
pay 
the 
same 
amount 
as 
farmers 
receiving 
the 
full 
scheduled amount.  Further, 
although 
it 
is 
meant 
to 
simplify 
delivery 
and 
payment, 
disputes 
over 
both 
persist 
and 
easily 
emerge 
discussions 
with 
groups 
of 
farmers. 
- 
99 
- 
Page  104
In 
four 
out 
of 
the 
six 
WUAs 
included 
in 
the 
social 
diagnosis, 
people mentioned 
that 
there 
had 
been 
conflicts 
over 
water, that 
the 
system 
for 
payments 
was 
unjust 
and that water 
paid for 
had 
not 
been 
delivered. 
Focus 
goup 
discussions 
often 
resulted 
in 
tension between 
members 
and 
WUA 
representatives, 
with 
denials 
and 
accusations 
on 
both 
sides. 
Some 
people mentioned 
that 
the 
position 
of 
the 
WUA 
chairman 
in 
some 
places 
had 
become one 
for which 
there 
was 
an 
unofficial 
price, 
which 
suggests 
that 
significant 
rents 
can 
be 
collected 
from 
this 
position. 
Whatever 
the 
reality 
of 
such 
statements, 
the 
frequency 
and fervor 
of 
such 
claims 
reveals 
a 
significant 
gap 
between 
the 
WUAs 
and 
their 
members. 
Many 
problems 
are 
within 
the 
responsibility 
of 
SAIC, 
beyond 
the current 
control 
of 
WUAs, 
and 
it 
is 
expected 
that 
the 
WUA 
would 
be 
held 
responsible 
for 
irrigation 
related 
problems. 
However 
there 
is 
a 
critical 
need 
to 
build 
the capacity 
of 
WUAs 
and 
to 
ensure 
that the 
main 
stake 
that 
the 
WUA 
has 
in 
irrigation 
is 
the 
efficient 
and equitable operation 
and maintenance 
of 
the 
irrigation 
system. 
Despite 
the 
shortcomings 
of 
WUAs, 
many 
of 
which 
would 
be 
addressed 
by 
the 
project, 
all 
of 
the 
WUAs 
in 
the social 
diagnosis 
had 
taken 
some measures 
to 
repair 
their 
irrigation 
and 
drainage 
infrastructure. 
The 
normal 
course 
of 
action 
has 
been 
to 
collect 
money and 
labor 
from 
households 
along 
a 
section 
of 
damaged 
canal 
to 
hire 
some 
machinery. 
However 
this 
rehabilitation 
is 
usually 
in 
the 
form 
of 
a 
temporary 
emergency measure and 
focus 
group 
discussions 
pointed 
to 
the 
poor 
infrastructure 
as 
the 
main 
source 
of 
the 
irrigation 
problem. 
It 
is 
clear that 
establishing 
responsibility 
for 
on-farm 
irrigation 
infrastructure 
is 
part 
of 
an 
evolving 
process 
in 
Azerbaijan 
relating to 
the 
range 
of 
basic 
institutional 
infrastructure 
for rural 
development. 
Decisions 
are 
still 
being 
taken 
on 
the 
basic 
structures, 
rights, 
responsibilities 
and 
relationships 
of 
local 
governance. 
WUAs 
are 
therefore 
being 
established 
in 
an environment 
in 
which 
fundamental 
issues 
of 
ownership, 
access 
and 
management 
rights 
over 
local 
resources 
are 
still 
being 
negotiated. 
WUAs 
would 
manage 
and 
collect 
funds 
from 
one 
of 
the 
most 
valuable 
local 
resources 
- 
water 
- 
thus 
they 
would 
inevitably 
come 
under 
pressure 
from 
forces 
-both 
formal 
and 
informal 
- 
that 
attempt 
to 
reduce 
their 
autonomy over 
funds, 
functions and infrastructure. 
The 
effectiveness 
of 
the 
WUA 
would 
therefore 
depend 
heavily 
on 
a 
legal 
and 
institutional 
structure 
that 
is 
cognizant 
of 
these 
external 
pressures 
and 
build 
local 
capacity 
to 
maintain downward 
accountability and 
transparency. 
One 
of 
the 
most 
critical 
tasks 
in 
this 
respect 
would 
be 
to 
create 
a clear 
separation 
of 
roles 
between 
the 
local 
self-governing 
institutions 
(belediye) 
and 
WAS. 
The 
diagnostic 
study 
established 
that 
the 
record 
of 
WUAs 
and the 
belediye 
on 
transparency, 
accountability 
and 
inclusive 
decision-making 
is 
currently very 
poor.  Further, 
their 
respective 
roles 
in 
water 
management 
are 
not 
very 
clear. 
This 
is 
due 
to 
the 
fact that 
WUAs 
are 
not 
very 
strong 
and 
belediye 
have therefore 
become 
involved 
in 
the 
management and 
supply 
of 
water. 
It 
is 
also due 
to 
the 
Law 
of 
the 
Azerbaijan Republic 
On 
the 
Water 
Economy 
of 
Municipalities, 
which 
indicates that 
the 
state 
owned 
water 
structures 
on 
municipality land 
should 
fall 
under 
municipalities, albeit 
that 
should 
only be 
for 
infrastructure 
that 
is 
located 
within 
the 
built-up 
part 
of 
a 
municipality. 
It 
is 
nevertheless 
critical 
for 
the 
legal 
framework 
for 
irrigation 
and 
amelioration, 
and 
the 
status 
of 
WUAs, 
that 
is 
currently 
being 
developed 
to clearly 
separate 
the 
roles 
of 
the 
WUA 
and 
the 
belediye 
in 
water 
management. 
It 
is 
equally 
critical, 
however, 
for 
all 
members 
of 
WUAs 
to 
understand 
these 
arrangements, 
which 
again 
argues 
for 
the importance 
of 
information 
campaigns 
and 
training 
programs 
to 
ensure 
that the 
issue 
is 
clearly 
understood 
by 
all 
parties. 
The 
Role 
of 
Women 
The 
privatization 
process 
in 
Azerbaijan 
has 
been especially 
hard 
on 
women 
and 
it 
is 
important 
that 
the 
WUA 
capacity 
building 
process takes 
their 
interests and 
capacities 
into 
account. 
Women 
were 
the 
first 
to 
lose 
their 
jobs 
as 
the 
privatization 
cycle 
first 
targeted 
areas 
in 
which 
women 
were 
traditionally 
employed, such 
as 
services, 
catering 
and 
food 
processing. 
At 
the 
same 
time 
the 
decline 
of 
basic 
social 
- 
100- 
Page  105
services 
has 
led 
to 
an 
increasing 
involvement 
of 
women 
in 
substituting 
these 
and a 
decreasing 
number 
of 
women 
taking 
an 
active 
part 
in 
the 
rural 
privatization 
process 
through 
managing 
agricultural 
production, 
setting 
up 
private 
businesses 
or 
income 
generating 
activities. 
The 
most 
significant 
gender 
issue 
for 
the 
project 
is 
the 
level 
of 
female ignorance 
about the 
economic and 
administrative 
details 
of 
agricultural production. 
It 
is 
important 
for 
women 
to 
be 
made 
aware 
of 
the 
implications 
that 
irrigation 
related works 
and the 
WUA 
would 
have 
for 
the household 
budget. 
It 
is 
also 
important, 
given 
the frequent and 
long 
migrations 
of 
the 
head 
of 
household, 
that 
they 
are 
given 
the 
rights 
to 
vote and 
represent 
household interests 
in 
the 
absence 
of 
the 
household head 
in 
the 
WUA. 
This 
awareness 
raising 
is 
unlikely 
to 
happen 
automatically, 
thus 
information 
campaigns 
should 
consider 
explicitly 
the 
question 
of 
how 
to 
reach women. 
Social 
Cohesion 
and 
Inclusion 
-- 
Important 
Project 
Objectives 
The 
social diagnosis 
highlighted 
a number 
of 
factors that 
influence 
irrigated 
agriculture 
management 
in 
Azerbaijan 
and 
pose challenges 
for 
the 
IDSMIP 
project, 
particularly 
the 
roles and 
activities 
of 
SAIC 
and 
WAS. 
These 
range 
from 
rural 
poverty, 
especially 
poverty 
related 
to 
inequitable 
access 
to 
irrigation 
water and 
poor 
irrigation 
management, 
to 
the 
influence 
of 
vested interests, 
conflict, 
the 
limited 
experience 
and 
skills 
of 
farmers, degraded 
infrastructure 
and the 
essential 
unavailability 
of 
information 
about 
WUAs 
and 
other 
institutions. 
IDSMIP 
can 
not 
be 
expected 
to 
address 
all 
of 
these 
issues 
directly, 
but 
if 
it 
succeeds 
in 
developing 
WUAs 
that 
are 
representative, autonomous, competent, 
equitable, 
efficient, 
transparent and accountable, 
it 
would 
make a 
major contribution 
to 
empowering 
water users 
to 
address 
many 
of 
these 
issues. 
Two 
overall 
social objectives can 
be 
achieved 
through 
this 
effort: 
first, 
to 
promote 
social 
cohesion 
in 
the 
irrigated 
areas, 
enabling 
farmers 
to 
work 
together 
to 
manage 
their 
most 
precious 
resource; 
and 
second, 
social 
inclusion, 
bringing 
all 
WUA 
members 
into 
the 
process 
by 
creating 
a 
level 
playing 
field, emphasizing 
communality 
of 
interests, 
and 
delivering 
results. 
Information 
dissemination 
is 
one 
of 
the 
most 
important 
elements 
of 
this effort, 
if 
not 
the 
most important 
one. 
The 
diagnostic survey 
has 
revealed a 
communication 
and 
information 
gap 
in 
local 
knowledge 
of 
the 
transition 
process, 
in 
general, 
and 
irrigation 
institutions and 
irrigation 
management, 
in 
particular. 
Local 
practices 
vary greatly 
regarding water 
fees, 
regulations 
on 
credit 
and 
tax, 
administrative 
norms, 
land 
lease 
practices and 
access 
to 
common 
property, 
as 
well 
as 
the 
structure 
and 
operations 
of 
WUAs. 
Some 
of 
this 
variation 
is 
due 
to 
successful 
local 
adaptation. 
In 
many 
instances, however, 
it 
also 
reflects 
govemance 
that 
is 
neither efficient, 
transparent 
nor 
equitable.  Poorer 
households 
and 
women 
have 
little 
understanding 
of 
the 
rules 
of 
the 
game, 
and 
thus 
have 
little 
influence, 
whereas 
wealthier 
households 
do 
have such understanding. 
To 
a 
great extent, 
the 
level 
of 
understanding 
in 
a household 
reflects 
the 
level 
of 
access 
of 
that 
household 
to 
the 
prevailing 
leadership structure, 
and 
thus 
information flows. 
New 
pattems 
of 
information 
dissemination 
must 
be 
established 
to 
overcome 
information 
bottlenecks 
and 
broaden the 
level 
of 
participation 
in 
decision-making 
to 
include 
a 
wide 
range 
of 
interests. 
A 
dedicated communications and 
public 
relations 
strategy 
would 
therefore 
be 
a 
prominent 
element 
of 
initial 
project 
implementation. 
This 
would 
help 
ensure 
that water 
users 
are 
aware 
of 
the 
project 
and 
its 
objectives and that they 
get 
adequate 
information 
to 
understand 
the 
function 
and 
responsibilities 
of 
WAS, 
their 
roles 
and 
rights 
in 
the 
WUA, 
and 
implications 
of 
participation 
in 
the 
project. 
Various 
stakeholders 
would 
be 
consulted 
while 
preparing 
the 
communication and 
information 
dissemination 
program, 
particularly 
water 
users, 
to 
determine 
the 
most 
efficient 
and effective 
ways 
to 
reach 
different 
stakeholders. 
The 
program 
should 
be 
monitored to 
evaluate effectiveness 
and 
adjusted, 
based 
on 
experience. 
Throughout 
the 
project, 
the 
dissemination 
networks 
can 
be 
utilized 
to 
deliver 
- 
101 
- 
Page  106
information 
and advice 
to 
WUAs 
and 
WUA 
members 
on 
topics ranging 
from 
management 
practices 
to 
legislation 
and agricultural 
extension. 
Information 
dissemination 
through 
the 
distribution 
of 
materials 
and 
media 
is 
essential 
for 
restructuring, 
reorienting 
and 
strengthening 
WUAs, 
but 
it 
would 
not 
be 
sufficient. 
Effective 
WUA 
development 
would 
also 
require 
household and 
neighborhood 
contact 
and 
discussions 
with 
WUA 
members. 
SAIC's 
support 
would 
also 
be 
important. 
This 
support 
must 
be 
structured and 
delivered 
in 
new 
ways, 
for which SAIC 
staff 
must 
be trained 
and 
gain 
experience. 
In 
order 
to 
develop 
its 
new 
roles 
as 
water wholesaler 
and technical 
advisor 
to 
WUAs, 
SAIC would 
need 
to 
prepare 
its 
field 
staff 
to 
establish 
a 
new 
field 
presence. 
Currently, 
staff 
of 
the 
SAIC 
regional offices 
communicate 
almost 
exclusively 
with 
the 
small 
management 
team 
of 
a 
WUA. 
In 
developing 
their 
new 
roles, 
staff 
of 
the 
WUA 
Support 
Units 
would 
need 
to 
expand 
their 
information 
and 
communication 
patterns 
markedly 
to 
reach 
all 
WUA 
members. 
They 
would 
be 
provided 
with 
new 
information 
materials that 
are 
designed 
to 
reach 
all 
households 
in 
a 
WUA, 
enabling 
all 
water 
users 
and 
family 
members 
to 
understand the need 
to 
separate 
executive 
authority 
from 
technical 
management 
and 
to 
establish 
mechanisms 
to 
expand 
participation 
and 
increase 
the 
transparency 
and 
accountability 
of 
the 
WUA. 
During 
early 
stages 
of 
the 
project, 
Support 
Unit 
staff 
would 
have resources 
to 
hire 
a 
part-time 
assistant 
in 
each 
WUA 
to 
help 
them 
carry 
out 
this 
information 
campaign 
and 
help 
WUA 
develop 
effective communication 
networks 
that 
reach 
all 
members. 
The 
WUA 
Support 
Units 
would 
also 
coordinate 
training 
of 
WUA 
members 
and 
officials to 
increase 
their 
respective 
competence. 
Most 
critically, staff 
of 
the Support 
Units 
would 
help 
WUAs 
(leaders 
and 
members) develop 
two 
inter-related 
but 
distinct 
agendas: 
first, 
a technical 
agenda 
designed 
to 
improve 
the 
quality 
and operation 
of 
the 
infrastructure 
and 
improve 
water 
distribution 
and 
management; 
and 
second, 
a 
management 
agenda, 
essentially a social 
agenda, 
that 
establishes 
the 
credibility 
of 
the 
WUA 
as 
a 
management 
entity 
with 
responsibility 
to 
deal 
with 
the social 
aspects 
of 
irrigation, 
including 
reporting to 
members, 
mobilizing 
resources, 
articulating 
rights 
and 
responsibilities, establishing 
effective 
appeal mechanisms, 
maintaining 
rules 
and 
regulations, 
and resolving 
disputes. 
This 
would 
also 
entail 
developing specific programs 
to 
address issues 
that 
can 
undermine 
the 
legitimacy 
of 
the 
WUA 
if 
they 
are 
not 
handled 
directly, 
such 
as 
social 
inclusion, 
where 
there 
are 
IDPs or 
new 
water 
users, 
or 
poverty, manifest 
as 
a 
real 
inability 
to 
pay 
minimal 
tariffs.  The 
socia! 
development 
objectives 
of 
the 
project 
would 
be realized 
if 
these 
two 
agendas, 
developed 
by 
each 
WUA, 
are 
given 
equal 
priority. 
Social 
Impact Monitoring 
The overall 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation framework 
would 
be formulated 
by 
the 
PIU 
M&E 
specialist, 
The 
IDSMIP 
would 
have economic, 
financial, 
institutional, 
engineering, 
environmental and 
social 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation 
needs 
that 
serve 
different 
purposes. 
The 
purpose 
of 
much 
of 
the 
social 
and 
institutional monitoring 
would 
be 
to 
understand the 
process 
of 
WUA 
formation and 
to 
provide 
WUAs 
and 
project 
management 
with 
early indications 
of 
progress, 
or lack 
thereof, 
to 
enable 
them 
to 
re-orient 
or 
adjust 
the 
process. 
Project 
evaluation 
would 
enable 
an 
assessment 
of 
the 
extent 
to which 
interventions 
have 
produced 
the 
intended 
outcomes 
and 
impacts. 
This 
section 
concentrates 
primarily 
on 
issues 
and 
indicators 
related 
to 
social 
impact monitoring, 
which would 
be 
integrated 
into 
the 
broader 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation 
framework. 
International 
experience 
has 
identified 
several 
common 
weaknesses 
of 
monitoring 
and evaluation 
that 
should 
be 
avoided. 
In 
a 
nutshell 
these 
include too 
much 
information; 
results 
that 
aren't 
utilized; 
overly 
standardized formats; 
poor 
baselines 
and 
poor 
sampling 
frames. 
The 
project should 
ensure 
that 
the 
minimum 
information 
needed 
is 
collected 
and therefore that the 
information 
requirements 
of 
the 
project 
are 
well 
thought out.  Social 
impact 
monitoring 
would 
focus 
on 
three 
kinds 
of 
information: 
- 
102 
- 
Page  107
0 
0 
0 
The 
institutional 
characteristics 
of 
WAS, 
including 
whether they 
are 
representative, 
efficient, 
competent, equitable, transparent 
and 
accountable; 
The 
quality 
of 
irrigation 
service and 
the 
impact 
of 
improved 
irrigation 
on 
livelihoods 
and 
incomes, 
overall, and 
on 
different 
social 
groups, 
particularly 
the vulnerable; and 
WUA 
formation 
and management 
in 
meeting 
internal 
objectives. 
The 
first 
two 
types 
of 
information 
are 
closely 
related 
to 
conventional 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation, 
while 
the 
last 
is 
closer 
to 
the 
participatory monitoring 
systems 
that 
are 
increasingly 
used 
in 
farmer 
managed 
irrigation 
projects 
to 
enable 
the 
WUA 
and 
its 
members 
to 
identify 
priorities, 
assess 
progress 
and be 
able 
to 
take 
corrective 
action when 
these 
priorities 
are 
not 
being 
realized. 
A 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation framework 
based 
on local 
needs 
and 
priorities 
can 
only 
be 
developed 
together 
with 
local 
communities. 
If 
participatory 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation 
is 
to 
be part 
of 
the 
project, and 
from 
a social 
standpoint 
this 
is 
recommended, 
then 
many 
of 
the 
indicators 
would 
have 
to 
be 
developed 
during 
project 
implementation. 
However 
the 
following 
are some 
of 
the 
possible 
indicators 
that 
could 
be 
used 
to 
identify 
process 
(in 
particular leading 
up 
to 
re-registration); 
immediate 
outcomes 
and 
ultimate 
impacts. 
These 
are 
presented 
in 
terms 
of 
relevant 
questions 
and related 
indicators. 
Process 
Issues 
and 
Indicators. 
Process 
indicators can be 
collected through participatory 
methods 
that enable 
WUA 
members 
to monitor 
the 
ongoing 
process 
of 
their 
WUA 
formation. 
Some 
process 
indicators 
can 
therefore 
be 
relevant 
mainly 
for 
the 
WUA 
concerned and therefore 
do not 
need 
to 
be 
comparable. 
However 
the 
indicators 
suggested 
below 
can 
all 
be 
aggregated 
and therefore 
be 
used 
by 
project 
management 
to 
monitor 
process. 
In 
particular, most 
of 
the 
indicators 
below 
can 
be 
used 
as 
criteria 
for 
the 
selection 
of 
WUAs 
for 
rehabilitation. 
In 
fact 
linking 
the 
collection 
of 
these 
indicators, 
analysis 
and 
local 
feedback 
to 
re-registration 
and 
rehabilitation 
would 
provide 
a 
time-bound objective 
and relevant 
focus 
for 
monitoring. 
Objective 
Elections 
for 
the 
WUA 
leaders democratic and 
perceived 
to 
represent 
the 
interests 
of 
all 
members. 
WUA 
members 
participate 
in 
the 
management 
of 
the 
WUA.

level 
of 
dispute 
and

WUA 
priorities 
and 
decisions. 
Payment 
of 
water 
charges, 
characteristics 
of 
defaulters and 
procedure 
for 
sanctions. 
Indicator 
Elections 
which 
were 
contested versus 
unanimous; 
WUA 
members 
who 
know 
the 
names 
and responsibilities 
of 
WUA 
leadership; 
WUA 
members 
who 
support 
WUA 
leaders 
and 
their 
policies. 
WUA 
members 
participated 
in 
the 
election 
of 
WUA 
leaders; 
WUA 
members attended 
key 
general 
assembly 
meetings 
for 
establishment 
of 
WUA 
by-laws 
and other 
basic 
policies; 
WUA 
members aware 
of 
basic 
policies 
and 
decisions 
(such 
as 
level 
of 
water 
charge, 
timing 
of 
general assembly meetings, 
the 
rules 
for 
water 
distribution). 
Water 
users 
from 
different 
areas 
of 
the 
irrigation 
system 
involved 
in 
key 
decisions 
(tailhead; 
pump/gravity, 
if 
applicable); 
Frequency 
of 
recorded 
conflicts 
within 
WUA; 
Frequency 
of 
non-recorded 
conflicts 
within 
WUA. 
Number 
of 
head 
versus 
tail-enders 
in 
default 
of 
payments; 
Number 
of 
large 
landholders 
versus 
small 
landholders 
in 
default 
of 
payments; 
Whether 
sanctions 
are 
applied

Outcome 
Issues 
and 
Indicators. 
Outcome 
issues 
are 
about the 
immediate 
and 
direct 
effects 
of 
the 
project 
and 
the achievement 
of 
key 
objectives. 
Important 
outcome 
objectives 
are: 
(i) 
improved 
efficiency, 
predictability 
and 
adequacy 
of 
irrigation 
supply; 
(ii) 
distribution 
of 
water equitable 
and 
reduced 
conflict 
over water 
supply; 
(iii) 
operation and 
maintenance 
of 
the 
system 
improved; and 
(iv) 
water 
fees 
closer 
to 
the 
actual 
cost 
of 
water. 
Many 
of 
these 
would not be 
directly 
related 
to 
the 
broader 
social objectives 
of 
the project, although 
of 
course 
they 
are 
closely 
linked. 
Impact 
Issues 
and Indicators. 
Impact 
issues 
refer 
to 
the 
ultimate, 
indirect 
effects 
of 
the 
project, 
or 
the 
realization 
of 
the 
basic goals 
and 
purposes 
of 
the 
project. 
They 
normally 
take 
longer 
to 
assess 
than 
outcomes 
and 
may 
be 
closer 
to 
evaluation 
than 
monitoring 
although 
the 
process 
of 
gathering 
information 
on 
these 
issues 
should 
start 
early 
on 
in 
the project. 
The 
impact 
of 
irrigation 
on 
social 
welfare 
and 
equity, 
concems 
considered 
earlier 
in 
the 
paper, are 
some 
of 
the 
key 
issues 
that 
should 
be 
monitored. The 
following 
are 
potential 
issues: 
1. 
What 
has 
been 
the 
effect 
of 
WUA 
restructuring 
and 
rehabilitation 
on 
farm 
income and 
economic 
returns 
to 
irrigation? 
a) 
Annual 
cropping 
intensity; 
b) 
Economic 
retum 
to 
irrigated 
land 
= 
gross 
or 
net 
value 
of 
outputfha; 
c) 
Economic 
retum 
to 
irrigation 
= 
gross 
or 
net value 
of 
output 
per 
m3; 
and 
d) 
Seasonal 
or 
annual 
farm 
income 
(gross 
or 
net) 
per 
irrigated 
hectare. 
2. 
What 
has 
been 
the 
effect 
of 
WUA 
restructuring 
and 
rehabilitation 
on 
land 
transactions? 
a) 
What 
are 
the 
leasehold 
arrangements 
and 
costs 
of 
leasing 
belediye 
land; 
b) 
What 
are 
the 
average 
leasehold 
arrangements 
and 
cost 
of 
leasing 
private 
land; 
c) 
Have 
the 
number 
of 
leaseholders increased 
or 
decreased; 
by 
how 
much; 
d) 
What 
is 
the 
average 
size 
of 
plot 
leased; 
e) 
What proportion 
of 
those 
leasing 
land 
are 
from 
inside 
and outside 
the 
village;  and 
f) 
Do 
those 
who 
lease 
land 
out 
stay 
in 
the 
village 
and what 
are 
their 
occupations. 
3. 
What 
has 
been 
the 
effect 
of 
WUA 
restructuring 
and 
rehabilitation 
on 
agricultural 
wages, 
employment 
- 
104- 
Page  109
and 
poverty? 
a) 
Annual 
average 
income 
in 
irrigated 
area 
before and 
after; 
b) 
Annual 
person 
days 
of 
labor per ha before 
and 
after; 
and 
c) 
Percentage 
of 
population 
below poverty 
line 
in 
scheme 
before 
and 
after. 
Monitoring 
and evaluation 
procedures 
should 
reflect 
the 
underlying 
objective 
of 
the 
project, 
which 
is 
to 
facilitate 
the 
effective 
transfer 
of 
decision-making 
authority 
to 
WUAs. 
Therefore 
the 
information 
collected should not 
only 
enable 
project 
management 
to 
monitor 
this 
process, 
but 
also 
meet 
the 
information 
needs 
of 
the 
WUAs 
themselves. 
In 
designing 
the 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation 
system 
it 
is 
therefore 
important to 
consider 
who 
would 
be 
involved 
in 
data 
collection, 
whose needs 
the 
information 
would 
serve, 
and 
how 
the 
data 
would 
be 
used 
and 
presented. 
The 
following 
are 
some 
of 
the social 
issues 
that 
are 
important 
to 
bear 
in 
mind 
in 
this 
design: 
As 
indicated, 
the 
PIU 
M&E 
specialist 
would 
oversee 
M&E 
during 
the 
project. 
However 
stakeholders such 
as 
SAIC, 
field 
operations staff, 
WUA 
members, 
WUA 
leaders 
and 
contractors 
should 
be 
actively 
involved 
in 
the 
M&E 
process. 
Ideally 
these 
key 
stakeholders 
should be 
involved 
in 
selecting 
or 
approving 
the 
indicators, 
as 
well 
as 
being 
sources 
of 
information 
for 
the 
M&E 
process. 
On 
the 
WUA 
level, 
the 
M&E 
process 
should 
start 
with 
a 
baseline questionnaire 
that includes 
indicators 
relevant 
to 
the 
social objectives 
and 
impacts 
of 
the 
project.  The 
data 
should 
be 
gathered 
by 
WUA 
Organizers 
located 
in 
the 
WUA, 
using 
an 
instrument 
that 
is 
readily 
available 
to 
WUA 
members 
and 
requires 
very 
little 
time to 
complete. 
The 
baseline 
should include 
information 
needed 
for 
future 
M&E, 
including 
the 
following: 
Number 
of 
members 
from 
each 
Village 
within 
the 
WUA; 
Number 
of 
people 
from 
each 
village 
in 
management 
and council; 
Number 
of 
non-members 
of 
the 
WUA 
and 
why 
they 
are 
non-members; 
Number 
of 
potential 
WUA 
members 
who 
receive 
no 
water; 
Number 
of 
women-headed households 
who 
are 
members; 
Number 
of 
members 
receiving 
exemptions 
or 
discounts 
from 
the 
irrigation 
service 
fee; 
Number 
of 
members 
identified 
as 
poor 
by 
the 
belediye; 
Number 
of 
members 
who 
are 
leasing 
land 
out 
land; 
average 
area; 
Number 
of 
members 
who 
are 
leasing 
land; 
average 
area; 
Number 
of 
members 
not 
cultivating; 
Number 
of 
non-residents 
who 
are 
cultivating land 
in 
the 
WUA 
area; 
Number 
of 
absentee 
/ 
migrant 
members; 
Location 
and 
characteristics 
of 
WUA 
members 
not 
paying 
fees 
on 
time; 
Location 
and 
characteristics 
of 
WUA 
members 
not 
paying 
fees; 
and 
Location 
of 
sites 
where there are 
conflicts 
over 
water 
distribution 
or 
unscheduled 
use. 
In 
addition 
to 
this 
WUA 
level 
base-line 
that can 
be 
collected 
by 
the 
WUA 
itself, 
a baseline 
should 
be 
taken 
from 
households whose 
land 
is 
located 
in 
different 
parts 
of 
the 
WUA 
service 
area. 
The 
data 
collected 
should 
be kept 
very 
simple and 
focused 
on 
both 
irrigation 
related 
production 
issues 
and 
institutional 
questions. 
A 
promising method 
for 
this 
would 
be 
report-cards, 
whereby 
the households 
would 
monitor 
themselves 
and 
the 
information 
would 
be 
collected, 
reviewed 
and 
tabulated 
biannually, 
analyzed and 
presented 
to 
WUA 
members, 
as 
well 
as 
the 
PIU 
and 
the 
IDA. 
WUA 
Support 
staff 
could 
attend meetings organized 
by 
the 
WUAs 
to 
conduct a 
collective 
assessment 
of 
WUA 
progress. 
The 
purpose 
would 
be 
to 
inform 
both 
WUA 
leadership 
and 
members 
of 
WUA 
progress, 
as 
well 
as 
provide 
the 
project 
with 
an 
indication 
of 
whether 
its 
strategies 
are 
successful 
- 
105- 
Page  110
or could 
use 
some 
modification 
and 
revision.  Social 
impact 
monitoring 
is 
an 
institutionally 
difficult 
task 
and 
is 
often 
resisted, 
thus 
it 
should 
focus 
on 
success 
and 
opportunities 
for 
improvement, rather 
than 
deficiencies,  and 
WUA-level 
assessments 
should be 
discussed 
in 
the 
WUA 
to avoid 
misrepresentation 
of 
results.  Regardless 
of 
the 
difficulty, 
however, social 
impact 
monitoring 
can 
be 
an 
important 
aspect 
of 
the 
project 
and 
should 
take advantage 
of 
the 
substantial progress made 
in 
methodologies that 
involve 
minimal 
external 
intrusion 
and extensive feed-back mechanisms.
